The Rational Objection Against Life in the Grave and Its Answer
Author presents and refutes a rational objection to life in the grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational objection against life in the grave and the theological response based on divine omnipotence and the possibility of a hidden mode of life.

Core Idea
Divine omnipotence overrides apparent rational impossibilities concerning life in the grave.

In this passage, the author addresses a natural human doubt: how can the dead, who lie motionless and may even be cremated, experience life and punishment? This objection seems reasonable from a purely empirical perspective. The author, however, grounds his answer not in observable nature but in the greater reality of divine power and the specific news brought by the truthful Prophet. Let's examine the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the rational objection is stated. Notice the examples given: the dead person seen without movement, buried in a tight space, burned to ashes. Understand why these seem to contradict life in the grave.
The objection is based on observed natural causation.
2
Identify the key assumption
The objection assumes that life requires a bodily structure (al-binya wa al-mizāj) and that the dead body lacks this. Ask yourself: is this assumption universally necessary?
This assumption is challenged by the author.
3
Study the first part of the answer
The author's first response is that improbability is irrelevant when the truthful One has informed. This means that divine revelation overrides human estimation of probability.
This is a fundamental principle in Islamic theology: revelation takes precedence over imperfect reason.
4
Examine the second and third parts of the answer
Even if we grant the need for bodily structure, it is not impossible that God preserves some original parts or uses the soul for punishment. Also, God can expand the grave. Think about how these points address each specific example in the objection.
The answer uses the concept of God's omnipotence and hidden realities.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Summarize how the author refutes the rational objection without denying the observable facts. The key is to recognize that God's power is not limited by our usual experiences.
This prepares us for understanding other matters of the unseen (sam'iyyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقل فلأن اللذة والألم والمكالمة، ونحو ذلك تتوقف على الحياة المتوقفة على البنية والمزاج، ولأن الميت ربما يرى مدة بحالة من غير تحرك وتكلم . وربما يدفن في مضيق لا يتصور جلوسه فيه . وربما يحرق فتذروه الرياح رماداً، وتجويز حياته وعذابه ليس بأبعد من تجويز سرير الميت وكلامه وعذابه . والجواب - أنه لا عبرة بالاستبعاد مع إخبار الصادق على أنه لو سلم اشتراط الحياة بالبنية، فلا يبعد أن يبقى من الأجزاء الأصلية ما يصلح بنيته، وأن يكون التعذيب والمسألة مع الروح أو الأجزاء الأصلية، فلا يشاهده الناظر، وأن يوسع القادر المختار اللحد بحيث يمكن الجلوس
And as for reason, because pleasure, pain, speech, and the like depend on life, which depends on bodily structure and temperament; and because the dead person may be seen for a period in a state without movement or speech; and he may be buried in a narrow space in which it is unimaginable for him to sit; and he may be burned and the winds scatter his ashes. And to allow his life and punishment is not more far-fetched than allowing the questioning, speech, and punishment of a dead person. The answer: That there is no consideration for improbability when the truthful One has informed. Moreover, even if we grant that life depends on bodily structure, it is not improbable that some original parts remain that can form his structure; and that the punishment and questioning occur with the soul or the original parts, so the observer does not see it; and that the Omnipotent and Free Agent expands the grave such that sitting is possible.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage addresses a rational objection to life in the grave. The objectors argue that pleasure, pain, speech, and similar experiences require life, and life depends on a bodily structure and temperament (al-binya wa al-mizāj). They point out that the dead are often observed lying motionless and silent; they may be buried in a cramped grave where sitting is impossible; or they may be cremated and their ashes scattered. Thus, it seems unreasonable to attribute life, questioning, or punishment to such bodies. The author responds by asserting that probability is irrelevant when the truthful (al-ṣādiq, i.e., God and His messenger) have informed us of this reality. Even if we accept that life normally requires a bodily structure, it is not far-fetched that some original parts (al-ajzā' al-aṣliyya) remain to form a suitable structure for the life of the grave. Moreover, the punishment and questioning may occur with the soul (rūḥ) or those original parts, invisible to human observers. Finally, the Omnipotent and Free Agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can expand the grave to accommodate sitting. Thus, the rational objection is refuted by appealing to divine omnipotence and the possibility of a hidden mode of life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life in the grave is impossible because dead bodies cannot sustain life.
Correction: God can create life in the grave without the usual bodily conditions, as He has informed us.

Checkpoint

A) Life in the grave is possible only if the body remains intact.
B) The objection is invalid because divine omnipotence can create life and punishment in ways unseen.

D) Rational arguments have no place in theological discussions.

Key Takeaway
The rational objection to life in the grave is overcome by affirming divine omnipotence and the primacy of revelation over human expectations of what is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The passage states that punishment may be experienced by the soul or original parts, hidden from observers. Are you living as if your actions—those unseen by people—are still recorded and will be experienced by your very soul in the grave? If not, which sins do you need to repent from now, before you face that reality alone?

Refuting the Impossibility of Life in the Grave
Objection against life in the grave and its theological refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic scholars use divine omnipotence and prophetic authority to answer naturalistic objections about bodily resurrection in the grave.

Core Idea
Divine power overrides all physical improbabilities.

The idea of life after death raises obvious physical questions: how can a decomposed or cremated body experience reward or punishment? This objection-and-answer passage shows the classical Sunni approach, grounding the doctrine in God's absolute power and the truthfulness of revelation rather than physical possibility. As you read, notice how the objection lists several physical impossibilities and how the answer pivots from those concerns to theological principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's difficulties
Read the first part of the passage and list the three physical scenarios the objector presents as impossible for life in the grave.
The objector uses observable facts about dead bodies.
2
Analyze the answer's strategy
Notice how the answer first dismisses the relevance of physical impossibility by appealing to the truthful informant, then offers three alternative explanations.
The answer does not deny the physical facts but offers theological possibilities.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Ask yourself: why does the author consider the objection invalid? What does this teach about the relationship between reason and revelation in Islamic theology?
This passage shows that revelation and divine power take precedence over human assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على البنية والمزاج، ولأن الميت ربما يرى مدة بحالة من غير تحرك وتكلم . وربما يدفن في مضيق لا يتصور جلوسه فيه . وربما يحرق فتذروه الرياح رماداً، وتجويز حياته وعذابه ليس بأبعد من تجويز سرير الميت وكلامه وعذابه . والجواب - أنه لا عبرة بالاستبعاد مع إخبار الصادق على أنه لو سلم اشتراط الحياة بالبنية، فلا يبعد أن يبقى من الأجزاء الأصلية ما يصلح بنيته، وأن يكون التعذيب والمسألة مع الروح أو الأجزاء الأصلية، فلا يشاهده الناظر، وأن يوسع القادر المختار اللحد بحيث يمكن الجلوس).
Based on structure and temperament, and because the dead person may be seen for a period in a state without movement or speech. And perhaps he is buried in a narrow space where sitting cannot be imagined. And perhaps he is burned and the winds scatter his ashes, and allowing his life and punishment is not more far-fetched than allowing the bed of the dead person and his speech and punishment. And the answer: that there is no consideration for improbability when the truthful one has informed. Even if we accept that life is conditional upon structure, it is not far-fetched that some of the original parts remain that suffice for his structure, and that the punishment and questioning occur with the spirit or the original parts, so the observer does not see it, and that the All-Powerful Chosen One expands the grave such that sitting is possible.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that life in the grave is impossible because the dead body lacks the biological structure and temperament for life, as it lies motionless and speechless. Moreover, the body may be buried in a narrow crevice where sitting cannot be conceived, or it may be cremated and scattered as ashes. The objector claims that allowing life and punishment in such a state is no more absurd than allowing the dead person's bed to speak. The answer dismisses the objection by stating that mere improbability carries no weight when a truthful informant (the Prophet, peace be upon him) has reported it. Even if one assumes that life requires a bodily structure, it is not far-fetched that some original parts of the body remain enough to reconstitute that structure. Additionally, punishment and questioning could be with the spirit or the original parts, invisible to onlookers. Lastly, God, the All-Powerful and Free Chooser, can expand the grave to allow sitting. The key point is that divine omnipotence and prophetic truthfulness override human assumptions about physical impossibility.

Key Takeaway
Life in the grave is possible because God is all-powerful and the Prophet informed us of it, regardless of physical appearance.

Consensus and Scriptural Proofs for the Questioning and Punishment in the Grave
The author cites consensus and scriptural proofs for the questioning and punishment in the grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus among Muslims on the reality of the questioning and punishment in the grave, and the scriptural proofs that support this doctrine.

Core Idea
The grave's questioning and punishment are established by consensus and clear scriptural evidence.

The author begins by affirming the consensus of all Muslims on the reality of the questioning by Munkar and Nakir and the punishment of disbelievers and some sinners in the grave. He then clarifies a misconception about the Mu'tazila and proceeds to cite verses and hadiths as evidence. This lesson examines those proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: consensus, clarification, then proofs.
The passage uses a dense scholarly style with embedded scriptural references.
2
Understand the consensus and clarification
Note that the author claims consensus (ijma) of all Muslims on the issue, and then clarifies that only Dirar bin Amr is known to deny it, not the Mu'tazila as a whole.
This shows the importance of defending Sunni doctrine against attribution to deviant groups.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
List the four verses cited and how each supports the doctrine: Pharaoh's family, people of Noah, two deaths/honor, and martyrs.
Each verse is interpreted to refer to an intermediate state before the Resurrection.
4
Examine the hadith proofs
Identify the hadiths mentioned: the grave as garden/pit, the two graves, the two angels, and the Prophet's prayers. These reinforce the doctrine from prophetic tradition.
The author describes them as mutawatir in meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في سؤال القبر وعذابه. اتفق الإسلاميون على حقية سؤال منكر ونكير في القبر وعذاب الكفار وبعض العصاة فيه . ونسب خلافه إلى بعض المعتزلة . قال بعض المتأخرين منهم : حكى إنكار ذلك عن ضرار بن عمرو). وإنما نسب إلى المعتزلة وهم براء منه لمخالطة ضرار إياهم وتبعه قوم من السفهاء المعاندين للحق لنا الآيات كقوله تعالى في آل فرعون النار يعرضون عليها غدواً وعشياً ) أي قبل القيامة، وذلك في القبر بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿ ويوم تقوم الساعة أدخلوا آل فرعون أشد العذاب ﴾ وكقوله تعالى من قوم نوح: أغرقوا فأدخلوا ناراً) والفاء للتعقيب وكقوله تعالى : وربنا أمتنا اثنتين وأحييتنا اثنتين )) وإحدى الحياتين ليست إلا في القبر، ولا يكون إلا لأنموذج ثواب أو عقاب بالاتفاق، وكقوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا تحسبن الذين قتلوا في سبيل الله أمواتا بل أحياء عند ربهم يرزقون فرحين بما آتاهم الله ﴾ والأحاديث المتواترة المعنى كقوله : ( القبر روضة من رياض الجنة أو حفرة من حفر النيران ) وكما روي أنه مر بقبرين فقال: «إنهما ليعذبان . . الحديث. وكالحديث المعروف في الملكين اللذين يدخلان القبر، ومعهما مرزبتان فيسألان الميت عن ربه، وعن دينه ، وعن نبيه . . إلى غير ذلك من الأخبار والآثار المسطورة في الكتب المشهورة. وقد تواتر عن النبي استعادته من عذاب القبر، واستفاض ذلك في الأدعية المأثورة
Concerning the questioning of the grave and its punishment. The Muslims have agreed on the reality of the questioning of Munkar and Nakir in the grave and the punishment of the disbelievers and some of the disobedient therein. Its disagreement is attributed to some of the Mu'tazila. Some of the later ones among them said: Denial of that was narrated from Dirar bin Amr. It is only attributed to the Mu'tazila, and they are innocent of it, because of Dirar's mixing with them and some foolish people resistant to the truth followed him. We have verses such as His saying concerning the family of Pharaoh: 'The Fire, they are exposed to it morning and evening' i.e. before the Resurrection, and that is in the grave, as evidence of His saying: 'And on the Day the Hour comes, admit the family of Pharaoh to the severest punishment.' And His saying concerning the people of Noah: 'They were drowned and then made to enter a Fire' and the fa' is for immediate sequence. And His saying: 'Our Lord, You have caused us to die twice and have given us life twice' and one of the two lives is only in the grave, and it is nothing but a sample of reward or punishment by consensus. And His saying: 'And never think of those who have been killed in the cause of Allah as dead; rather, they are alive with their Lord, receiving provision, rejoicing in what Allah has given them.' And the hadiths with recurrent meaning such as his saying: 'The grave is a garden from the gardens of Paradise or a pit from the pits of Hellfire.' And as it is narrated that he passed by two graves and said: 'Indeed they are being punished...' the hadith. And the well-known hadith about the two angels who enter the grave, with them two maces, and they ask the dead person about his Lord, his religion, and his Prophet... and so on from the reports and traditions recorded in famous books. And it has been recurrently narrated from the Prophet his seeking refuge from the punishment of the grave, and that is widespread in the transmitted supplications.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents positive evidence for the reality of the questioning and punishment in the grave. It begins by stating the consensus of all Muslims (Islamiyun) on the truth of the questioning by the two angels Munkar and Nakir and on the punishment of disbelievers and some sinful believers in the grave. It notes that disagreement is attributed to some Mu'tazila, but clarifies that the view of Dirar bin Amr, who denied it, should not be imputed to the Mu'tazila as a whole; rather, his mixing with them led to false attribution, and only some foolish people followed him. Then the author provides scriptural proofs. First, the verse about the family of Pharaoh being exposed to the Fire morning and evening is interpreted as referring to punishment in the grave before the Resurrection, because another verse says that on the Day of Judgment they will be admitted to the severest punishment, indicating a prior punishment. Second, the verse about the people of Noah: 'They were drowned and then made to enter a Fire' uses the particle 'fa' for immediate sequence, implying punishment in the grave. Third, the verse 'You have caused us to die twice and given us life twice' is explained such that one of the two lives occurs in the grave, which is a sample of reward or punishment by consensus. Fourth, the verse about martyrs being alive with their Lord is taken as evidence of life in the grave. Then hadiths are cited: the well-known hadith that the grave is either a garden of Paradise or a pit of Hell; the hadith about the Prophet passing by two graves and saying they are being punished; the hadith about the two angels entering the grave with maces and questioning the deceased; and the numerous transmitted reports of the Prophet seeking refuge from the punishment of the grave in his supplications. This collection of evidence is meant to establish the doctrine through multiple sources.

Correction: The author clarifies that only Dirar bin Amr and his followers denied it, and the Mu'tazila are innocent of this view.

Key Takeaway
The questioning and punishment in the grave are affirmed by consensus, multiple Quranic verses, and authentic hadiths, making it an established doctrine in Sunni Islam.

Interpreting 'Except the First Death'
The author refutes the objection from the verse about only one death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'لا يذوقون فيها الموت إلا الموتة الأولى' is interpreted to refute the denial of life in the grave and why it does not contradict the possibility of an intermediate state.

Core Idea
The verse only denies death in Paradise, not the life before resurrection.

This lesson addresses a key objection raised by those who deny the reality of life in the grave. They cite a Quranic verse that seems to indicate only one death before Paradise. The author explains the correct interpretation of the exception in this verse, showing that it does not negate the possibility of life in the grave. We will follow his logical refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the objection and the author's response.
Pay attention to the key terms: 'الاستثناء المنقطع' and 'الاستثناء المتصل'.
2
Identify the objection
The deniers argue that if there were life in the grave, there would be two deaths before Paradise, contradicting the verse. Understand this argument.
3
Understand the two interpretations
The author gives two possible explanations: the exception is discontinuous or continuous but hyperbolic. Compare them.
The discontinuous exception means 'but they already tasted the first death'. The hyperbolic one means even if death were possible, it would be the first already past.
4
Connect to the broader argument
See how this interpretation preserves the possibility of life in the grave. The verse only denies death in Paradise, not the intermediate state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك المنكرون بالسمع والعقل. أما السمع، وهو للمعترفين بظواهر الشرائع فقوله تعالى : لا يذوقون فيها الموت إلا الموتة الأولى . ولو كان في القبر حياة - ولا محالة - يعقبها موت إذ لا خلاف في إحياء الحشر لكان لهم قبل دخول الجنة موتتان لا موتة واحدة فقط . فإن قيل : ما معنى هذا الاستثناء، ومعلوم أن لا موت في الجنة اصلاً، ولو فرض فلا يتصور ذوق الموتة الأولى فيها . قلنا : هو منقطع . أي لكن ذاقوا الموتة الأولى، أو متصل على قصد المبالغة في عدم انقطاع نعيم الجنة بالموت بمنزلة تعليقه بالمحال، أي لو أمكنت فيها موتة ، لكانت الموتة الأولى التي مضت وانقضت، لكن ذلك محال .
The deniers have held fast to both transmitted and rational arguments. As for the transmitted, which is for those who acknowledge the apparent meanings of the revealed laws, it is His saying: 'They do not taste death therein except the first death.' And if there were life in the grave—inevitably followed by death, since there is no dispute about the resurrection of the gathering—then for them before entering Paradise there would be two deaths, not only one death. Then it is said: What is the meaning of this exception, when it is known that there is no death in Paradise at all, and if it were assumed, the tasting of the first death in it cannot be conceived? We say: It is discontinuous (munqati'), meaning 'but they tasted the first death', or continuous on the purpose of hyperbole in the non-interruption of the bliss of Paradise by death, like conditioning it on the impossible, i.e., if death were possible in it, it would be the first death that has passed and gone, but that is impossible.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the deniers of punishment in the grave, who use both transmitted and rational arguments. The transmitted argument is based on the verse from Surah al-Dukhan (44:56): 'They do not taste death therein except the first death.' The deniers argue that if there were life in the grave after the first death, then that life would be followed by another death before the resurrection, resulting in two deaths before entering Paradise, contradicting the verse which mentions only one death. The author then presents an objection to this interpretation: the exception in the verse is problematic because there is no death in Paradise at all, so what does 'except the first death' mean? The answer is that the exception is 'munqati'' (discontinuous), meaning that the exception is not really from the same category; rather, it means 'but they have already tasted the first death (in the world)'. Alternatively, the exception could be 'muttasil' (continuous) but used hyperbolically to emphasize that the bliss of Paradise is never interrupted by death, as if to say that if death could occur there, it would be that first death which is already past—but that is impossible. Thus, the verse does not negate life in the grave; it simply affirms that there is no death after entering Paradise. The key point is that the verse is not addressing the period between death and resurrection, so it does not contradict the possibility of life and punishment in the grave.

الاستثناء المنقطع
الاستثناء المتصل
المبالغة
تعليقه بالمحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves there is no life until the Day of Judgment because it says only one death is tasted.
Correction: The verse only negates death after entering Paradise; it does not address the period between death and resurrection. The exception can be interpreted discontinously or hyperbolically, preserving the possibility of life in the grave.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain the exception in the verse 'لا يذوقون فيها الموت إلا الموتة الأولى'?
A) The exception is continuous, meaning they will taste death only in Paradise
B) The exception is discontinuous, meaning they have already tasted the first death, or hyperbolic to emphasize the eternity of Paradise
C) The exception proves that there is only one death and no life in between
D) The exception indicates that the first death occurs after resurrection

Key Takeaway
The verse 'لا يذوقون فيها الموت إلا الموتة الأولى' does not deny life in the grave; it only emphasizes that Paradise has no death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'لا يذوقون فيها الموت إلا الموتة الأولى', do you immediately assume you understand its meaning, or do you pause to consider that it might have a deeper interpretation that requires scholarly explanation? What does that say about your reliance on your own reasoning versus the guidance of scholars?
?
The deniers in this passage used a verse to reject a belief—punishment in the grave—that many Muslims accept. Is there any belief or practice in your own life that you have dismissed based on a superficial reading of a text? Will you commit to re-examining that assumption with the help of reliable scholars?
?
This lesson shows that an apparent contradiction in the Quran can be resolved by understanding linguistic nuances like discontinuous exceptions. When you encounter something in the Quran that seems to contradict other teachings, do you trust that there is a valid explanation, or do you become skeptical? How can you cultivate a deeper trust in Allah's Book while seeking knowledge?

Evidences for Punishment in the Grave
Taftazani enumerates the proofs for punishment in the grave from consensus, Quran, and mutawatir hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and hadith evidence that establishes the reality of punishment in the grave according to Taftazani.

Core Idea
The punishment of the grave is proven by consensus, Quran, mutawatir hadith, and prophetic practice.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid lays out the key proofs for the doctrine of punishment in the grave. He begins with the consensus of the scholars, then cites a Quranic verse indicating life in the barzakh, and follows with several hadiths that are widely transmitted in meaning. This combined evidence establishes the reality of this eschatological event as a matter of belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the order: consensus, Quran, hadith, prophetic practice.
2
Identify the order of proofs
List the proofs Taftazani mentions in the exact order he presents them.
He starts with consensus, then Quran, then hadith examples, then the Prophet's seeking refuge.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at verse 3:169. How does it imply life in the barzakh and thus punishment or reward?
Martyrs are described as alive and receiving provision.
4
Reflect on the mutawatir hadiths
Consider the hadiths about the grave being a garden or pit, and the two angels. Why are they considered mutawatir in meaning?
Multiple narrations convey the same essential message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالاتفاق، وكقوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا تحسبن الذين قتلوا في سبيل الله أمواتا بل أحياء عند ربهم يرزقون فرحين بما آتاهم الله ) (۱) والأحاديث المتواترة المعنى كقوله : ( القبر روضة من رياض الجنة أو حفرة من حفر النيران (۲) وكما روي أنه مر بقبرين فقال: «إنهما ليعذبان . . (۳) الحديث. وكالحديث المعروف في الملكين اللذين يدخلان القبر، ومعهما مرزبتان فيسألان الميت عن ربه، وعن دينه ، وعن نبيه . . إلى غير ذلك من الأخبار والآثار المسطورة في الكتب المشهورة. وقد تواتر عن النبي استعادته من عذاب القبر، واستفاض ذلك في الأدعية المأثورة
By consensus, and as His statement the Exalted: 'And do not think those who are killed in the way of Allah are dead; rather, they are alive with their Lord, being provided for, rejoicing in what Allah has given them' (1), and the hadiths mutawatir in meaning like his statement (peace be upon him): 'The grave is a garden from the gardens of Paradise or a pit from the pits of the Fire' (2), and as it is narrated that he passed by two graves and said: 'Indeed, they are both being punished...' (3) the hadith. And like the well-known hadith about the two angels who enter the grave, with them two iron maces, and they ask the deceased about his Lord, his religion, and his prophet... to other such reports and traditions recorded in well-known books. And it has been reported in tawatur from the Prophet (peace be upon him) his seeking refuge from the punishment of the grave, and that is widespread in the transmitted supplications.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the proofs for the punishment of the grave. Taftazani begins by stating that it is established by consensus (الاتفاق). Then he cites Quran 3:169, which indicates that martyrs are alive in the barzakh, implying life in the grave. Next, he adduces hadiths that are mutawatir in meaning, such as the Prophet's description of the grave as a garden of Paradise or a pit of Hellfire, and the hadith about the Prophet passing by two graves and saying they are being punished, and the well-known hadith about the two angels questioning the deceased. Finally, he notes that the Prophet himself used to seek refuge from punishment of the grave, and this is widely reported in supplications. All this evidence establishes the reality of punishment in the grave through transmitted proofs.

الأحاديث المتواترة المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The punishment of the grave is only metaphorical or allegorical.
Correction: Taftazani presents it as literal based on consensus, Quran, and mutawatir hadith, which indicate a real experience.

Refuting the Denial Argument from Quran 44:56
Taftazani responds to an objection based on Quran 44:56 regarding the number of deaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani interprets the exception in Quran 44:56 to defend the doctrine of life in the grave.

Core Idea
The exception in 44:56 does not prove only one death; it can be read as disconnected or hyperbolic.

This passage presents an objection from deniers of punishment in the grave based on Quran 44:56. They argue that the verse states Paradise inhabitants taste no death except the first death, implying only one death. But if there were life in the grave followed by death at resurrection, that would mean two deaths. Taftazani raises a further question about the meaning of the exception and provides two plausible interpretations that neutralize the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note how the objection is structured and how Taftazani responds.
The passage uses a dialectical format (فإن قيل ... قلنا).
2
Identify the deniers' argument
Find where the deniers cite Quran 44:56 and their logical inference that only one death occurs, contradicting life in the grave.
The key phrase is 'لا يذوقون فيها الموت إلا الموتة الأولى'.
3
Understand the dialectical question
Taftazani poses a further objection: if there is no death in Paradise, how can the exception even be meaningful? Grasp why this question arises.
The question challenges the straightforward reading of the exception.
4
Learn the two interpretations
Study Taftazani's answer: he offers both a disconnected (munqati') and connected (muttasil) interpretation, each resolving the difficulty.
The disconnected reading makes the exception apply to worldly death; the connected reading makes it hyperbolic.
5
Reflect on the impact
Consider how these interpretations preserve the possibility of life in the grave without contradicting the Quranic verse.
This shows the flexibility of Arabic grammar in theological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك المنكرون بالسمع والعقل. أما السمع، وهو للمعترفين بظواهر الشرائع فقوله تعالى : لا يذوقون فيها الموت إلا الموتة الأولى (٤) . ولو كان في القبر حياة - ولا محالة - يعقبها موت إذ لا خلاف في إحياء الحشر لكان لهم قبل دخول الجنة موتتان لا موتة واحدة فقط فإن قيل : ما معنى هذا الاستثناء، ومعلوم أن لا موت في الجنة اصلاً، ولــو فرض فلا يتصور ذوق الموتة الأولى فيها . قلنا : هو منقطع . أي لكن ذاقوا الموتة الأولى، أو متصل على قصد المبالغة في عدم انقطاع نعيم الجنة بالموت بمنزلة تعليقه بالمحال، أي لو أمكنت فيها موتة ، لكانت الموتة الأولى التي مضت وانقضت، لكن ذلك محال .
The deniers cling to scripture and reason. As for scripture—and this is for those who acknowledge the apparent meanings of the laws—it is His statement the Exalted: 'They shall not taste death therein except the first death' (44:56). And if there were life in the grave—without doubt—followed by death (since there is no disagreement about the resurrection), then they would have before entering Paradise two deaths, not just one death. If it is said: What is the meaning of this exception, when it is known that there is no death in Paradise at all, and if it were supposed, tasting the first death therein cannot be conceived? We say: It is disconnected (munqati‘). That is, but they tasted the first death [in the world], or it is connected (muttasil) for the purpose of hyperbole in the continuity of the bliss of Paradise without death, akin to linking it to the impossible. That is, if death were possible in it, it would have been the first death which has passed and ended, but that is impossible.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from deniers of punishment in the grave based on Quran 44:56. They argue that the verse states the inhabitants of Paradise will not taste death except the first death, implying only one death. If there were life in the grave followed by another death before resurrection, that would entail two deaths, contradicting the verse. Then Taftazani raises a further question (فإن قيل) about the meaning of the exception, given that there is no death in Paradise at all. The answer (قلنا) explains that the exception is either munqati‘ (disconnected), meaning the excepted thing is not of the same kind—so it means they have already tasted the first death in the world—or muttasil (connected) as a hyperbole, meaning that if death were possible in Paradise, it would be the first death, but that is impossible. This answer refutes the denial.

Key Terms
الموتة الأولى
الاستثناء المنقطع
الاستثناء المتصل
المبالغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The exception in 44:56 straightforwardly proves there is only one death, negating life in the grave.
Correction: Taftazani shows that the exception can be interpreted as disconnected (referring to worldly death) or hyperbolic (emphasizing eternal bliss), thus it does not rule out life in the grave.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani interpret the exception in Quran 44:56?
A) As a disconnected exception meaning they already tasted death in the world
B) As a connected exception meaning no death in Paradise
C) Both A and B depending on context
D) As a proof of life in the grave

Key Takeaway
Taftazani refutes the objection by interpreting the exception in Quran 44:56 as either disconnected or hyperbolic, preserving the possibility of life in the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How did you respond—with dismissal or with effort to understand deeper interpretations like Taftazani's? Will you commit to not taking any verse at face value when it conflicts with well-established teachings of Islam?
?
Taftazani offers two interpretations for the same verse, both valid. Are you comfortable with the idea that divine speech can sustain multiple meanings? Or do you feel the need for a single 'correct' interpretation? What does your comfort (or discomfort) reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom and the scholars' depth? Resolve to embrace the richness of Quranic interpretation and to seek knowledge from those who can explain it.

The Meaning of 'First Death' in the Quran
Taftazani answers the argument that the word 'first' in the verse implies a second death, which would be after life in the grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the objection that the word 'first' in Quran 44:56 implies a second death.

Core Idea
'First' does not necessitate a second; it can be relative to a hypothetical.

This lesson addresses a key objection to using Quran 44:56 as evidence for the finality of death. Critics argue that describing death as 'the first' implies a second death, which would occur after life in the grave. Taftazani responds by clarifying that 'the first' is relative to any death imagined in Paradise, thus the verse actually supports the intended argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the gist of the objection and response.
Notice the structure: 'If it is said... We say...'
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that describing death as 'the first' linguistically implies a second death, which would occur after life in the grave. This would make the verse counterproductive for the proponent.
3
Grasp Taftazani's response
Taftazani explains that 'the first' is relative to any death that might be imagined in Paradise. The purpose of the verse is to negate any possibility of death in Paradise, not to affirm a second death.
The word 'first' is used rhetorically, not to indicate an actual sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : وصف الموتة بالأولى يشعر بموتة ثانية ، وليست إلا بعد إحياء القبر، فتكون الآية حجة على المتمسك لا له. قلنا : المراد بالأولى بالنسبة إلى ما يتوهم في الجنة، ويقصد نفيها .
If it is said: The description of death as 'the first' suggests a second death, and it would be only after the reviving of the grave, so the verse becomes an argument against the one who clings to it, not for him. We say: By 'the first' is meant relative to what is imagined in Paradise, and its intention is to negate it.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage addresses a second objection: The verse describes death as 'the first', which linguistically implies a second death. Deniers argue that this second death would occur after life in the grave, thus the verse actually supports their position. Taftazani responds that 'the first' is meant relative to any death that might be imagined in Paradise. The point of the verse is to negate the possibility of death in Paradise, not to affirm a second death. So the word 'first' is not referring to a sequence of actual deaths but is used rhetorically.

Refuting the Generic Interpretation of 'First Death'
Taftazani refutes a genus-based interpretation of 'first death' using morphology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Arabic morphology prevents interpreting 'al-mawtah al-ula' as a genus and forces it to denote a single occurrence.

Core Idea
Arabic suffixes reveal that 'first death' is a single event, not a genus.

In this passage, Taftazani confronts an objection that tries to interpret 'first death' broadly as the genus of death, which would include death in the grave. He responds by pointing to the grammatical structure of the word itself—the feminine suffix and the morphology for 'once'—which clearly indicate a single instance. Let's see how this linguistic insight closes the door on the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector suggests that 'first death' might not refer to a numerical one but to the genus of death that covers both worldly death and grave death. Notice that this interpretation would make the exception in the verse meaningless.
The objector uses 'al-jins' (genus) to argue for a broader reference.
2
Study Taftazani's reply
Taftazani rejects the objection by citing two morphological features: 'bina' al-marra' (the morphology indicating a single occurrence) and 'ta' al-wahdah' (the suffix of unity). These features force the word to denote a specific, single death.
The feminine suffix -ah in 'mawtah' is key.
3
Connect to the larger argument
Understand why this refutation matters: if 'first death' is a single instance, then the exception in the verse (except the second death) cannot be interpreted as including death in the grave. This supports the doctrine that everyone experiences one death except Paradise inhabitants.
This is part of Taftazani's refutation of arguments that try to reconcile with objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : يجوز أن لا يراد الواحد بالعدد بل الجنس المتحقق المقابل بهذا المتوهم على ما يتناول موتة الدنيا، وموتة القبر. قلنا : يأباه بناء المرة، وتاء الوحدة
If it is said: It is possible that it does not mean the numerical one but rather the realized genus, corresponding to what is imagined, that includes the death of this world and the death of the grave. We say: This is prevented by the morphology of 'once' (al-marra) and the suffix of unity (tā’ al-waḥdah).
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection about the word 'first' (الأولى). The objector suggests that 'first death' might not refer to a specific numerical instance, but to the genus of death that includes both the death of this world and the death in the grave. Thus, the exception would cover all deaths except that genus. Taftazani replies that the structure of the word 'first death' (الموتة الأولى) uses the feminine suffix -ah (تاء الوحدة) which indicates a single occurrence (المرة). This morphological feature prevents it from being interpreted as a generic reference. Therefore, it must refer to a specific, single death, not a genus.

Key Terms
الموتة الأولى
تاء الوحدة
بناء المرة
الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the word 'first death' could be interpreted generically to include death in the grave.
Correction: Taftazani shows that Arabic morphology (بناء المرة and تاء الوحدة) forces it to mean a single, specific death, not a genus.

Checkpoint

B) The feminine suffix indicating a single instance
C) The word 'first'
D) The context of Paradise

Key Takeaway
The Arabic morphology of 'first death' (المَوْتَة الأولى) proves it is a single occurrence, not a genus, thereby refuting the objection that it includes death in the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objector here wanted to avoid a clear truth by reinterpreting a word. Is there a command or belief in Islam that you have ever tried to 'interpret away' because it felt too heavy? Will you accept it as it is starting now?
?
One small suffix (تاء الوحدة) turned the entire meaning. How often do you dismiss seemingly minor details in the Quran or Sunnah? Will you commit to learning the Arabic language so that no such detail escapes you?

Two Deaths and Two Lives
Interpretation of the two deaths and two lives in Quran 40:11.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main interpretations of the 'two deaths and two lives' in Quran 40:11 and their implications for the doctrine of life in the grave.

Core Idea
The two deaths and two lives affirm the reality of the intermediate state (barzakh).

The Quranic verse 'Our Lord, You have made us die twice and given us life twice' (40:11) has been interpreted by Sunni scholars to include the life of the grave, which is central to the doctrine of barzakh. In this passage, the author presents two interpretations that harmonize the verse with the belief in an intermediate state. By studying these interpretations, you will see how kalam scholars defend the reality of the grave's life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two distinct interpretations marked by 'قيل' (it is said).
2
Identify the two interpretations
The first interpretation assigns deaths to this world and the grave, and lives to the grave and the hereafter. The second shifts the second life to the resurrection, noting a reason related to knowledge of Allah.
3
Understand the term 'معاين'
The author notes that the life in the hereafter is omitted from the enumeration in the first view because it is 'witnessed' (معاين) - meaning self-evident or beyond need of inferential proof.
4
Connect to barzakh
Both interpretations include life in the grave, supporting the Sunni belief in an intermediate state of awareness and experience between death and resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في قولهم : أمتنا اثنتين وأحييتنا اثنتين) فالإماتتان في الدنيا وفي القبر، وكذا الإحياءان، وترك ما في الآخرة لأنه معاين، وقيل: بل ما في القبر وما في الحشر، لأن المراد إحياء تعقبه معرفة ضرورية بالله واعتراف بالذنوب.
As for their statement: 'Our Lord, You have made us die twice and given us life twice' – the two deaths are in this world and the grave, and likewise the two lives, and what is in the hereafter is omitted because it is witnessed. And it is said: rather, what is in the grave and what is in the resurrection, because the intended life is followed by necessary knowledge of God and confession of sins.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage addresses the verse from Surah Ghafir (40:11). The author explains the two deaths and two lives. Some say the two deaths are in this world and the grave, and the two lives are in the grave and the hereafter. Others say the two lives are in the grave and the resurrection, because the life in the grave leads to recognition of God. This interpretation supports the Sunni belief in life in the grave (barzakh).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only refers to this world and the hereafter, excluding any life in the grave.
Correction: Both interpretations included in this passage assign a death and a life to the grave, affirming the Sunni doctrine of barzakh.

Checkpoint

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The life in the grave is a foretaste of what is to come. If you were to die tonight, would that foretaste be sweet or bitter? And what are you doing right now to change that?
?
The scholars disagree on the exact timing of the two deaths and two lives, but all affirm a conscious state in the grave. Are you living as if death is merely annihilation, or as if it is the door to a life of accountability? How would your daily choices change if you truly internalized that you will be revived twice?

Quran 35:22 as a Simile for Disbelievers
Explanation of Quran 35:22 as a simile for disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rhetorical nature of Quran 35:22 as a simile comparing disbelievers to dead people, and why this interpretation avoids conflict with the doctrine of life in the grave.

Core Idea
Quran 35:22 is a simile, not a literal denial of hearing in the grave.

This lesson unpacks a concise but theologically significant point from the text of Sharh al-Maqasid. The author explains a key verse often cited in discussions about the state of disbelievers after death. By recognizing the verse as a simile (tamthil), we can reconcile it with other texts that affirm life in the grave.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that the verse is a simile (tamthil). Notice that he does not say the dead cannot hear; he says the verse is a comparison.
3
Understand the implication
This interpretation allows for the possibility that the dead in graves may have a different form of life or hearing, thus preserving the doctrine of barzakh life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿وما أنت بمسمع من في القبور﴾ فتمثيل لحال الكفرة بحال الموتى ولا نزاع في أن الميت لا يسمع.
As for His statement: 'And you are not one to make hear those who are in the graves' – it is a simile for the state of the disbelievers with the state of the dead, and there is no dispute that the dead does not hear.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage clarifies the meaning of Quran 35:22. The author states that it is a simile comparing disbelievers to dead people who cannot hear. It does not actually deny that the dead in the graves might have a different form of life or hearing; it is merely a rhetorical comparison. This interpretation allows for the possibility of life in the grave.

Key Terms

Misconception: The verse literally denies that the dead in graves can hear anything.
Correction: The verse is a simile for the disbelievers' state; it does not negate the possibility of hearing or life in the grave in a different sense.

Quran 35:22 uses the simile of dead people to describe disbelievers, not to deny the possibility of life in the grave.

Rational Objections and Refutation on Life in the Grave
Presentation and refutation of rational arguments against life in the grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational objections to life in the grave and the theological refutation that upholds divine omnipotence and the authority of revelation.

Core Idea
Rational improbability does not negate God's power or the truth of revelation.

The passage presents a series of rational arguments against the possibility of life and punishment in the grave. These arguments rely on observable physical phenomena. The author then refutes them, first summarily by appealing to divine power and revelation, then in detail by proposing alternative mechanisms such as punishment of the spirit or remaining body parts. The refutation also addresses the charge of sophistry and mentions deviant views.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the structure: objections followed by refutation.
Note that the passage ends mid-sentence discussing Ibn al-Rawandi.
2
Identify the rational objections
List the main objections: life requires a physical structure, dead bodies show no movement or sensation, decomposition and consumption destroy the body.
The objector also compares to other miracles like the speaking bed.
3
Study the summary refutation
The author says these are mere improbabilities, not impossibilities, and since the truthful messenger reported them, belief is necessary.
This is a key principle: revelation overrides rational improbability.
4
Examine the detailed refutation
The author offers four points: life may not require physical structure; punishment can be for the spirit or original parts; God can conceal the process; God can preserve a grain of dust.
These respond to specific objections.
5
Note the deviant views
The passage mentions al-Salihiyya and al-Karamiyya (punishment without life) and Ibn al-Rawandi (life in every dead). Consider why these are considered problematic.
The passage is incomplete on these views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقل فلأن اللذة والألم والمسألة والتكلم ونحو ذلك لا يتصور بدون العلم والحياة، ولا حياة مع فساد البنية وبطلان المزاج ولو سلم فإنا نرى الميت أو المقتول أو المصلوب يبقى مدة من غير تحرك وتكلم ، ولا أثر تلذذ أو تألم، وربما يدفن في صندوق أو لحد ضيق لا يتصور فيه جلوسه على ما ورد في الخبر، وربما يذر على صدره كف من الذرة فترى باقية على حالها بل ربما يأكله السباع أو تحرقه النار، فيصير رماداً تذروه الرياح في المشارق والمغارب . فكيف يعقل حياته وعذابه وسؤاله وجوابه؟ وتجويز ذلك سفسطة . وليس بأبعد من تجويز حياة سرير الميت وكلامه وتعذيب خشبة المصلوب واحتراقها. ونحن نراها بحالها . والجواب إجمالاً - أن جميع ما ذكرتم استبعادات لا تنفي الإمكان كسائر خوارق العادات، وإذ قد أخبر الصادق بها، لزم التصديق . وتفصيلاً - أنا لا نسلم اشتراط الحياة بالبنية، ولو سلم فيجوز أن يبقى من الأجزاء قدر ما يصلح بنية، والتعذيب والمسألة يجوز أن يكون للروح الذي هو أجسام لطيفة، أو للأجزاء الأصلية الباقية، فلا يمتنع أن لا يشاهده الناظر ، ولا أن يخفيه الله تعالى عن الإنس والجن لحكمة لا اطلاع لنا عليها، ولا أن يتحقق مع كون الميت في بطون السباع . ومن قال بالقادر المختار المحيي المميت لا يستبعد توسيع اللحد والصندوق، ولا حفظ الذرة على صدر المتحرك، والقول بأن تجويز أمثال ذلك يفضي إلى السفسطة إنما يصح فيما لم يقم عليه الدليل، ولم يخبر به الصادق، وأما ما يقول به الصالحية والكرامية من جواز التعذيب بدون الحياة، لأنها ليست شرطاً للإدراك، وابن الراوندي () من أن الحياة موجودة في كل ميت لأن الموت ليس
As for the intellect, because pleasure, pain, questioning, speech and the like cannot be conceived without knowledge and life, and there is no life with corruption of the bodily structure and nullification of the temperament. And even if we concede, we see the dead person, the slain, or the crucified remains for a period without movement or speech, and no trace of pleasure or pain. Perhaps he is buried in a box or a narrow niche in which sitting is not conceivable as reported in the tradition. Perhaps a handful of ants is placed on his chest and it remains in its state, or perhaps beasts eat him or fire burns him, so he becomes ashes that the winds scatter in the east and west. So how can his life, punishment, questioning, and answering be conceived? And allowing that is sophistry. And it is not more far-fetched than allowing the life of the bed of the dead, the speech of the dead, and the torture of the wood of the crucified and its burning, while we see them in their state. And the answer in summary: that all that you have mentioned are improbabilities that do not negate possibility, like other extraordinary things, and since the truthful informer has informed of them, affirmation is necessary. And in detail: we do not concede the condition of life by physical structure. And even if we concede, it is possible that enough parts remain to form a structure. And punishment and questioning may be for the spirit, which is a subtle body, or for the remaining original parts, so there is no impossibility that the observer does not see it, nor that God conceals it from humans and jinn for a wisdom we have no knowledge of, nor that it occurs while the dead is in the bellies of beasts. And one who affirms the omnipotent, free agent, who gives life and death, does not consider it far-fetched to widen the grave and the box, nor to preserve a grain of dust on the chest of the moving. And the claim that allowing such things leads to sophistry is only valid regarding what no proof has been established and the truthful has not informed of. As for what the Salihiyya and Karamiyya say, allowing punishment without life because life is not a condition for perception, and Ibn al-Rawandi, that life exists in every dead person because death is not...
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents rational objections against the possibility of life and punishment in the grave, followed by a detailed refutation. The objector argues that life requires a physical structure and that dead bodies decompose, making life impossible. The author responds by summarizing that these are merely improbabilities, not impossibilities, and since revelation has confirmed it, belief is required. In detail, he argues that life does not necessarily require a particular physical structure; punishment may be experienced by the spirit or by the remaining original parts; God can conceal the process; and such events are no more miraculous than other supernatural occurrences. The passage concludes with a mention of deviant views, including those who allow punishment without life (al-Salihiyya and al-Karamiyya) and the claim that life exists in every dead person (Ibn al-Rawandi).

مزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life in the grave is rationally impossible because dead bodies decompose and show no signs of life.
Correction: The author argues that such improbabilities do not negate possibility, especially when confirmed by revelation; God's power can overcome natural limitations.

Checkpoint

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objectors in this passage rely entirely on what they can see: decay, immobility, destruction. Where in your own life are you letting 'what you see' override 'what Allah has promised'? Will you today choose to trust His Word over your limited observation, even if it feels uncomfortable?

If you truly believed that Allah can preserve a grain of dust on a moving corpse, would you fear anything else? Defending the Possibility of Life in the Grave
Author presents objections to life in the grave and gives detailed answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians defend the possibility of life in the grave against rational objections, emphasizing divine omnipotence and the role of prophetic testimony.

Core Idea
Life in the grave is possible because God is omnipotent and the truthful Prophet informed us of it.

The belief in life in the grave (hayat al-qabr) is a core element of Sunni eschatology, but rational objections have been raised against it. In this passage, the author presents the strongest objections and then provides both a general and a detailed response, affirming that divine power transcends natural limitations. He also refutes deviant views that either deny life or redefine it incorrectly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe that it begins with an objection, then gives a general answer and a detailed answer, and ends with a refutation of three deviant views.
This is a standard objection-and-answer (shubha wa jawab) format in kalam.
2
Identify the rational objections
List the specific difficulties raised: body decomposition, narrow grave, consumption by beasts or fire, etc. Understand why these seem to contradict life and sensation.
The opponent argues that these natural processes make life in the grave impossible.
3
Understand the general answer
The author responds that these are mere improbabilities (istib'adat) that do not negate possibility (imkan), because God performs miracles (kharq al-'adat). Since the truthful Prophet informed of these events, belief is necessary.
This principle is key: divine omnipotence overrides natural laws.
4
Analyze the detailed answer
The author makes several points: life is not conditional on the physical body; punishment may affect the spirit (ruh) or original parts; God can hide these events from human sight; preservation of the body is possible even when eaten by beasts.
These answers show the flexibility of theological reasoning.
5
Examine the refutation of deviant views
The author rejects three groups: al-Salihiyya and al-Karramiyya who allow punishment without life, and Ibn al-Rawandi who claims life remains in every dead person. These are declared false and contrary to Sunni principles.
This clarifies the boundaries of correct belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معاين، وقيل: بل ما في القبر وما في الحشر، لأن المراد إحياء تعقبه معرفة ضرورية بالله واعتراف بالذنوب. وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿وما أنت بمسمع من في القبور﴾ فتمثيل لحال الكفرة بحال الموتى ولا نزاع في أن الميت لا يسمع، أما العقل فلأن اللذة والألم والمسألة والتكلم، ونحو ذلك لا يتصور بدون العلم والحياة، ولا حياة مع فساد البنية وبطلان المزاج ولو سلم فإنا نرى الميت أو المقتول أو المصلوب يبقى مدة من غير تحرك وتكلم ، ولا أثر تلذذ أو تألم، وربما يدفن في صندوق أو لحد ضيق لا يتصور فيه جلوسه على ما ورد في الخبر، وربما يذر على صدره كف من الذرة فترى باقية على حالها بل ربما يأكله السباع أو تحرقه النار، فيصير رماداً تذروه الرياح في المشارق والمغارب . فكيف يعقل حياته وعذابه وسؤاله وجوابه؟ وتجويز ذلك سفسطة . وليس بأبعد من تجويز حياة سرير الميت وكلامه وتعذيب خشبة المصلوب واحتراقها. ونحن نراها بحالها . والجواب إجمالاً - أن جميع ما ذكرتم استبعادات لا تنفي الإمكان كسائر خوارق العادات، وإذ قد أخبر الصادق بها، لزم التصديق . وتفصيلاً - أنا لا نسلم اشتراط الحياة بالبنية، ولو سلم فيجوز أن يبقى من الأجزاء قدر ما يصلح بنية، والتعذيب والمسألة يجوز أن يكون للروح الذي هو أجسام لطيفة، أو للأجزاء الأصلية الباقية، فلا يمتنع أن لا يشاهده الناظر ، ولا أن يخفيه الله تعالى عن الإنس والجن لحكمة لا اطلاع لنا عليها، ولا أن يتحقق مع كون الميت في بطون السباع . ومن قال بالقادر المختار المحيي المميت لا يستبعد توسيع اللحد والصندوق، ولا حفظ الذرة على صدر المتحرك، والقول بأن تجويز أمثال ذلك يفضي إلى السفسطة إنما يصح فيما لم يقم عليه الدليل، ولم يخبر به الصادق، وأما ما يقول به الصالحية والكرامية من جواز التعذيب بدون الحياة، لأنها ليست شرطاً للإدراك، وابن الراوندي من أن الحياة موجودة في كل ميت لأن الموت ليس ضدا للحياة بل آفة كلية معجزة عن الأفعال الاختيارية، غير منافية للعلم فباطل لا يوافق أصول أهل الحق .
Seen? And it is said: Rather, what is in the grave and what is in the gathering, because the intended meaning is a revival followed by necessary knowledge of God and confession of sins. As for His saying, the Exalted: "You cannot make hear those who are in the graves," it is a simile of the state of the disbelievers to the state of the dead, and there is no dispute that the dead does not hear. As for reason, because pleasure, pain, questioning, speech, and the like are inconceivable without knowledge and life, and there is no life with corruption of the body and invalidation of temperament. Even if it is granted, we see the dead, the killed, or the crucified remains for a period without movement or speech, and no trace of pleasure or pain, and perhaps he is buried in a box or a narrow grave in which his sitting is inconceivable as reported in the tradition, and perhaps a handful of grain is scattered on his chest and it remains as it is, rather perhaps beasts eat him or fire burns him, so he becomes ashes that the winds scatter in the east and west. So how can his life, punishment, questioning, and answering be conceived? And deeming that permissible is sophistry. And it is not more improbable than deeming permissible the life of the bed of the dead, its speech, the torture of the wood of the crucified, and its burning. And we see them as they are. The answer in general: That all that you have mentioned are improbabilities that do not negate possibility, like other miraculous events, and since the truthful one has informed of them, affirmation is necessary. And in detail: We do not concede that life is conditional upon the body, and even if conceded, it is possible that a portion sufficient for a structure remains of the parts, and punishment and questioning may be for the spirit which is a subtle body, or for the remaining original parts, so it is not impossible that the observer does not see it, nor that God the Exalted hides it from humans and jinn for a wisdom we have no knowledge of, nor that it occurs while the dead is in the bellies of beasts. And whoever says with the Chosen Powerful, the Giver of life, the Giver of death, does not deem improbable the expansion of the grave and the box, nor the preservation of the grain on the chest of the moving one. And the statement that deeming such things permissible leads to sophistry is only valid concerning what no proof has been established for and the truthful one has not informed of. As for what the Salihiyya and Karramiyya say about the permissibility of punishment without life, because it is not a condition for perception, and Ibn al-Rawandi that life exists in every dead because death is not contrary to life but a total infirmity preventing voluntary actions, not incompatible with knowledge, it is false, not conforming to the principles of the people of truth.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence defending the Islamic belief in the life of the grave (barzakh). It begins by mentioning a previous discussion about the meaning of certain Quranic verses regarding resurrection. An opponent raises a rational objection: the dead body decomposes, is eaten, or burned, so how can it experience punishment, questioning, and speech? Such a thing is deemed sophistry. The author first gives a general answer: these are mere improbabilities (istib'adat) that do not negate possibility (imkan), since God is omnipotent and miracles (kharq al-'adat) occur. Since the truthful Prophet informed of these events, they must be believed. Then a detailed answer follows: the author denies that life is conditional upon the physical body (al-binya). Even if it were, enough of the body may remain. Punishment may be experienced by the spirit (al-ruh), which is a subtle body, or by the original parts that remain. God can hide this from human sight or preserve the body even when eaten by beasts. The author then refutes two deviant views: the Salihiyya and Karramiyya allow punishment without life altogether, and Ibn al-Rawandi claims that life is present in every dead person because death is not the opposite of life but a hindrance to voluntary actions—both are false according to Ahl al-Haqq.

Key Terms
حياة القبر
استبعاد
إمكان
خوارق العادات
الصادق
الصالحية
الكرامية
ابن الراوندي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that natural impossibility (like body decay) proves life in the grave is impossible.
Correction: Islamic theology holds that God is omnipotent and can create life even in a decayed body; prophetic testimony confirms this, so it is not irrational to believe.

Checkpoint
What is the main rational objection to life in the grave presented in the passage?
A) The body decomposes and cannot support life.
B) The spirit is immaterial and cannot feel pain.
C) The Quran denies any life after death.
D) The dead are not conscious of time.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of life in the grave is rationally defensible through divine omnipotence and prophetic testimony, with the spirit as the locus of experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious teaching that seems to contradict your empirical experience of the world, do you instinctively trust your senses over revelation? What would it take for you to submit to the Prophet's report even when your reason recoils?
?
The author says God can hide these phenomena 'for a wisdom we have no knowledge of.' Is there an area of your faith where you demand full intellectual clarity before believing? How does this lesson challenge your need for complete comprehension?
?
If you truly believed that your deeds are being tested and recorded even now in a hidden dimension (the grave), would it change how you live this very moment? What specific sin or neglect would you repent from if you could feel the reality of the grave's accountability?

The Type of Life in the Grave and the Soul's Return
Sanusi concludes the discussion on life in the grave, affirming a partial life without full capacities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dead in the grave possess a partial life sufficient for pleasure and pain, but without the complete life requiring power and voluntary actions, and the scholarly debate on whether the soul is returned.

Core Idea
The dead feel pleasure and pain, but their life is incomplete like a coma.

This concluding passage from Imam al-Sanusi addresses a key issue in Islamic eschatology: the nature of life in the grave. The people of truth agree that God grants the dead a type of life sufficient for experiencing reward or punishment, but they debated whether the soul is returned. This lesson explains the minimal life they possess and the objections raised.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between 'type of life' and 'complete life'.
2
Identify the three key claims
The passage makes three main points: (1) The dead have a life sufficient for sensation, (2) The return of the soul is debated, (3) The impossibility of life without soul is denied for this partial life.
These correspond to the logical flow of the conclusion.
3
Understand the analogy with apoplexy (coma)
Sanusi compares the dead to someone struck by a seizure—alive but without normal function. This helps explain why the life is not obvious.
This analogy is crucial for grasping the subtlety.
4
Consider the problem with Munkar and Nakir
The passage ends by noting a difficulty: the dead must answer the angels. How is this possible if they lack full life? Reflect on how the partial life might suffice for this interaction.
This shows the author's attention to potential objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة - (خاتمة) قد ثبتت بالضرورة من أن للميت في القبر نوع حياة قدر ما يتألم ويتلذذ، ولكن في إعادة الروح إليه تردد وامتناع الحياة بدون الروح ممنوع . اتفق أهل الحق على أن الله يعيد إلى الميت في القبر نوع حياة قدر ما يتالم ويتلذذ ويشهد بذلك الكتاب والأخبار والآثار، لكن توقفوا في أنه هل يعاد الروح إليه أم لا؟ وما يتوهم من امتناع الحياة بدون الروح ممنوع . وإنما ذلك في الحياة الكاملة التي يكون معها القدرة والأفعال الاختيارية. وقد اتفقوا على أن الله تعالى لم يخلق في الميت القدرة والأفعال الاختيارية . فلهذا لا يعرف حياته كمن أصابته سكتة . ويشكل هذا بجوابه لمنكر ونكير على ما ورد في الحديث .
He said: Conclusion. (Conclusion) It has been established necessarily that the dead has in the grave a type of life sufficient to suffer pain and experience pleasure, but there is hesitation concerning the return of the soul to him, and the impossibility of life without the soul is denied. The people of truth agreed that God returns to the dead in the grave a type of life sufficient to suffer pain and experience pleasure, and the Book, reports, and traditions testify to that, but they hesitated as to whether the soul is returned to him or not. And what is imagined of the impossibility of life without the soul is denied. That is only concerning the complete life with which power and voluntary actions exist. And they agreed that God the Exalted did not create in the dead power and voluntary actions. For this reason, his life is not known, like one struck by apoplexy. And this is problematic with his answering Munkar and Nakir as reported in the hadith.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a concluding statement (khatima) about the nature of life in the grave. It affirms that it is known by necessity that the dead possesses a type of life (naw' hayah) sufficient for experiencing pain and pleasure. However, there is disagreement (taraddud) as to whether the soul (al-ruh) is returned to the body for this life. The author notes that Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) agree that God gives the dead this type of life, as evidenced by the Quran, hadith, and reports of the Salaf, but they differed on the return of the soul. The author then denies the claim that life is impossible without the soul (imtina' al-hayah bila al-ruh), clarifying that this impossibility applies only to complete life (al-hayah al-kamila) which includes power (qudra) and voluntary actions (af'al ikhtiyariyya). Since God does not create these faculties in the dead, the dead's life is unrecognizable, like a person in a coma (sakta). The passage ends by noting a difficulty: the dead must answer the angels Munkar and Nakir, which requires some form of life and understanding.

Key Terms
حياة القبر
روح
سكتة
منكر ونكير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all life requires a soul, so the dead cannot have any life without the soul's return.
Correction: The passage explains that life without a soul is possible for a partial, non-volitional life; the impossibility only applies to complete life with power and voluntary actions.

Checkpoint
Why is the life of the dead in the grave not recognized like normal life?
A) Because the soul is not present.
B) Because God does not create in them power and voluntary actions.
C) Because they are unconscious.
D) Because they are in a deep sleep.

Key Takeaway
The dead in the grave experience a real but partial life enabling sensation, without the complete life that includes volition, thus resolving the challenge of their answering the angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The dead in the grave experience a real, though partial, life: they feel pleasure from good deeds and pain from sins. If you could hear the verdict of your own grave tonight, what would it be? Are you living in a way that those sensations would bring you comfort or torment?
?
The scholars hesitated about the soul's return, yet they affirmed the dead's ability to answer Munkar and Nakir. This mystery means your accountability begins before the Resurrection. What actions have you done today that you will be asked about in that confined darkness?
?
The life in the grave is like a coma: you are aware but powerless. If you were struck unconscious right now, what would your last conscious deed be? Is it enough to make that intermediate state bearable? Will you change one thing today so that your grave becomes a garden rather than a pit of fire?

Affirming Eschatological Realities
Author affirms eschatological realities as obligatory belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all eschatological matters reported in the Quran and Sunnah are possible and must be affirmed because they come from the truthful Prophet.

Core Idea
Eschatological realities are possible and obligatory to affirm.

In this passage, the author begins by establishing the foundational principle that everything reported about the afterlife—the Reckoning, Sirat, Mizan, Hawd, and details of Paradise and Hell—are matters that are possible in themselves. Because they are reported by the truthful Prophet, it becomes obligatory to believe in them without metaphorical reinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmation statement
Read the Arabic passage that lists the eschatological items and the ruling that they must be affirmed.
2
Identify the items listed
Notice the specific items: Reckoning and its terrors, Sirat, Mizan, Hawd, and details of Paradise and Hell.
3
Understand the logical connection
The author argues that these are possible (mumkin) matters and the Prophet (truthful) reported them, so affirming them is obligatory (wajib).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث السابع - المبحث السابع - سائر ما ورد في الكتاب والسنة من المحاسبة وأهوالها، والصراط ، والميزان ، والحوض، وتفاصيل أحوال الجنة والنار أمور ممكنة أخبر بها الصادق، فوجب التصديق وأنكر بعض المعتزلة الصراط والميزان على ما وصفا لأن ما هو أدق من الشعر، وأحد من السيف ، والعبور عليه لو أمكن فعذاب، والأعمال أعراض لا يعقل وزنها. فالصراط طريق الجنة وطريق النار، أو الأدلة الواضحة، أو العبادات والشريعة والميزان العدل الثابت في كل شيء، أو الإدراك كالحواس للمحسوسات، والعلم للمعقولات . والجواب - أن الله يسهل الطريق حتى يمر البعض كالبرق الخاطف وهكذا .
He said: The Seventh Section. The Seventh Section - All that is reported in the Book and the Sunnah of the reckoning and its terrors, the Sirat, the Mizan, the Hawd, and details of the states of Paradise and Hell are possible matters reported by the truthful one, so affirmation is obligatory. And some of the Mu'tazila denied the Sirat and the Mizan as they are described, because that which is finer than hair and sharper than a sword, crossing it, if possible, would be torment, and deeds are accidents which cannot be weighed. So the Sirat is the path to Paradise and the path to Hell, or clear proofs, or acts of worship and the Shari'a, and the Mizan is justice established in everything, or perception like the senses for sensible things and knowledge for intelligibles. And the answer: That God makes the path easy so that some pass like a flash of lightning and so on.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal affirmation of eschatological realities reported in the Quran and Hadith: the Reckoning (muhasaba) with its terrors, the Bridge (Sirat), the Scales (Mizan), the Heavenly Pool (Hawd), and details of Paradise and Hell. The author states these are possible (mumkin) matters informed by the truthful Prophet, so they must be affirmed (wajib al-tasdiq). The author then mentions the objection of some Mu'tazila who denied the literal Sirat and Mizan. Their reasoning: the Sirat is described as finer than hair and sharper than a sword; crossing it would be torture, not a test. The Mizan requires weighing deeds, but deeds are accidents (a'rad) which cannot be weighed. Therefore, they interpret these as metaphors: the Sirat is the clear path of guidance or the religion, and the Mizan is divine justice. The author's response is simple and relies on divine omnipotence: God makes the crossing easy for some believers, so they pass like a flash of lightning. The answer reaffirms the literal reality without further elaboration.

Key Terms
صراط
ميزان
حوض
محاسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must eschatological matters be affirmed according to the author?
A) Because they are rationally necessary.
B) Because they are reported by the truthful Prophet.
C) Because they are mentioned in the Torah.
D) Because they are agreed upon by all Muslims.

Key Takeaway
All eschatological details reported in revelation must be affirmed as real because they are possible and come from the truthful Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed the Reckoning and its terrors will happen to you personally, what is one thing in your daily life that would change immediately?
?
Your affirmation of the Sirat, Mizan, and Hawd—is it merely intellectual assent, or does it shape how you treat others today?
?
When someone dismisses these eschatological details as symbolic, does your conviction waver? Would you be willing to defend their literal reality based solely on the truthfulness of the one who reported them?

Refuting Mu'tazilite Denials of Sirat and Mizan
Author refutes Mu'tazilite metaphorical reinterpretations of Sirat and Mizan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objections to the literal Sirat and Mizan and the Ash'ari response based on divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
God's power makes the impossible possible, including the crossing of Sirat.

After affirming the necessity of belief in eschatological matters, the author addresses the objections of the Mu'tazila who denied the literal reality of the Bridge (Sirat) and Scales (Mizan). They argued that these concepts conflict with reason: the Sirat would be torturous and deeds cannot be weighed. The author responds simply, invoking God's ability to make the crossing easy for the believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite objection
Read the part where some Mu'tazila deny the literal Sirat and Mizan based on rational difficulties: the Sirat is too fine and sharp, and deeds are accidents.
2
Understand their metaphorical interpretations
The Mu'tazila offer alternative interpretations: Sirat as the path to Paradise/Hell or clear proofs, Mizan as justice or perception.
3
Study the author's answer
The author replies that God makes the path easy, allowing some to cross like lightning, reaffirming the literal reality.

Source Text

Misconception: The Sirat and Mizan are only metaphors for moral concepts or divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
