The Balance (Mizan): Interpretations and Objections
Discussion of the Balance (Mizan) and its interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse scholarly interpretations of the Balance (al-Mizan) and how they address the objection that deeds are non-physical accidents.

Core Idea
The Balance symbolizes divine justice, perception, or weighed scrolls, not a physical scale.

How can deeds be weighed if they are intangible and perishable? This lesson explores the answers given by scholars, including justice, perception, and transformation of deeds into bodies. The plural form 'موازين' also receives explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on the Balance
Read the full Arabic text about the Balance (from 'ذهاباً' to 'عظمة المقام'). Focus on the initial objection and the series of interpretations.
The passage is dense with different views; try to track each one.
2
Identify the objection and its resolution
Notice that the passage begins with an objection: deeds are accidents that cannot be weighed. The first resolution is that 'Balance' means justice.
3
List the interpretations presented
Enumerate the interpretations: justice, perception, weighing scrolls, deeds transformed into bodies, and each person having their own balance.
The plural form is explained as either magnification or multiplicity of balances.
4
Understand the purpose of these interpretations
Reflect on why scholars offered these metaphorical explanations—to reconcile scriptural descriptions with rational principles.
This shows the method of ta'wil (interpretation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهاباً إلى أن الأعمال أعراض لا يمكن وزنها ، فكيف إذا زالت وتلاشت؟ بل المراد به العدل الثابت في كل شيء، ولذا ذكره بلفظ الجمع، وإلا فالميزان المشهور واحد. وقيل: هو الإدراك . فميزان الألوان البصر، والأصوات السمع والطعوم الذوق، وكذا سائر الحواس. وميزان المعقولات العلم والعقل . وأجيب بأنه يوزن صحائف الأعمال. وقيل: بل تجعل الحسنات أجسامـاً نورانية، والسيئات أجساماً ظلمانية، وأما لفظ الجمع فللاستعظام، وقيل: لكل مكلف ميزان . وإنما الميزان الكبير واحد إظهاراً لجلالة الأمر وعظمة المقام، ومنها الحوض . قال تعالى : إنا أعطيناك الكوثر (١) . وفي الحديث: «حوضي مسيرة شهر وزواياه سواء، ماؤه أبيض من اللبن، وريحه أطيب من المسك، وكيزانه أكثر من نجوم السماء، من شرب منها فلا يظمأ أبداً». وقال الصحابة له عليه (السلام أين نطلبك يوم الحشر؟ فقال: على الصراط . فإن لم تجدوا فعلى الميزان فإن لم تجدوا فعلى الحوض.
Proceeding to the idea that deeds are accidents that cannot be weighed, so how [can they be weighed] when they have passed and perished? Rather, what is intended by it [the Balance] is the justice established in everything, and therefore it was mentioned in the plural form; otherwise the well-known Balance is one. And it was said: it is perception. So the balance of colors is sight, of sounds is hearing, of tastes is taste, and likewise the other senses. And the balance of intelligibles is knowledge and intellect. And it was answered that the scrolls of deeds are weighed. And it was said: rather, good deeds are made luminous bodies, and evil deeds are made dark bodies. As for the plural word, it is for magnification. And it was said: for every morally responsible person there is a balance. But the great Balance is one, as a display of the majesty of the matter and the greatness of the station. And among them [the matters of the Hereafter] is the Basin. He, exalted, said: 'Indeed, We have given you al-Kawthar' (Quran 108:1). And in the hadith: 'My Basin is a month's journey, its corners are equal; its water is whiter than milk, its scent better than musk, its jugs more numerous than the stars of the sky; whoever drinks from it will never thirst again.' And the Companions said to him, peace be upon him: 'Where shall we seek you on the Day of Gathering?' He said: 'On the Path (al-Sirat). If you do not find me, then at the Balance. If you do not find me, then at the Basin.'
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage discusses the interpretation of the Balance (al-Mizan) and the Basin (al-Hawd) in Islamic eschatology. It begins by addressing a potential objection: if deeds are accidents that perish, how can they be weighed? The answer is that by 'Balance' is meant justice itself, and the plural form (ميزان) is used to indicate multiplicity of justice, though the famous Balance is one. Other interpretations are presented: some say Balance means perception—each sense has its own balance; others say the scrolls of deeds are weighed; still others say deeds are transformed into luminous or dark bodies. The plural is said to be for magnification, or that each individual has a balance, but the great Balance is one to show the majesty of the event. Then the passage turns to the Basin (al-Hawd), citing the verse on al-Kawthar and a hadith describing the Basin's vastness, whiteness, and fragrance. Finally, a tradition is narrated where the Companions ask where to find the Prophet on the Day of Gathering, and he replies they will find him on the Path (al-Sirat), then at the Balance, then at the Basin. This passage presents multiple scholarly opinions on these matters without settling on one, reflecting interpretive diversity.

Key Terms
الميزان
موازين
العدل
الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Balance (Mizan) is a physical scale like those used in markets.
Correction: The passage explicitly rejects physical weighing of deeds and offers metaphorical interpretations such as justice, perception, or weighing of scrolls.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an interpretation of the Balance?
A) Justice
B) Perception
C) Physical scales weighing bodies
D) Weighing of scrolls

Key Takeaway
The Balance (Mizan) is interpreted metaphorically as justice, perception, or weighing of deeds, reflecting the diversity of Islamic theological thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every small action you do will be weighed with perfect justice? How would your day change if you lived with that awareness?
?
Among the interpretations of the Balance, which one makes you take your actions more seriously? Why?
?
If your deeds were to be transformed into luminous or dark bodies tomorrow, which color would dominate? And what are you going to do about it today?

The Basin (Hawd) of the Prophet
Description of the Basin (Hawd) and where to find the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the description of the Prophet's Basin (al-Hawd) and its significance, as well as where to find the Prophet on the Day of Gathering.

Core Idea
The Basin is a vast, blessed water source for the Prophet's followers, and he can be found on the Path, Balance, or Basin.

The Basin (al-Hawd) is a promised blessing for the Prophet Muhammad and his community. This lesson examines the Quranic verse about al-Kawthar, the hadith describing the Basin, and a tradition indicating where the Prophet will be on the Day of Judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Basin
Read from 'ومنها الحوض' to the end. Note the verse, the hadith, and the Companions' question.

The Basin is a gift from Allah to the Prophet.
3
Analyze the hadith description
The hadith describes the Basin's vastness (a month's journey), whiteness, fragrance, and numerous jugs. Emphasize the promise that drinkers will never thirst again.
4
Identify where to find the Prophet
The Companions ask where to seek the Prophet on the Day of Gathering. He replies: first on the Path (al-Sirat), then at the Balance, then at the Basin.
This shows the order of events on that Day.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith in the passage, what is the size of the Prophet's Basin?
A) A day's journey
B) A month's journey
C) A year's journey
D) The size of a large lake

Key Takeaway
The Basin (Hawd) is a vast, blessed gift for the Prophet's followers, and the Prophet will be found on the Path, Balance, or Basin on the Day of Gathering.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet described his Basin with such vividness: its water whiter than milk, its scent better than musk. Do you truly long to drink from it? What in your life right now distracts you from this eternal thirst?
?
The Companions asked where to find the Prophet on the Day of Gathering. He gave them three places: the Path, the Balance, the Basin. Where in your daily life are you looking for the Prophet's guidance? Are you seeking him in the right places now, or only hoping to find him then?
?
Whoever drinks from the Basin will never thirst again. What are you drinking from today that leaves you still thirsty? What would it mean for you to prepare to drink from that eternal cup?

The Philosophers' Thesis: Paradise and Hell as Spiritual Representations
The philosophers' thesis that afterlife rewards are spiritual representations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' thesis that descriptions of Paradise and Hell are symbolic representations of the spiritual states of souls, and their assertion that only compound ignorance leads to everlasting misery.

Core Idea
Paradise and Hell are symbols of the soul's perfection or deficiency.

This passage presents the view of the 'researchers among the philosophers' (al-muḥaqqiqūn min al-ḥukamā') on the afterlife. They argue that the detailed descriptions in revelation are not literal but symbolic of spiritual realities. Before we examine their classification, we must grasp their core claim that souls experience reward and punishment through their own perfections and deficiencies, and that only deeply rooted ignorance causes eternal suffering.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the philosophical tone and the claim that descriptions are 'tamthīl wa taṣwīr' (representation and depiction).
2
Identify the main thesis
Look for the claim that Paradise and Hell are not literal places but symbolic of the soul's states of happiness (saʿādah) and misery (shaqāwah). Understand that reward is enjoyment of perfections, punishment is suffering from deficiencies.
The soul is imperishable.
3
Note the exception for everlasting misery
The passage states that misery is not eternal for all; only compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) that is firmly rooted leads to perpetual punishment. This is a key nuance.
Compound ignorance is ignorance that is certain and ingrained.
4
Reflect on why souls are unaware in this world
Ask yourself why, according to the philosophers, souls do not perceive their true state now. The answer lies in attachments and obstacles that vanish at death (al-mufāraqah).
This explains the need for afterlife revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثامن - · ذهب المحققون من الحكماء إلى أن ما ورد في الشرع من تفاصيل أحوال الجنة والنار، والثواب والعقاب تمثيل وتصوير لمراتب النفوس وأحوالها في السعادة والشقاوة ولذاتها وآلامها فإنها لا تفنى بل تبقى ملتذة بكمالاتها (٢) فذلك ثوابها وجنانها، أو متألمه بنقصانها ، فذلك عقابها ونيرانها، وإنما لم تتنبه لذلك في هذا العالم لما بها من العلائق والعوائق الزائلة بالمفارقة وليست شقاوتها سرمدية (۳) البتة، بل قد تتدرج من درجات الشقاوة إلى درجات السعادة وإنما الشقاوة السرمدية هي الجهل المركب الراسخ، والشرارة المضادة للملكة الفاضلة وتفصيل ذلك أن فوات كمال النفس يكون إما لأمر عدمي كنقصان الغريزة، أو وجودي راسخ، أو غير راسخ . كل من بحسب القوة النظرية أو العملية . فالذي بحسب نقصان الغريزة لا عذاب عليه . والذي بحسب مضاد راسخ في القوة النظرية كالجهل المركب، فعذابه دائم . والثلاثة الباقية تزول بعد عذاب مختلف في الكيف (۱) والكم (۲) بحسب اختلاف الهيئات المضادة في شدة الرداءة وضعفها وفي سرعة الزوال وبطئه، وإن كانت النفس خالية عن الكمال والشوق إليه وعما يضاده فهي في سعة من رحمة الله تعالى.
The eighth investigation. The researchers among the philosophers hold that what has come in the Law concerning the details of the states of Paradise and Hell, and reward and punishment, is a representation and depiction of the ranks of souls and their states in happiness and misery, and their pleasures and pains. For they [the souls] do not perish but remain enjoying their perfections—that is their reward and their gardens—or suffering from their deficiencies—that is their punishment and their fires. They were not aware of this in this world because of the attachments and obstacles that vanish at separation. And their misery is not everlasting at all; rather, they may gradually move from levels of misery to levels of happiness. Only compound ignorance that is firmly rooted, and the disposition opposed to the virtuous character, are everlasting misery. The detail of that: the loss of the soul's perfection is either due to a privative matter like deficiency of innate disposition, or an existent matter that is firmly rooted or not firmly rooted. Each is according to the theoretical or practical faculty. That which is due to deficiency of innate disposition—there is no punishment upon it. That which is due to a firmly rooted opposite in the theoretical faculty, like compound ignorance—its punishment is perpetual. The remaining three vanish after a punishment differing in quality and quantity according to the difference of the opposing states in intensity and weakness of badness, and in speed and slowness of vanishing. And if the soul is devoid of perfection, yearning for it, and what opposes it, then it is in an expanse of Allah's mercy.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth investigation (al-mabḥath al-thāmin) and states the view of the 'researchers among the philosophers' (al-muḥaqqiqūn min al-ḥukamā') regarding Paradise, Hell, reward, and punishment. They argue that the detailed descriptions in revelation are symbolic representations (tamthīl wa taṣwīr) of the spiritual states of souls—their ranks in happiness (saʿādah) and misery (shaqāwah), and their pleasures and pains. Souls are imperishable and experience either enjoyment of their perfections (that is their reward and paradise) or suffering from their deficiencies (that is their punishment and hellfire). The reason souls do not perceive this in the present world is due to attachments and obstacles that vanish at death (al-mufāraqah). Importantly, misery is not eternal for all; souls can gradually progress from misery to happiness. Everlasting misery is only for compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) that is deeply rooted, and the disposition opposed to virtue. The passage then provides a detailed classification: loss of perfection (fawāt kamāl al-nafs) can be due to a privative cause (ʿadamī) like deficiency of innate disposition (nuqṣān al-gharīzah), or an existential cause (wujūdī) that is either firmly rooted (rāsikh) or not firmly rooted (ghayr rāsikh). These apply to both the theoretical and practical faculties. The one due to deficiency of innate disposition incurs no punishment. The one due to a firmly rooted opposite in the theoretical faculty (like compound ignorance) has perpetual punishment. The remaining three categories (existential not firmly rooted, both theoretical and practical, plus perhaps the privative in practical?) are said to vanish after a punishment that varies in quality (kayf) and quantity (kamm) according to the intensity and speed of the opposing states. If a soul is empty of perfection, desire for it, and its opposite, it is in an expanse of Allah's mercy. This classification is a nuanced philosophical account of afterlife states.

Key Terms
الحكماء
تمثيل
السعادة
الشقاوة
الجنة
النار
الجهل المركب
الملكة الفاضلة
النفس
الكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers completely deny any physical reality to Paradise and Hell.
Correction: They affirm spiritual realities but consider the descriptions in revelation as symbolic representations of those spiritual states, not as literal physical places. They do not deny afterlife but reinterpret it.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, what is the reason souls are not aware of their true state in this world?
A) They are too busy with worldly affairs
B) Due to attachments and obstacles that vanish at death
C) They lack intellectual capacity
D) They are deceived by Satan

Key Takeaway
The philosophers reinterpret Paradise and Hell as spiritual states of the soul's perfection or deficiency, with everlasting misery only for compound ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you actively seeking to purify your soul from deficiencies, or are you content with ignoring them, knowing that they will cause you pain after death?
?
The philosophers teach that compound ignorance—being ignorant of your own ignorance—leads to eternal misery. In what area of your faith or character are you most resistant to admitting your ignorance?
?
If the true 'reward' is the perfection of your soul, what one habit or trait are you cultivating today that will bring you closer to that perfection, and what are you neglecting?

The Classification of Souls: Types of Perfection Loss and Their Outcomes
Detailed classification of soul's perfection loss and punishment types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed classification of souls based on the loss of perfection and the corresponding afterlife punishments as presented by the philosophers.

Core Idea
Souls are punished according to the type and root of their deficiency.

In this passage, the philosophers provide a detailed classification of how souls lose perfection and the resulting afterlife states. They distinguish between privative and existential causes, firmly rooted and non-rooted, and apply this to theoretical and practical faculties. This taxonomy determines whether a soul suffers no punishment, perpetual punishment, or temporary punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This passage is more technical and enumerative. Note the structure: it starts with 'The detail of that' (Wa tafṣīlu dhālika).
2
Identify the two main categories
Find the two causes of loss of perfection: privative (ʿadamī, deficiency of innate disposition) and existential (wujūdī, an actual opposing state).
Privative means absence; existential means presence of a defect.
3
Understand the subcategories under existential
Existential causes can be firmly rooted (rāsikh) or not firmly rooted (ghayr rāsikh). Each applies to theoretical and practical faculties. Note that only the firmly rooted opposite in the theoretical faculty (compound ignorance) leads to perpetual punishment.
4
Learn the outcomes for each category
The text lists: (1) privative: no punishment; (2) existential firmly rooted in theoretical: perpetual punishment; (3) the remaining three (existential non-rooted theoretical, existential firmly rooted practical, existential non-rooted practical): temporary punishment varying in quality and quantity. Additionally, a soul devoid of all (perfection, desire, and opposite) is in mercy.
The three temporary categories vanish after punishment.

The Soul After Death: Reincarnation vs. Celestial Attachment
Presentation of the soul's post-mortem options: reincarnation or attachment to a celestial body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that the soul must attach to a body after death, and the distinction between reincarnation and attachment to a celestial or aerial body.

Core Idea
The soul cannot remain idle; it must either reincarnate or attach to a celestial body for imagination and pleasure.

In this passage, the author presents the view of some philosophers who reject the idea that the soul becomes inactive after death. They argue instead that it must connect to another body—either through reincarnation (tanāsukh) or by using a celestial or aerial body as an instrument for imaginative pleasure. This sets the stage for a broader discussion of the afterlife in philosophical thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two options
The passage presents two possible ways the soul can attach to another body. Find them.
Reincarnation vs. using a body for imagination.
2
Understand the nature of the second body
Notice that the second body is not a normal body but a celestial or aerial body, not a mixture (mizāj) that would generate a new soul.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why philosophers might prefer this view over complete idleness of the soul.
This view maintains the soul's activity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يجوز بعضهم كونها معطلة عن الإدراك، فزعم أنها لا بد أن تتعلق بجسم آخر ، على أن تكون نفساً له تدبره. وهذا هو هو التناسخ، أو على أن تستعمله لإمكان التخيل، فتتخيل الصور التي كانت عندها، وتلتذ بذلك، ولا يكون أن تستعمله لإمكان التخيل، فتتخيل الصور التي كانت عندها، وتلتذ بذلك، ولا يكون ذلك الجسم مزاجاً ليقتضي فيضان نفس، بل يكون جرماً سماوياً، أو هوائياً (۳) أو نحو ذلك . ولم يستبعد بعضهم المعاد الجسماني، لأن للتبشير والإنذار نفعاً ظاهراً في أمر النظام والإيفاء بذلك بثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي ازدياد للنفع بالقياس إلى الأكثرين، وإن كان ضرراً للمعذب). في تقرير مذهب الحكماء في الجنة والنار والثواب والعقاب، أما القائلون بعالم المثل، فيقولون بالجنة والنار، وسائر ما ورد به الشرع من التفاصيل، لكن في عالم المثل لا من جنس المحسوسات المحضة على ما يقول به الإسلاميون. وأما الأكثرون فيجعلون ذلك من قبيل اللذات والآلام العقلية، وذلك أن النفوس البشرية سواء جعلت أزلية كما هو رأي أفلاطون (٤) ، أو لا كما هو رأي ارسطو(ه) ،
And some of them did not allow that [the soul] be idle from perception, so they claimed that it must attach to another body, either to be a soul that manages it—and this is exactly reincarnation (al-tanāsukh)—or to use it for the possibility of imagination, so it imagines the forms that were with it and takes pleasure in that, and it cannot use it for the possibility of imagination, so it imagines the forms that were with it and takes pleasure in that, and that body is not a temperament requiring the emanation of a soul, but rather is a celestial or aerial body or the like. And some of them did not deem impossible bodily resurrection (al-maʿād al-jismānī), because giving glad tidings and warning have a manifest benefit in the matter of order, and fulfilling that by rewarding the obedient and punishing the disobedient increases benefit in comparison to the majority, even if it is harmful to the punished. In the exposition of the doctrine of the philosophers concerning Paradise and Hell and reward and punishment: As for those who affirm the world of Forms (ʿālam al-mithāl), they affirm Paradise and Hell and all the details brought by the Law, but in the world of Forms, not of the kind of pure sensibles, as the Muslims say. As for the majority, they make that of the category of intellectual pleasures and pains, because human souls—whether they are posited as eternal, as is the opinion of Plato, or not, as is the opinion of Aristotle—
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of philosophers' views on the afterlife. It begins by addressing the problem of the soul after death: some philosophers reject that the soul remains inactive without perception, and thus claim it must attach to another body. This can be either reincarnation (al-tanāsukh)—where the soul becomes the animating principle of a new body—or it may use a body (celestial or aerial) as an instrument for imagination, thereby imagining forms from its previous life and taking pleasure in them. This body is not a mixture (mizāj) that requires an emanation of a new soul, but serves as a vehicle. Next, the passage notes that some philosophers do not reject bodily resurrection (al-maʿād al-jismānī) because the promise of reward and threat of punishment have practical benefits for social order, even if it causes pain to the punished. Finally, the passage begins an exposition (taqrīr) of the philosophers' doctrine: those who affirm the 'world of Forms' (ʿālam al-mithāl) affirm Paradise and Hell as real but in that world, not in the physical sense as Muslims hold. The majority (al-aktharūn) consider the afterlife pleasures and pains as intellectual (ʿaqliyyah), and the text begins to discuss the pre-eternity of souls according to Plato and Aristotle, though the passage cuts off. This section shows the variety of philosophical opinions on the nature of the afterlife and the soul's post-mortem existence.

The Pragmatic Justification for Bodily Resurrection
Justification of bodily resurrection through its social utility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that bodily resurrection is not rejected by some philosophers because of its social utility in promoting order through reward and punishment.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is deemed possible because of its practical benefit for social order.

Continuing the discussion, the author notes that some philosophers do not dismiss bodily resurrection (al-maʿād al-jismānī) outright. They recognize that the promise of reward and threat of punishment serve a clear purpose in maintaining social order and benefiting the majority, even if it causes suffering to the punished. This pragmatic view sets the stage for the philosophers' diverse doctrines on Paradise and Hell.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on bodily resurrection
Notice the reason given: the benefit of glad tidings and warning for the system.
The benefit increases for the majority.
2
Understand the trade-off
The passage acknowledges that although it may harm the punished, the overall benefit to society is greater.
3
Connect to the next topic
This leads into the exposition of philosophers' views on the afterlife.
The passage ends with a new section on the world of Forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يجوز بعضهم كونها معطلة عن الإدراك، فزعم أنها لا بد أن تتعلق بجسم آخر ، على أن تكون نفساً له تدبره. وهذا هو هو التناسخ، أو على أن تستعمله لإمكان التخيل، فتتخيل الصور التي كانت عندها، وتلتذ بذلك، ولا يكون أن تستعمله لإمكان التخيل، فتتخيل الصور التي كانت عندها، وتلتذ بذلك، ولا يكون ذلك الجسم مزاجاً ليقتضي فيضان نفس، بل يكون جرماً سماوياً، أو هوائياً (۳) أو نحو ذلك . ولم يستبعد بعضهم المعاد الجسماني، لأن للتبشير والإنذار نفعاً ظاهراً في أمر النظام والإيفاء بذلك بثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي ازدياد للنفع بالقياس إلى الأكثرين، وإن كان ضرراً للمعذب). في تقرير مذهب الحكماء في الجنة والنار والثواب والعقاب، أما القائلون بعالم المثل، فيقولون بالجنة والنار، وسائر ما ورد به الشرع من التفاصيل، لكن في عالم المثل لا من جنس المحسوسات المحضة على ما يقول به الإسلاميون. وأما الأكثرون فيجعلون ذلك من قبيل اللذات والآلام العقلية، وذلك أن النفوس البشرية سواء جعلت أزلية كما هو رأي أفلاطون (٤) ، أو لا كما هو رأي ارسطو(ه) ،
And some of them did not allow that [the soul] be idle from perception, so they claimed that it must attach to another body, either to be a soul that manages it—and this is exactly reincarnation (al-tanāsukh)—or to use it for the possibility of imagination, so it imagines the forms that were with it and takes pleasure in that, and it cannot use it for the possibility of imagination, so it imagines the forms that were with it and takes pleasure in that, and that body is not a temperament requiring the emanation of a soul, but rather is a celestial or aerial body or the like. And some of them did not deem impossible bodily resurrection (al-maʿād al-jismānī), because giving glad tidings and warning have a manifest benefit in the matter of order, and fulfilling that by rewarding the obedient and punishing the disobedient increases benefit in comparison to the majority, even if it is harmful to the punished. In the exposition of the doctrine of the philosophers concerning Paradise and Hell and reward and punishment: As for those who affirm the world of Forms (ʿālam al-mithāl), they affirm Paradise and Hell and all the details brought by the Law, but in the world of Forms, not of the kind of pure sensibles, as the Muslims say. As for the majority, they make that of the category of intellectual pleasures and pains, because human souls—whether they are posited as eternal, as is the opinion of Plato, or not, as is the opinion of Aristotle—
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of philosophers' views on the afterlife. It begins by addressing the problem of the soul after death: some philosophers reject that the soul remains inactive without perception, and thus claim it must attach to another body. This can be either reincarnation (al-tanāsukh)—where the soul becomes the animating principle of a new body—or it may use a body (celestial or aerial) as an instrument for imagination, thereby imagining forms from its previous life and taking pleasure in them. This body is not a mixture (mizāj) that requires an emanation of a new soul, but serves as a vehicle. Next, the passage notes that some philosophers do not reject bodily resurrection (al-maʿād al-jismānī) because the promise of reward and threat of punishment have practical benefits for social order, even if it causes pain to the punished. Finally, the passage begins an exposition (taqrīr) of the philosophers' doctrine: those who affirm the 'world of Forms' (ʿālam al-mithāl) affirm Paradise and Hell as real but in that world, not in the physical sense as Muslims hold. The majority (al-aktharūn) consider the afterlife pleasures and pains as intellectual (ʿaqliyyah), and the text begins to discuss the pre-eternity of souls according to Plato and Aristotle, though the passage cuts off. This section shows the variety of philosophical opinions on the nature of the afterlife and the soul's post-mortem existence.

Correction: Some philosophers affirm bodily resurrection due to its practical benefits.

Checkpoint
Why do some philosophers not reject bodily resurrection according to the passage?
A) It is logically necessary
B) It has manifest benefit for order
C) It is proven by scripture
D) It is the only way to experience pleasure

Key Takeaway
Some philosophers accept bodily resurrection based on its utility for social order, not on metaphysical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the reward of Paradise and the punishment of Hell are real events awaiting you after death, or have you reduced them to mere motivators for social behavior? What would change in your daily life if you were absolutely certain of a bodily resurrection tomorrow?
?
When you see someone suffering as a result of their choices—whether in this life or the next—do you feel only pity, or can you accept that their harm might be part of a greater good for the majority? How does that acceptance sit with your personal sense of justice?
?
The passage implies that even a purely worldly argument can make bodily resurrection plausible. Have you ever relied on worldly reasoning to justify your faith, rather than on the revealed truth itself? What does that say about the strength of your conviction?

Philosophers' Doctrines on the Afterlife: World of Forms vs. Intellectual Pleasures
Exposition of philosophical views on Paradise, Hell, and the nature of afterlife rewards.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main philosophical interpretations of Paradise and Hell: those who place them in the world of Forms, and those who consider them as intellectual pleasures and pains.

Core Idea
For philosophers, the afterlife is either in the world of Forms or experienced as intellectual pleasures.

In this passage, the author begins to expound the philosophers' doctrine (taqrīr) regarding Paradise and Hell. He distinguishes two groups: those who affirm the world of Forms (ʿālam al-mithāl) and believe that Paradise and Hell exist there in a spiritual sense, not as physical realities; and the majority who regard the pleasures and pains as intellectual (ʿaqliyyah), connected to the nature of human souls. The passage starts to discuss the pre-eternity of souls but is cut off.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
The passage mentions 'those who affirm the world of Forms' and 'the majority.' Find what each says.
2
Understand the world of Forms view
They affirm Paradise and Hell as per the Law but in the world of Forms, not as pure sensibles.
This is similar to what Muslims say.
3
Understand the intellectual pleasures view
The majority consider the pleasures and pains as intellectual, based on the nature of human souls.
The passage begins a discussion on the eternity of souls but ends.

Three Types of Soul Deficiencies
Sanusi classifies soul deficiencies and their punishments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of soul deficiencies—non-existential, existential firm, and existential non-firm—and how they determine the type and duration of punishment in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The soul's deficiency type shapes its afterlife fate: firm theoretical ignorance incurs eternal torment.

Sanusi presents a detailed taxonomy of spiritual defects, linking each to a specific consequence in the next life. Before we examine the afterlife, we must grasp how deficiencies are classified. This passage lays the foundation for understanding divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main categories
Read the passage and find the three types: non-existential (ʿadamī), existential firm (wujūdī rāsikh), and existential non-firm (ghayr rāsikh). Note the example given for non-existential: deficiency of instinct.
2
Connect each category to the theoretical or practical faculty
Observe that each deficiency can apply to either the theoretical or practical faculty. The author gives the example of compound ignorance as a firm opposite in the theoretical faculty.
3
Understand the punishment outcomes
Notice that deficiency of instinct has no punishment, firm opposite in theoretical leads to eternal punishment, and the remaining three (non-firm theoretical, firm and non-firm practical) have temporary punishment varying in quality and duration. Consider the clause about the empty soul being in God's mercy.
This empty state refers to a soul devoid of perfection, desire for it, and its opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدمي الثلاثة كنقصان الغريزة، أو وجودي راسخ، أو غير راسخ . كل من بحسب القوة النظرية أو العملية . فالذي بحسب نقصان الغريزة لا عذاب عليه . والذي بحسب مضاد راسخ في القوة النظرية كالجهل المركب، فعذابه دائم . والثلاثة الباقية تزول بعد عذاب مختلف في الكيف والكم بحسب اختلاف الهيئات المضادة في شدة الرداءة وضعفها وفي سرعة الزوال وبطئه، وإن كانت النفس خالية عن الكمال والشوق إليه وعما يضاده فهي في سعة من رحمة الله تعالى. ولم يجوز بعضهم كونها معطلة عن الإدراك، فزعم أنها لا بد أن تتعلق بجسم آخر ، على أن تكون نفساً له تدبره. وهذا هو هو التناسخ، أو على أن تستعمله لإمكان التخيل، فتتخيل الصور التي كانت عندها، وتلتذ بذلك، ولا يكون أن تستعمله لإمكان التخيل، فتتخيل الصور التي كانت عندها، وتلتذ بذلك، ولا يكون ذلك الجسم مزاجاً ليقتضي فيضان نفس، بل يكون جرماً سماوياً، أو هوائياً أو نحو ذلك . ولم يستبعد بعضهم المعاد الجسماني، لأن للتبشير والإنذار نفعاً ظاهراً في أمر النظام والإيفاء بذلك بثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي ازدياد للنفع بالقياس إلى الأكثرين، وإن كان ضرراً للمعذب.
The three [types are] non-existential, like deficiency of instinct, or existential firm, or non-firm. Each is according to the theoretical or practical faculty. So that which is due to deficiency of instinct has no punishment. And that which is due to a firm opposite in the theoretical faculty, like compound ignorance, its punishment is eternal. And the remaining three pass away after punishment varying in quality and quantity according to the difference of the opposing states in severity of badness and weakness and in speed of removal and slowness. And if the soul is empty of perfection and desire for it and what opposes it, it is in the expanse of God's mercy. And some did not permit it to be devoid of perception, so they claimed that it must attach to another body, either to be a soul that governs it—and this is transmigration (al-tanāsukh)—or to use it for the possibility of imagination, so it imagines the images that were with it and enjoys that, and that body is not a mixture that necessitates the flow of a soul, but rather it is a celestial body, or an aerial body, or the like. And some did not deem bodily resurrection far-fetched, because for glad tidings and warnings there is an apparent benefit in the matter of order and fulfilling that with reward of the obedient and punishment of the disobedient, an increase of benefit in relation to the majority, even if it is harm for the punished.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the deficiencies of the soul and their corresponding punishments in the afterlife. The author begins by stating that there are three types of deficiencies: (1) non-existential (ʿadamī), such as a lack of natural instinct; (2) existential firm (wujūdī rāsikh), meaning a firmly rooted opposing quality; (3) existential non-firm (ghayr rāsikh). Each of these is then considered in two faculties: the theoretical faculty (al-quwwa al-naẓariyya) and the practical faculty (al-quwwa al-ʿamaliyya). The author explains the consequences: a deficiency due to lack of instinct incurs no punishment. A firm opposite in the theoretical faculty, such as compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), leads to eternal punishment. The other three types (non-firm theoretical, and firm and non-firm practical) result in punishment that is temporary, varying in quality and quantity depending on the severity and persistence of the opposing states. If the soul is devoid of perfection, desire, and opposite, it is in God's mercy. The author then addresses a philosophical objection: some thinkers refuse to allow the soul to be without perception after death, claiming it must attach to another body—either as a governing soul (transmigration) or as a means for imagination using a celestial or aerial body. Finally, the author notes that some do not reject bodily resurrection because it serves a useful purpose for exhortation, even if it is harmful for the punished.

Key Terms
عدمي
وجودي راسخ
وجودي غير راسخ
القوة النظرية
القوة العملية
الجهل المركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All deficiencies lead to eternal punishment.
Correction: Only a firm opposite in the theoretical faculty (compound ignorance) results in eternal punishment; other deficiencies have temporary or no punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the classification in this passage, what determines whether a soul receives eternal punishment?
A. Any deficiency in the theoretical faculty
B. A firm existential opposite in the theoretical faculty, like compound ignorance
C. Lack of instinct in the practical faculty
D. Any existential deficiency

Key Takeaway
The punishment for a soul deficiency depends on its type and faculty, with only compound ignorance in the theoretical faculty receiving eternal torment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What belief do you hold that you have never questioned, even though it might be compound ignorance? What step will you take this week to examine it honestly?
?
Are you satisfied with having only a natural instinct for good without actively pursuing perfection? How does your soul's emptiness or fullness affect your hope for God's mercy?
?
If your deficiency in the practical faculty is non-firm, do you rely on temporary punishment being light? What action can you take today to uproot a bad habit before it becomes firm?

Transmigration and Bodily Resurrection
Sanusi discusses transmigration and bodily resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theories of transmigration and attachment to other bodies as alternatives to bodily resurrection, and the rationale for affirming bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
Some thinkers propose the soul never ceases perception by attaching to another body, but bodily resurrection remains doctrinally sound.

Having discussed the fate of deficient souls, Sanusi now addresses a philosophical challenge: if the soul is always perceiving, it cannot be inactive after death. This leads to theories of transmigration and use of celestial bodies. The passage concludes by affirming the benefit of bodily resurrection for obedience and warning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Some scholars refused to allow the soul to be without perception, claiming it must attach to another body.
This is the premise that leads to transmigration.
2
Understand the two possibilities
The soul either governs the new body (transmigration) or uses it for imagination of past images.
The second possibility does not require a new soul to flow into the body.
3
Identify the type of body
The body used is not a mixture (mizāj) but a celestial or aerial body.
This avoids the need for a new soul generation.
4
Grasp the rationale for bodily resurrection
The author notes that some do not find bodily resurrection far-fetched because it serves the purpose of reward and punishment for the majority, even if harmful for the punished.
This is a concession that resurrection has practical benefits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The three [types are] non-existential, like deficiency of instinct, or existential firm, or non-firm. Each is according to the theoretical or practical faculty. So that which is due to deficiency of instinct has no punishment. And that which is due to a firm opposite in the theoretical faculty, like compound ignorance, its punishment is eternal. And the remaining three pass away after punishment varying in quality and quantity according to the difference of the opposing states in severity of badness and weakness and in speed of removal and slowness. Commentary
This passage is a classification of the deficiencies of the soul and their corresponding punishments in the afterlife. The author begins by stating that there are three types of deficiencies: (1) non-existential (ʿadamī), such as a lack of natural instinct; (2) existential firm (wujūdī rāsikh), meaning a firmly rooted opposing quality; (3) existential non-firm (ghayr rāsikh). Finally, the author notes that some do not reject bodily resurrection because it serves a useful purpose for exhortation, even if it is harmful for the punished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you living with the awareness that your current actions are building a ledger that will be read back to you in bodily form, or do you compartmentalize your spiritual life from your physical life?
?
If you were to face the moment between death and resurrection, what would you hope for your soul's state? Are you investing in a relationship with your Creator that will sustain you even if all imagined attachments are stripped away?

Philosophers' Interpretation of Paradise and Hell
Author expounds the philosophers' view on paradise, hell, reward, and punishment as intellectual states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') reinterpret paradise, hell, reward, and punishment as intellectual pleasures and pains, and why they consider scriptural descriptions as metaphors.

Core Idea
The philosophers see paradise and hell as intellectual states, not physical places.

The philosophers (al-ḥukamā') offered a rational interpretation of the afterlife that differs from the literal scriptural view. This passage expounds their doctrine, distinguishing between those who affirm a world of images and the majority who reduce everything to intellectual states. Let's explore how they reconcile revelation with reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the distinction between two groups of philosophers.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage mentions two groups: those who affirm a world of images (ʿālam al-mithāl) and the majority (al-aktharūn) who interpret paradise and hell as intellectual pleasures and pains.
Both groups affirm an afterlife, but differ on its nature.
3
Understand the majority view
The majority view holds that human souls are eternal and experience either delight in perfections (felicity) or pain from loss of perfections and corrupted beliefs (misery). This is their concept of paradise and hell.
They consider souls to be eternal, whether pre-eternal or not.
4
Grasp the reason for unawareness
The passage explains that we are unaware of these intellectual states in this world because we are absorbed in managing the body and the turbidities of the natural world.
Awareness returns after death.
5
Recognize the metaphor conclusion
The author concludes that scriptural details of reward and punishment are metaphors for states of felicity and misery. Eternal misery is specifically compound firm ignorance, not simple ignorance.
This rationalizes revelation within a philosophical framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في تقرير مذهب الحكماء في الجنة والنار والثواب والعقاب، أما القائلون بعالم المثل، فيقولون بالجنة والنار، وسائر ما ورد به الشرع من التفاصيل، لكن في عالم المثل لا من جنس المحسوسات المحضة على ما يقول به الإسلاميون. وأما الأكثرون فيجعلون ذلك من قبيل اللذات والآلام العقلية، وذلك أن النفوس البشرية سواء جعلت أزلية كما هو رأي أفلاطون، أو لا كما هو رأي ارسطو، فهي أبدية عندهم ، لا تفنى بخراب البدن، بل تبقى ملتذة بكمالاتها، مبتهجة بإدراكاتها، وذلك سعادتها وثوابها وجنانها على اختلاف المراتب وتفاوت الأحوال، أو متألمة بفقد الكمالات، وفساد الاعتقادات ، وذلك شقاوتها وعقابها ونيرانها على مالها من اختلاف التفاصيل، وإنما لم تتنبه لذلك في هذا العالم لاستغراقها في تدبير البدن ، وانغماسها في كدورات عالم الطبيعة لما بها من العلائق والعوائق الزائلة بمفارقة البدن. فما ورد في لسان الشرع من تفاصيل الثواب والعقاب، وما يتعلق بذلك من السمعيات فهي مجازات وعبارات عن تفاصيل أحوالها في السعادة والشقاوة، و اختلاف أحوالها في اللذات والآلام والتدرج مما لها من دركات الشقاوة إلى درجات السعادة . فإن الشقاوة السرمدية إنما هي الجهل المركب الراسخ ، والشرارة المضادة للملكة الفاضلة ، لا الجهل البسيط ، والأخلاق الخالية عن غايتي الفضل والشرارة، فإن شقاوتها متقطعة بل ربما لا تقتضي الشقاوة أصلاً.
In establishing the doctrine of the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') regarding paradise, hell, reward, and punishment: As for those who affirm the world of images (ʿālam al-mithāl), they affirm paradise, hell, and all the details brought by revelation, but in the world of images, not of the kind of pure sensible things as the Islamic followers say. And as for the majority, they make that of the kind of intellectual pleasures and pains. And that is because human souls, whether they are made pre-eternal as is the opinion of Plato, or not as is the opinion of Aristotle, are eternal according to them, they do not perish with the destruction of the body, but rather remain delighting in their perfections, rejoicing in their perceptions—that is their felicity, reward, and gardens (jīnān) according to the difference of ranks and variation of states—or pained by the loss of perfections and corruption of beliefs—that is their misery, punishment, and fires (nīrān) according to what they have of differing details. And they are not aware of that in this world because of their absorption in managing the body and their immersion in the turbidities of the natural world due to the attachments and obstacles that vanish with separation from the body. So what has come in the language of revelation (sharʿ) of details of reward and punishment and what pertains to that of revealed matters (al-samʿiyyāt) are metaphors and expressions for the details of their states in felicity and misery, and the variation of their states in pleasures and pains and the gradual progression from whatever depths of misery they have to degrees of felicity. For eternal misery is only compound firm ignorance and the evil trait opposing the virtuous disposition, not simple ignorance nor traits devoid of the two ends of virtue and evil, for their misery is intermittent, indeed perhaps it does not necessitate misery at all.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (al-ḥukamā') doctrine on paradise, hell, reward, and punishment. It distinguishes two groups: those who affirm a world of images (ʿālam al-mithāl) and take scriptural descriptions literally but in that separate world, and the majority who interpret everything in terms of intellectual pleasures and pains. The author explains that human souls, whether pre-eternal (Plato) or not (Aristotle), are eternal and survive the body. They experience either delight in perfections (felicity, reward, paradise) or pain from loss of perfections and corrupted beliefs (misery, punishment, hell). These experiences vary in rank. The reason we are unaware of them in this life is our absorption in body management and the obstacles of the natural world. The author concludes that scriptural details are metaphors for states of felicity and misery. Eternal misery is specifically compound firm ignorance and the evil trait opposing virtue; simple ignorance or neutral traits do not cause eternal misery and may not even cause punishment.

Six Types of Soul Deficiencies
Author details the six types of soul deficiencies by combining three kinds with two faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixfold classification of soul deficiencies based on their cause (non-existential or existential, and whether firm) and affected faculty (theoretical or practical), and the corresponding consequences after death.

Core Idea
Soul deficiencies are classified by cause and faculty, determining eternal fate.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a detailed taxonomy of defects that prevent the soul from achieving its perfection. By examining whether the defect is due to a lack or an opposing quality, and whether it affects the theoretical or practical faculty, the author identifies six distinct categories, each with its own consequence after death. This classification is crucial for understanding Islamic eschatology and the nature of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three causes of deficiency
The passage divides deficiencies into three broad categories: those due to a non-existential cause (lack), those due to an existential cause that is firmly rooted, and those due to an existential cause that is not firmly rooted. Identify these three.
Non-existential deficiency is like a deficiency in intellectual instinct.
2
Multiply by the two faculties
Each of the three causes can affect either the theoretical or the practical faculty. This gives six types total. Note the author's use of 'القوة النظرية' and 'القوة العملية'.
The theoretical faculty deals with knowledge, the practical with action.
3
Learn the outcomes for each type
The author explains the post-death fate for each type: some are not punished, some suffer eternal punishment, and some vanish after death. Pay attention to the specific example of compound ignorance (الجهل المركب) which is firm theoretical.
The remaining three vanish after death but differ in severity of badness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفصيل ذلك أن فوات كمالات النفس يكون إما لأمر عدمي كنقصان غريزة العقل ، أو وجود كوجود الأمور المضادة للكمالات، وهي إما راسخة أو غير راسخة. وكل واحد من الأقسام الثلاثة إما أن يكون بحسب القوة النظرية أو العملية، يصير ستة ، فالذي بحسب نقصان الغريزة في القوتين معاً فهو غير مجبور بعد الموت، ولا عذاب بسببه أصلاً ، والذي بسبب مضاد راسخ في القوة النظرية كالجهل المركب الذي صار صورة للنفس غير مفارقة عنها، فغير مجبور أيضاً، لكن عذابه دائم . وأما الثلاثة الباقية، أعني النظرية، غير الراسخة ، كاعتقادات العوام ، والمقلدة، والعملية الراسخة وغير الراسخة كالاختلاف والملكات الردية المستحكمة وغير المستحكمة فيزول بعد الموت لعدم رسوخها، أو لكونها هيئات مستفادة من الأفعال والأمزجة ميزول بزوالها، لكنها تختلف في شدة الرداءة
And the detailed explanation of that is that the loss of perfections of the soul is either due to a non-existential matter, like deficiency of the instinct of reason, or an existential matter, like the existence of things opposing the perfections, and they are either firm or non-firm. And each of the three divisions is either according to the theoretical faculty or the practical faculty, making six. So that which is due to deficiency of instinct in both faculties together is not compelled after death, and there is no punishment due to it at all. And that which is due to a firm opposite in the theoretical faculty, like compound ignorance that has become a form of the soul inseparable from it, it is also not compelled, but its punishment is eternal. And as for the remaining three, namely the theoretical non-firm, like the beliefs of commoners and imitators, and the practical firm and non-firm, like bad character traits and vicious dispositions, firmly established or not, they vanish after death due to their lack of firmness, or because they are states acquired from actions and temperaments that cease with their ceasing, but they differ in severity of badness
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed breakdown of the six types of soul deficiencies by combining the three kinds (non-existential, existential firm, existential non-firm) with the two faculties (theoretical and practical). The author first restates the three causes: non-existential (lack of intellectual instinct) or existential (opposing qualities) which are either firm or non-firm. Multiplying by two faculties yields six. The author then explains each: (1) Deficiency of instinct in both faculties: no punishment, the soul is not compelled. (2) Firm existential in the theoretical faculty (compound ignorance that becomes a form of the soul): eternal punishment, also not remediable. (3-5) The remaining three—theoretical non-firm (e.g., beliefs of commoners), practical firm (bad habits firmly established), and practical non-firm (bad habits not firmly established)—vanish after death because they are not firmly rooted or because they are acquired from actions and temperaments that cease with the body. However, they differ in severity of badness. The passage ends abruptly but the classification is clear.

Key Terms
عدمي
وجودي
راسخ
غير راسخ
القوة النظرية
القوة العملية
الجهل المركب
الهيئات المستفادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All existential deficiencies (those involving opposing qualities) lead to eternal punishment.
Correction: Only firm existential deficiencies in the theoretical faculty (like compound ignorance) lead to eternal punishment. Other existential deficiencies vanish after death.

Checkpoint
Which of the following soul deficiencies leads to eternal punishment according to the passage?
A. Deficiency of instinct in both faculties
B. Firm existential opposite in the theoretical faculty
C. Non-firm existential opposite in the practical faculty
D. Non-firm existential opposite in the theoretical faculty

Key Takeaway
The only soul deficiency that leads to eternal punishment is compound ignorance firmly rooted in the theoretical faculty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Soul's Eternal Happiness or Misery After Death
Describes the soul's eternal happiness or misery after death and its unawareness in this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical view that the soul is eternal and experiences happiness or misery after death, and why it is unaware of this in this world.

Core Idea
The soul's eternal destiny is hidden in this life by its absorption in the body.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents the philosophical position on the soul's afterlife. According to the philosophers, the soul does not perish with the body but continues eternally in a state of happiness or misery based on its perfections or corrupt beliefs. The reason we are not aware of this now is our complete engagement with bodily existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
The passage uses the term 'أبدية' (eternal) as a key concept.
2
Identify the two states after death
The passage describes two possible experiences: happiness (سعادة) and misery (شقاوة). Note what each is associated with: happiness with perfections and perceptions, misery with loss of perfections and corruption of beliefs.
3
Understand the reason for unawareness
The text explains that the soul is unaware of this eternal state in the present world because it is absorbed in governing the body and immersed in the turbidities of the natural world. These ties vanish at death.
This explains why we do not experience the afterlife now.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider that this view affirms an eternal afterlife for the soul, with different degrees of reward and punishment. The unawareness is not due to non-existence but to bodily preoccupation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهي أبدية عندهم ، لا تفنى بخراب البدن، بل تبقى ملتذة بكمالاتها، مبتهجة بإدراكاتها، وذلك سعادتها وثوابها وجنانها على اختلاف المراتب وتفاوت الأحوال، أو متألمة بفقد الكمالات، وفساد الاعتقادات ، وذلك شقاوتها وعقابها ونيرانها على مالها من اختلاف التفاصيل، وإنما لم تتنبه لذلك في هذا العالم لاستغراقها في تدبير البدن ، وانغماسها في كدورات عالم الطبيعة لما بها من العلائق والعوائق الزائلة بمفارقة البدن.
They [the philosophers] hold that it [the soul] is eternal, does not perish with the corruption of the body, but remains delighting in its perfections, rejoicing in its perceptions — that is its happiness, reward, and paradise, according to different ranks and varying states — or pained by the loss of perfections and corruption of beliefs — that is its misery, punishment, and hellfire, with all their differing details. It is not aware of this in this world because it is absorbed in managing the body and immersed in the turbidities of the natural world, due to the ties and impediments that vanish upon separation from the body.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines the post-mortem state of the soul according to the philosophers. It states that the soul is eternal (أبدية), not perishing with the body's destruction. After death, it either experiences happiness by enjoying its perfections and perceptions (which is reward and paradise) or suffers misery due to loss of perfections and corrupted beliefs (which is punishment and hell). The degrees of happiness and misery vary. The reason the soul is unaware of this in the present world is its total engagement with governing the body and its immersion in the material world's obscurities, which are ties and obstacles that disappear only upon separation from the body.

Key Terms
نفس
سعادة
شقاوة
أبدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul's unawareness in this world means it does not exist or does not have a future state.
Correction: The text explains that unawareness is due to bodily preoccupation, not non-existence; the soul exists eternally and will experience its state after death.

Checkpoint

A) Because it does not yet exist
B) Because it is absorbed in managing the body and immersed in worldly turbidities
C) Because it has no awareness at all
D) Because it is destroyed with the body

Key Takeaway
The soul is eternal and after death experiences happiness or misery, but in this world it is unaware due to its immersion in bodily management.

Scriptural Descriptions Are Metaphors for the Soul's States
Explains scriptural descriptions as metaphors for the soul's states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that scriptural descriptions of paradise and hell are metaphors for the soul's inner states of happiness and misery, and that everlasting misery stems from deeply rooted ignorance and vice.

Core Idea
Heaven and hell are not just places; they are states of the soul.

This lesson explores a profound theological principle: the detailed descriptions of reward and punishment in the Quran and Hadith are not merely literal accounts of physical places. Instead, they are metaphors that reflect the soul's own condition. By understanding this, we can grasp the deeper meaning of eternal bliss and eternal suffering.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's claim that scriptural details are metaphors for the soul's states.
Pay attention to the terms 'مجازات' (metaphors) and 'عبارات' (expressions).
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that all detailed descriptions of reward and punishment in the Sharia are metaphors and expressions for the soul's happiness or misery. This is the central idea.
Consider how this reinterpretation changes the understanding of the afterlife.
3
Distinguish types of ignorance
The passage differentiates between compounded ignorance (جهل مركب) and simple ignorance. Everlasting misery is only caused by ingrained compounded ignorance and vice, not by simple ignorance or neutral morals.
This distinction is crucial for understanding why some souls suffer eternally and others do not.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this metaphorical interpretation affects your view of reward and punishment. Try to explain a scriptural description (e.g., 'rivers of milk') as a metaphor for a virtuous soul.
This helps internalize the concept beyond mere memorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما ورد في لسان الشرع من تفاصيل الثواب والعقاب، وما يتعلق بذلك من السمعيات فهي مجازات وعبارات عن تفاصيل أحوالها في السعادة والشقاوة، و اختلاف أحوالها في اللذات والآلام والتدرج مما لها من دركات الشقاوة إلى درجات السعادة . فإن الشقاوة السرمدية إنما هي الجهل المركب الراسخ ، والشرارة المضادة للملكة الفاضلة ، لا الجهل البسيط ، والأخلاق الخالية عن غايتي الفضل والشرارة، فإن شقاوتها متقطعة بل ربما لا تقتضي الشقاوة أصلاً.
Thus what has come in the language of the Law of detailed reward and punishment, and what pertains to that of the sam'iyyat (things heard from revelation), are metaphors and expressions for the details of its [the soul's] states in happiness and misery, and the variation of its states in pleasures and pains, and the progression from the depths of misery to the degrees of happiness. For everlasting misery is only compounded ignorance that has become ingrained, and the vice opposed to virtuous character, not simple ignorance, nor morals devoid of the extremes of virtue and vice, for their misery is intermittent, or perhaps does not necessitate misery at all.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of scriptural descriptions of the afterlife. It states that all the detailed descriptions of reward and punishment in the Sharia, and the sam'iyyat (matters known only from revelation), are actually metaphors (مجازات) and expressions expressing the soul's detailed states of happiness and misery. The variation in pleasures and pains, and the progression from lower degrees of misery to higher degrees of happiness correspond to these states. It then distinguishes everlasting misery: it is only compounded ignorance (الجهل المركب) that is deeply rooted, and the vicious trait opposed to virtuous character (الملكة الفاضلة). Simple ignorance, or morals that are neutral (neither virtuous nor vicious), do not lead to permanent misery; their misery is intermittent or may not be misery at all.

Key Terms
ثواب (reward)
عقاب (punishment)
مجاز (metaphor)
جهل مركب (compounded ignorance)
ملكة فاضلة (virtuous character)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that scriptural descriptions of paradise and hell are literal physical places.
Correction: The passage emphasizes they are metaphors for psychological states of happiness or misery.

Checkpoint

A) Simple ignorance
B) Compounded ignorance that is deeply rooted
C) Neutral morals
D) Physical punishment

Key Takeaway
Scriptural accounts of reward and punishment are metaphors for the soul's own states of virtue or ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you clinging to any form of compounded ignorance—any area in which you think you know but actually don't—and using that to avoid the truth about your own soul? What would it cost you to admit that ignorance today?
?

?
If the scriptural descriptions of reward and punishment are metaphors for your own inner states, then which state are you currently in—deep-rooted misery, intermittent discomfort, or ascending toward happiness? What will you do with that honest answer?

The Fate of Neutral Souls: Islamic View and Philosophical Theories
Describes neutral souls and philosophers' theory of celestial attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic view on the fate of neutral souls and the philosophical theories of reincarnation and celestial attachment.

Core Idea
Neutral souls rest in mercy, but philosophers debate their need for bodily perception.

This passage presents a crucial point in eschatology: what happens to souls that are neither perfected nor wicked? The author describes their state in divine mercy, then introduces a philosophical challenge that forces us to consider the nature of perception and the soul's need for a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage moves from a clear statement to a philosophical objection.
2
Identify the three categories of souls
The passage implies three categories: perfect souls, souls opposed to perfection (wretched), and neutral souls devoid of both. Focus on the neutral souls.
The neutral souls are called 'النفوس السليمة'.
3
Understand the philosophers' objection
Some philosophers argue that souls cannot be idle from perception; they must attach to bodies. Identify the two options they propose.
The options are reincarnation and celestial attachment.
4
Compare the Islamic view with the philosophical alternatives
The author presents the Islamic view first (mercy and happiness), then reports the philosopher's view as a counterpoint. Consider why the Islamic view is preferred.
The philosophers' view is not endorsed by the author; it is merely reported.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفوس السليمة الخالية عن الكمال، وعما يضاده، وعن الشوق الى الكمال، فتبقى في سعة من رحمة الله تعالى ، خالصة من البدن إلى سعادة تليق بها غير متألمة بما يتأذى به الأشقياء، إلا أنه ذهب بعض الفلاسفة إلى أنها لا يجوز أن تكون معطلة عن الإدراك، فلا بد أن تتعلق بأجسام أخر لما أنها لا تدرك إلا بالآلات الجسمانية، وحينئذ إما أن تصير مبادىء صور لها، وتكون نفوساً لها، وهذا هو القول بالتناسخ، وإما أن لا تصير، وهذا هو الذي مال إليه ابن سينا (۳) والفارابي (٤) من أنها تتعلق بأجرام سماوية، لا على أن تكون نفوساً لها مدبرة لأمورها، بل على أن تستعملها لإمكان التخيل، ثم تتخيل الصور التي كانت معتدة عندها وفي وهمها، فتشاهد الخيرات الأخروية على حسب ما تتخيلها .
As for the sound souls that are devoid of perfection, and devoid of what opposes it, and devoid of longing for perfection, they remain in the expanse of God's mercy, purified from the body to a happiness befitting them, not suffering from what the wretched suffer. Except that some philosophers held that they cannot be idle from perception, so they must attach to other bodies because they only perceive through bodily instruments. Then either they become principles of forms for them and become souls for them – and this is the doctrine of reincarnation – or they do not become so, and this is what Ibn Sina and al-Farabi inclined to: that they attach to celestial bodies, not as souls managing their affairs, but to use them for the possibility of imagination, then imagine the forms that were customary for them and in their imagination, and thus they witness the otherworldly goods according to what they imagine.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This is an explanatory passage that discusses the fate of souls that are neither perfect nor actively opposed to perfection. The author first describes such souls as remaining in divine mercy and attaining a suitable happiness without suffering. However, he then introduces a philosophical objection: some philosophers argue that souls cannot remain idle; they must perceive through bodily instruments. This leads to two possibilities: either they become souls for other bodies (reincarnation) or they attach to celestial bodies without being their souls, as Ibn Sina and al-Farabi maintained. In the latter view, they use celestial bodies for imagination and envision their mental images of the afterlife. The passage thus presents a non-Islamic philosophical theory about the intermediate state of souls.

النفوس السليمة
الكمال
الفلاسفة
التناسخ
الأجرام السماوية
التخيل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage endorses reincarnation or celestial attachment as valid doctrines.
Correction: The passage merely reports the views of some philosophers; the author's own view is the first part describing the soul's mercy and happiness.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers mentioned, why must the neutral soul attach to another body?
A) Because it cannot exist alone without a body.
B) Because it cannot perceive except through bodily instruments.
C) Because it longs for perfection.
D) Because it is punished for its deficiency.

Key Takeaway
Neutral souls rest in divine mercy, but some philosophers argue they must attach to bodies, leading to reincarnation or celestial attachment for imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The philosophers argued that souls need bodily instruments to perceive, but the Islamic view says these souls are purified from the body and experience happiness without them. Are you so attached to your physical senses that you struggle to imagine a happiness beyond them? How can you train your soul to desire the unseen realities of the afterlife?
?
In this lesson, the neutral souls are described as being in 'the expanse of God's mercy.' But mercy is not automatic—it requires a sound state. If your soul were examined today, would it be sound (خالية عن الكمال وما يضاده) or would it be tainted by sin and negligence? What will you do to ensure your soul is in that expanse of mercy?

Philosophers' View on Bodily Resurrection and Divine Wisdom
Philosophers explain their stance on bodily resurrection and its divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers regarded bodily resurrection as possible and beneficial within divine wisdom, without rationally proving it.

Core Idea
Philosophers saw bodily resurrection as possible and beneficial, not rationally provable but divinely wise.

This lesson explores a nuanced philosophical stance on bodily resurrection. While philosophers did not establish it through reason, they did not deny it outright. They considered it possible and even beneficial for social and spiritual order. The passage shows how they reconciled this with their principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a preliminary claim about celestial bodies, then the philosophers' position.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the preliminary claim
The first sentence mentions that a celestial body can be generated from air and vapors without requiring a human soul. This sets the stage for the possibility of a physical afterlife without the usual soul-body connection.
3
Understand the philosophers' position
Philosophers neither prove nor utterly deny bodily resurrection. They consider it possible, but not as a restoration of the exact same matter. They allow literal interpretation of relevant Quranic verses.
Key phrase: 'جعلوها من الممكنات لا على وجه إعادة المعدوم'.
4
Recognize the divine wisdom argument
The passage argues that promises and threats benefit social order and afterlife reform. Reward and punishment increase benefit for the majority, even if harmful for some, akin to cutting a limb to save the body.
This shows the philosophical reasoning for accepting bodily resurrection as a possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : ويجوز ان يكون هذا الجرم متولداً من الهواء والأدخنة من غير أن يقارن مزاجاً يقتضي فيضان نفس إنسانية . ثم إن الحكماء ، وإن لم يثبتوا المعاد الجسماني، والثواب والعقاب المحسوسين، فلم ينكروها غاية الإنكار، بل جعلوها من الممكنات لا على وجه إعادة المعدوم ، وجوزوا حمل الآيات الواردة فيهـا علـى ظواهرها ، وصرحوا بأن ذلك ليس مخالفاً للأصول الحكمية والقواعد الفلسفية، ولا مستبعد الوقوع في الحكمة الإلهية لأن للتبشير والإنذار نفعاً ظاهراً في أمر نظام المعاش وصلاح المعاد ، ثم الإيفاء بذلك التبشير والإنذار بثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي تأكيد لذلك وموجب لازدياد النفع ، فيكون خيراً بالقياس إلى الأكثرين، وإن كان ضراً في حق المعذب، فيكون من جملة الخير الكثير الذي يلزمه شر ،قليل بمنزلة قطع العضو لإصلاح البدن
They said: It is possible that this celestial body is generated from air and vapors without being accompanied by a temperament that requires the flowing of a human soul. Then, the philosophers, even if they did not establish the bodily resurrection and the perceptible reward and punishment, did not deny them to the utmost denial. Rather, they made them among possibilities, not on the basis of restoring the non-existent. And they allowed carrying the verses related to them on their apparent meanings, and they declared that this is not contrary to the philosophical principles and rules, nor improbable to occur in divine wisdom, because giving glad tidings and warnings has a clear benefit for the order of livelihood and the reform of the afterlife. Then, fulfilling that glad tiding and warning with reward for the obedient and punishment for the disobedient is an affirmation of that and a cause for increased benefit. So it is good in relation to the majority, even if it is harmful for the one punished. Thus it is part of the great good that entails a slight evil, like cutting off a limb to reform the body.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the stance of the philosophers (الحكماء) on bodily resurrection. It begins with a claim that a celestial body can be generated from air and vapors without requiring a human soul, thus opening the possibility for a physical afterlife without traditional soul-body unity. Then it explains that philosophers, while not proving bodily resurrection through reason, do not reject it outright. They consider it possible—not as a restoration of the exact same matter, but as a new creation. They allow interpreting Quranic verses literally. They argue this does not contradict philosophical principles, since divine wisdom includes maximizing benefit: the promises and threats serve social order and spiritual reform. The resulting reward and punishment increase benefit for the majority, even if it entails harm for some. This is compared to cutting a limb to save the body—a permissible small evil. The author is reporting this philosophical position without necessarily endorsing it, but showing it is not fundamentally opposed to revelation.

المعاد الجسماني
الثواب
العقاب
الحكماء
إعادة المعدوم
التبشير والإنذار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think philosophers categorically denied bodily resurrection.
Correction: The passage shows they considered it possible and beneficial, though not rationally provable.

Checkpoint
How do the philosophers view bodily resurrection according to this passage?
A) They deny it completely.
B) They affirm it as rationally necessary.
C) They consider it possible and allow literal interpretation of verses.
D) They say it is impossible because matter cannot be restored.

Key Takeaway
Philosophers did not reject bodily resurrection but considered it possible and beneficial, fitting divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What step can you take today to surrender that trial to Him?

The passage says reward and punishment are for the order of livelihood and reform of the afterlife. How does your own pursuit of pleasure or avoidance of discomfort align with that higher purpose? Are you living for the fleeting benefit of this world or for the lasting benefit of the next? What is one concrete change you need to make to prioritize akhirah?
?
The philosophers allowed literal interpretation of resurrection verses, though they couldn't prove it rationally. On what basis do you personally affirm the afterlife—reason, tradition, or both? Is your certainty strong enough to shape your daily choices? If not, what is one practice you can adopt to strengthen that conviction?
