Philosophers' Stance on Bodily Resurrection
Reports the philosophers' opinion that bodily resurrection is possible and beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some philosophers viewed bodily resurrection as possible but not necessary, and how they reconciled it with their philosophical principles.

Core Idea
Philosophers allowed bodily resurrection as possible for its worldly benefit.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from the philosophers (falasifa) that presents their nuanced position on bodily resurrection. While they did not affirm it as a central doctrine, they did not deny it outright. Instead, they considered it possible and permitted interpreting Quranic verses literally, arguing that the benefit of reward and punishment for social order makes it acceptable within divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the initial claim about the body being generated from air and vapors.
The passage begins with a conditional physical possibility.
2
Identify the philosophers' stance
Find where the text says they did not establish bodily resurrection but did not deny it utterly. They made it possible, not on the basis of restoring the nonexistent.
3
Understand the rationale
The text explains why this is acceptable: the benefit of promising and warning for worldly and spiritual order. Fulfilling the promises is beneficial for the majority, even if it harms some.
The key phrase is 'لأن للتبشير والإنذار نفعاً ظاهراً'.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The passage uses the analogy of cutting off a limb to heal the body. What does this imply about the philosophers' view of divine justice?
The analogy suggests permissible harm for a greater good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : ويجوز ان يكون هذا الجرم متولداً من الهواء والأدخنة من غير أن يقارن مزاجاً يقتضي فيضان نفس إنسانية . ثم إن الحكماء ، وإن لم يثبتوا المعاد الجسماني، والثواب والعقاب المحسوسين، فلم ينكروها غاية الإنكار، بل جعلوها من الممكنات لا على وجه إعادة المعدوم ، وجوزوا حمل الآيات الواردة فيها على ظواهرها ، وصرحوا بأن ذلك ليس مخالفاً للأصول الحكمية والقواعد الفلسفية، ولا مستبعد الوقوع في الحكمة الإلهية لأن للتبشير والإنذار نفعاً ظاهراً في أمر نظام المعاش وصلاح المعاد ، ثم الإيفاء بذلك التبشير والإنذار بثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي تأكيد لذلك وموجب لازدياد النفع ، فيكون خيراً بالقياس إلى الأكثرين، وإن كان ضراً في حق المعذب، فيكون من جملة الخير الكثير الذي يلزمه شر قليل بمنزلة قطع العضو لإصلاح البدن
They said: It is possible that this body is generated from air and vapors without being accompanied by a temperament that necessitates the emanation of a human soul. Then the philosophers, even though they did not establish bodily resurrection and sensible reward and punishment, they did not deny them with utmost denial, but rather made them among possibilities, not on the basis of restoring the nonexistent, and they allowed carrying the verses that occur concerning them upon their apparent meanings, and they stated that this is not contrary to the philosophical principles and rules, nor unlikely to occur in divine wisdom because for promising and warning there is an apparent benefit in the order of livelihood and the rightness of the hereafter, then fulfilling that promise and warning with reward for the obedient and punishment for the disobedient is an affirmation of that and a cause for increased benefit, so it is good in relation to the majority, even if it is harmful to the punished, so it is among the great good that is accompanied by a little evil, like cutting off a limb to heal the body.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents an objection or statement from some philosophers (possibly the falasifa) regarding bodily resurrection. It begins with a claim that it is possible for the body to be generated from air and vapors without requiring a specific temperament that would necessitate a human soul. Then it explains the philosophers' stance: although they did not establish bodily resurrection and physical reward and punishment, they did not vehemently deny them. Instead, they considered them as possibilities, not in the sense of restoring what has become nonexistent (since they believe in the eternity of the world? but here they allow it as possible). They permitted interpreting the relevant Qur'anic verses according to their apparent meanings, stating that this does not contradict philosophical principles nor is it unlikely in divine wisdom. The reason is that the benefit of promising reward and warning punishment is apparent for the order of worldly life and the rectification of the hereafter. Fulfilling that promise and warning by rewarding the obedient and punishing the disobedient confirms that benefit and increases it. Thus, it is good for the majority, even if it harms the punished individual, making it similar to cutting off a limb to heal the overall body. This passage shows the philosophers attempting to reconcile religious teachings with their philosophical system.

Reward is Grace, Punishment is Justice
Sanusi states that reward is grace and punishment is justice, not obligatory, with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reward is considered grace and punishment justice according to Sunni theology, and the three rational proofs for this position.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are not earned rights; they are divine grace and justice.

This passage from the ninth investigation establishes a foundational principle: while Allah has promised reward and threatened punishment, these are not obligatory upon Him in the sense of being deserved by human effort. Rather, reward flows from His grace and punishment from His justice. Sanusi presents this position and then offers three proofs, engaging with Mu'tazilite objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Understand the position that reward is grace, punishment is justice, and how 'obligatory' is qualified.
Notice the clarification that 'obligatory' only applies in the sense of promise and threat.
2
Examine the first two proofs
The general principle that nothing is obligatory upon Allah, and the insufficiency of acts to thank blessings.
The second proof includes an objection and reply – follow the dialectic.
3
Analyze the third proof
The argument from nullification by later disbelief/belief, and the condition problem.
This proof shows that if reward/punishment were deserved rights, they could not be nullified, but Islamic theology holds they are.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث التاسع - الثواب فضل، والعقاب عدل، لا يجبان على الله إلا بمعنى أنه وعد وأوعد فلا يخلف على اختلاف في الوعيد، ولا يستحقهما العبد إلا بمعنى ترتبهما على الأفعال والتروك، وملائمة إضافتهما إليها في مجاري العقول. ووافقنا على ذلك البصريون من المعتزلة وكثير من البغدادية . لنا وجو الأول - ما مر من أنه لا يجب عليه شيء. الثاني - الطاعات، وإن كثرت لا تفي بشكر بعض النعم، فلا يستحق عوض عليها. فإن قيل: تكليف الشكر على الإحسان مستقبح عقلاً، والشكر بلا مشقة صحيح، فلا بد للمشاق من عوض لئلا تكون عبثاً. قلنا : بعد تسليم قاعدة الحسن و القبح ، ولزوم الغرض المستقبح هو الإحسان للشكر، لا إيجاب الشكر على الإحسان . ولوسلم لزوم كون الغرض هو العوض فيكفي التفضل عوضاً. الثالث - لو وجبا استحقاقاً لما سقطا عمن واظب طول عمره على الطاعات ثم كفر أو على المعصية ثم آمن ولو كان الموت على الطاعة أو المعصية شرطاً في الاستحقاق لم يتحقق أصلاً لعدم اجتماع العلة والشرط.
The ninth investigation: Reward is grace, and punishment is justice; they are not obligatory upon Allah except in the sense that He promised and threatened, so He does not break (His promise) with a difference regarding the threat; and the servant does not deserve them except in the sense of their being consequent upon deeds and omissions, and the appropriateness of attributing them to them in the course of intellects. And on that the Basrans among the Mu'tazilites and many of the Baghdadis agreed with us. We have proofs: The first: What has passed that nothing is obligatory upon Him. The second: Acts of obedience, even if numerous, do not suffice for thanking some blessings, so no compensation is deserved for them. For if it is said: Imposing the duty of gratitude for beneficence is rationally ugly, and gratitude without hardship is valid, so hardship must have compensation lest it be futile. We say: After granting the basis of the good and the ugly, and the necessary purpose that is ugly is the beneficence for gratitude, not imposing gratitude for beneficence. And if it is granted that the purpose is compensation, then grace is sufficient as compensation. The third: If they were obligatory by way of desert, they would not be nullified for one who perseveres in obedience all his life then disbelieves, or for one who persists in disobedience then believes; and if death upon obedience or disobedience were a condition for desert, it would never materialize because of the non-conjunction of the cause and the condition.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the position of the ninth investigation: reward is grace and punishment is justice, meaning that neither is obligatory upon Allah in the sense of being a duty that He must fulfill due to the servant's claim. Rather, the only sense in which they are 'obligatory' is that Allah promised reward and threatened punishment, and He does not break His promise (although there is a difference regarding the threat—breaking it is not a deficiency). The servant does not 'deserve' them except in the sense that they are consequent upon deeds and omissions, and that it is rationally fitting to attribute them to those deeds. The author notes that the Basran Mu'tazilites and many Baghdadis agree with this. Then three proofs are given. The first proof is a general principle already established: nothing is obligatory upon Allah. The second proof: acts of obedience, even if abundant, cannot fully repay the blessings of Allah, so no compensation is deserved. An objection is anticipated: requiring gratitude for beneficence is rationally ugly, and gratitude without hardship is fine, so hardship must be compensated. The reply: even if we accept the basis of good and evil, the ugly purpose here would be the beneficence itself for gratitude, not imposing gratitude for beneficence. And if it is argued that the purpose is compensation, then grace itself suffices as compensation. The third proof: if reward and punishment were obligatory as a deserved right, they would not be nullified when a lifelong obedient person later disbelieves, or a lifelong disobedient person later believes—yet in Islamic theology, faith nullifies previous sins and disbelief nullifies previous good deeds. If it is said that death upon obedience/disobedience is a condition for desert, then desert would never be realized because the cause (the deed) and condition (death) never coincide simultaneously.

Key Terms
الثواب فضل
العقاب عدل
استحقاق
وعد
وعيد
قاعدة الحسن و القبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'not obligatory' means Allah might not reward the obedient.
Correction: The author clarifies it means not a deserved right, while promise ensures fulfillment.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why do acts of obedience not deserve reward?
A) Because reward is grace.
B) Because they are insufficient to thank even some blessings.
C) Because Allah is not obligated.
D) Because they are futile.

Key Takeaway
Reward and punishment are not earned but are consequences of divine promise and justice, grounded in Allah's grace and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you catch yourself feeling that Allah now 'owes' you something in return? How does this lesson's teaching that reward is grace, not a right, challenge that hidden sense of entitlement?
?
The third proof shows that if reward were deserved, it could not be nullified by later disbelief. Are there good deeds in your past that you secretly rely on as a guarantee of salvation, forgetting that faith is the condition? How does this reshape your trust in Allah's grace alone?
?
You know that your obedience cannot repay even one of Allah's blessings. Yet when you fall short in worship, do you feel you have let Allah down as if you owed Him a debt? How does understanding that punishment is justice (not vengeance) and reward is grace free you from that burden?

First Objection: Hardship Without Benefit and Its Refutation
First objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite objection against the non-obligatoriness of reward and punishment and how it is refuted by pointing to alternative purposes such as gratitude and the intrinsic wajh of hardship.

Core Idea
Hardship can be purposeful without requiring reward as benefit.

The first objection claims that imposing hardships without corresponding benefit is injustice, and if there is benefit, then supererogatory acts would be obligatory. This lesson presents the objection and its refutation, showing that the purpose may be gratitude or the hardship itself as a wajh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part where the opponent argues that imposing hardships without benefit is unjust.
Notice the connection between hardship and reward.
2
Follow the refutation
The refutation grants the need for a purpose but offers alternatives: gratitude, joy, or the hardship itself as wajh.
The key is that benefit is not limited to reward.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this refutation preserves non-obligation of reward while justifying hardship.

احتج المخالف بوجوه : الأول - إلزام المشاق بلا منفعة تقابلها - وهي الثواب ـ ظلم ، وبلا مضرة في تركها ـ وهي العقاب - مستلزم لوجوب النوافل لثبوت المنفعة في فعلها . ورد بعد تسليم لزوم الغرض بأنه يجوز أن يكون الشكر على النعم، أو السرور بالمدح على أداء الواجب ، وأن يكون إيجاب الفعل بناء على أن له وجه وجوب بصفة المشقة، أو جعل شاقاً لغرض آخر . الثاني - عدم وجوبهما يفضي إلى التواني في الطاعات والاجتراء على المعاصي . ورد بأن مجرد جواز الترك مع شمول الوعد والوعيد وكثرة النصوص في الوقوع غير قادح في المقصود . الثالث - لو لم يجبا، لزم الخلف والكذب في إخبار الصادق . ورد بأن الوقوع لا يستلزم الوجوب والاستحقاق من الله تعالى . والعقاب عـدل من غير وجوب عليه ولا استحقاق من العبد خلافاً للمعتزلة، إلا أن الخلف في الوعد نقص لا يجوز أن ينسب إلى الله تعالى، فَيُثيبُ المطيع البتة إنجازاً لوعده، بخلاف الخلف في الوعيد، فإنه فضل وكرم يجوز إسناده اليه ، فيجوز ان لا يعاقب العاصي. ووافقنا في ذلك البصريون من المعتزلة وكثير من البغداديين، ومعنى كون الثواب أو العقاب غير مستحق أنه ليس حقاً لازماً يقبح تركه . وأما الاستحقاق بمعنى ترتبهما على الأفعال والتروك ، وملائمة إضافتهما إليهما في مجاري العقول والعادات فمما لا نزاع فيه. كيف وقد ورد بذلك الكتاب والسنة في مواضع لا تحصى، وأجمع السلف على أن
The opponent argued with several points: The first: Imposing hardships with no benefit corresponding to them—which is reward—is injustice, and with no harm in leaving them—which is punishment—necessitates the obligation of supererogatory acts because of the benefit in their performance. It is refuted after granting the necessity of purpose by saying that it is possible that the gratitude for blessings, or the joy of praise upon performing the obligatory, and that imposing the act is based on it having a wajh of obligation by virtue of hardship, or making it difficult for another purpose. The second: Their non-obligation leads to laziness in acts of obedience and audacity toward sins. It is refuted by saying that mere permissibility of omission along with the inclusiveness of promise and threat and the abundance of texts on occurrence do not damage the intended purpose. The third: If they were not obligatory, there would be breach of promise and lying in the report of the truthful. It is refuted by saying that occurrence does not necessitate obligation and entitlement from Allah. And punishment is justice without obligation upon Him and without entitlement from the servant, contrary to the Mu'tazilites, except that breach in promise is a deficiency that must not be attributed to Allah, so He rewards the obedient certainly as fulfillment of His promise, unlike breach in threat, for it is grace and generosity permissible to be attributed to Him, so it is permissible that He not punish the disobedient. And on that the Basrans among the Mu'tazilites and many of the Baghdadis agreed with us. The meaning of reward or punishment being undeserved is that it is not a necessary right whose abandonment is ugly. As for entitlement in the sense of their being consequent upon deeds and omissions, and the appropriateness of attributing them to them in the course of intellects and customs, there is no dispute about that. How indeed has the Book and the Sunnah come with that in countless places, and the Salaf are agreed that
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the three objections of the opponent (presumably Mu'tazilites) against the position that reward and punishment are not obligatory, along with their refutations, and then a concluding clarification. The first objection: imposing hardships without corresponding benefit (reward) is unjust, and without harm in leaving them (punishment) would entail that supererogatory acts become obligatory since they have benefit. The refutation: after granting that there must be a purpose, it is possible that the purpose is gratitude for blessings or joy of praise, not necessarily reward; or the action is imposed because it has a wajh of obligation by virtue of hardship itself, or it is made difficult for another purpose. The second objection: if reward and punishment are not obligatory, people would become lazy in obedience and bold in sin. The refutation: mere permissibility of omission, given the comprehensive promise and threat and abundant texts, does not undermine the intended purpose. The third objection: if they are not obligatory, then the truthful (Allah) would be breaking His promise and lying. The refutation: occurrence does not necessitate obligation and entitlement from Allah. The author then clarifies: punishment is justice without obligation on Allah or entitlement from the servant, unlike the Mu'tazilites who hold it is obligatory. However, breaking a promise (i.e., not rewarding) is a deficiency and cannot be attributed to Allah, so He necessarily rewards the obedient to fulfill His promise. But breaking a threat (i.e., not punishing) is grace and generosity, permissible to attribute to Allah, so He may forgive the disobedient. The author notes that Basran Mu'tazilites and many Baghdadis agree with this. Finally, he explains the meaning of 'not deserved': it is not a necessary right whose abandonment is ugly. But the sense of 'desert' as consequence and appropriateness is agreed upon, as confirmed by the Book, Sunnah, and consensus of the Salaf.

Key Terms
ظلم
إلزام المشاق
النوافل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the first objection, which of the following is NOT mentioned as a possible purpose for hardships?
A) Gratitude for blessings
B) Joy of praise for performing the obligatory
C) To test the servant's patience
D) The hardship itself as a wajh of obligation

Key Takeaway
Hardships can have purposes other than reward, so non-obligation of reward does not imply injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult act of worship, do you ever resent it because you feel you're not getting enough reward? How does this lesson challenge that feeling and call you to a deeper trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
The refutation mentions that hardship can be for gratitude for blessings. Think of one blessing you often take for granted. How can you transform the difficulty of a specific worship into an act of gratitude for that blessing starting today?
?
What if the hardship you face in obedience is actually a mercy from Allah designed to purify you or draw you closer? Are you willing to embrace that hardship without demanding a visible reward, and if so, what is the first step you will take to change your attitude?

Second Objection: Non-Obligation Leads to Laziness and Its Refutation
Second objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that non-obligation leads to laziness and audacity, and why mere permissibility of omission does not undermine obedience.

Core Idea
Permissibility of omission does not cause negligence when promise and threat are affirmed.

The second objection argues that if reward and punishment are not obligatory, people would become lazy in worship and audacious in sin. The refutation shows that the mere possibility of omission is harmless given the comprehensive promise and threat and the abundance of texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The opponent claims non-obligation will lead to laziness in obedience and boldness in sin.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation says that mere permissibility of omission, along with the inclusiveness of promise and threat and many texts, does not damage the intended purpose.
The intended purpose is to encourage obedience and discourage sin.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider how the presence of strong incentives (promise and threat) can still motivate even if not obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Third Objection: Breach of Promise and Lying, and Clarification on Desert
Third objection, refutation, and clarification on desert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection about breach of promise and lying, its refutation, and the distinction between promise and threat, as well as the meaning of 'deserved' reward and punishment.

Core Idea
Breach of promise is a deficiency, but breach of threat is grace and generosity.

The third objection is the strongest: if reward and punishment are not obligatory, would that not imply that Allah could break His promise? This lesson presents the refutation that distinguishes between promise and threat, and clarifies that 'deserved' does not mean a necessary right.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent says non-obligation would entail breach of promise and lying in the truthful one (Allah).
2
Follow the refutation
The refutation states that occurrence does not entail obligation and entitlement from Allah. It then distinguishes: breach of promise is a deficiency, but breach of threat is grace.
3
Clarify the meaning of desert
The author explains that 'undeserved' means it is not a necessary right whose abandonment is ugly; but in the sense of consequence and appropriateness, it is agreed upon.
4
Note the agreement
The Basran Mu'tazilites and many Baghdadis agree on the permissibility of not punishing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objection and Answer on the Condition of Muwafaat
Author refutes objection on muwafaat condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of dying in a state of obedience (al-muwafaat) for reward leads to a logical impossibility, as argued by the author.

Core Idea
A condition that can never be met when the cause is present makes entitlement impossible.

The author now addresses a key objection from those who hold that final state (muwafaat) is a condition for reward. If true, entitlement would never occur because the cause and condition cannot coexist. Let's see the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, an objection, and a reply.
The objection is introduced by 'لا يقال' and the answer by 'لأنا نقول'.
2
Identify the objection
Find what the objector claims: that dying in obedience or disobedience could be a condition for reward/punishment according to the principle of muwafaat.
The objector is likely a Mu'tazilite.
3
Understand the author's reply
The author argues that if muwafaat were a condition, entitlement would never be realized because when the cause (the act) is present, the condition (state at death) is absent; when the condition is present, the cause has already ended.
This is a temporal mismatch argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان في آخر عمره ضرورة تحقق الوجوب والاستحقاق. واللازم باطل بالاتفاق. لا يقال: يجوز أن يكون موت المطيع على الطاعة، والعاصي على المعصية شرطاً في استحقاق الثواب والعقاب على ما هو قاعدة الموافاة. لأنا نقول: لو كان كذلك لم يتحقق الاستحقاق أصلاً لعدم الشرط عند تحقق العلة، وانقضاء العلة عند تحقق الشرط.
Faith at the end of life necessitates the realization of obligation and entitlement. And the necessary consequence is false by consensus. It cannot be said: it is possible that the death of the obedient one upon obedience, and the disobedient one upon disobedience, is a condition for entitlement to reward and punishment according to the principle of al-muwafaat (dying in a state of faith or disbelief). For we say: if it were so, entitlement would not be realized at all, due to the absence of the condition when the cause is present, and the cessation of the cause when the condition is present.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair. The author first asserts that faith at the end of life necessarily entails obligation and entitlement to reward/punishment, and that the opposite (the necessary consequence being false) is agreed upon. The objector then proposes that perhaps dying in a state of obedience or disobedience is a condition for entitlement, according to the Mu'tazilite principle of al-muwafaat (i.e., that one's final state determines desert). The author replies that if this were the case, entitlement would never occur: because when the cause (the act of obedience/disobedience) is present, the condition (the state at death) is not yet known, and when the condition is fulfilled, the cause has already passed away. Thus, the condition cannot be coherently applied.

Key Terms
الموافاة
العلة
الشرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition of dying in obedience is necessary for reward.
Correction: The author shows this condition leads to impossibility because the cause and condition cannot coexist temporally.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the condition of al-muwafaat for entitlement to reward?
A) Because the condition is impossible to verify
B) Because it would mean entitlement never occurs due to temporal mismatch
C) Because God does not need conditions
D) Because the objector conceded the point

Key Takeaway
The principle of muwafaat cannot be a condition for reward because it creates an unresolvable temporal gap between cause and condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your entitlement to reward depended entirely on the state of your heart at the moment of death, and you do not know when that moment will come, does your current obedience give you any guarantee? How does this lesson expose the risk of complacency in your deeds? What specific change in your attitude towards sin and repentance does this demand from you today?
?
You might assume that your past good deeds secure your place, but this passage shows that the condition for reward cannot be met if your end is bad. Are you gambling with your afterlife by delaying sincere repentance? What is one area of disobedience you are tolerating, thinking you will fix it later? Commit to addressing it now.
?
The author refutes the muwafaat condition because it makes reward logically impossible. But if your faith today is not accompanied by fear of a bad end, are you truly believing in the gravity of the final moment? How can you live today with the awareness that your next breath may be your last, and that your deeds will be judged by what you are upon it?

The Opponent's First Proof: Obligation Requires Reward
The opponent argues that obligations require reward to avoid injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that imposing obligations without reward would be injustice, and why reward is considered necessary.

Core Idea
If obligation had no benefit, it would be unjust — so reward must exist.

The first challenge to the Ash'ari position on divine obligations is presented here: if God imposes duties that involve hardship, must He not also provide a corresponding benefit (reward) to avoid injustice? This lesson unpacks the opponent's reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully and its translation. Notice how the argument builds from the premise that hardship without benefit is injustice.
2
Identify the two key inferences
The opponent first claims that benefit (reward) is needed to avoid injustice. Then they argue that acts are obligatory only to repel harm, not gain benefit. See how these lead to the conclusion that punishment must follow omission.
3
Reflect on the logical flow
Consider the conditional: if hardship without benefit is injustice, and reward is the benefit, then reward must be attached to obligation. Then, since obligation is for repelling harm, abandoning must entail punishment.
This sets up the need for the author's rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المخالف بوجوه: الأول - أن إلزام المشاق من غير منفعة مؤقتة تقابلها تكون ظلماً، والله منزه عن الظلم، وتلك المنفعة هي الثواب. ثم إن الفعل لا يجب عقلاً لأجل تحصيل المنفعة وإلا لوجب النوافل وإنما يجب لدفع المضرة، فلزم استحقاق العقاب بتركه ليحسن إيجابه. ورد بعد تسليم لزوم الغرض بأنه يجوز أن يكون شكراً للنعم السابقة، أو يكون الغرض أمراً آخر، كحصول السرور بالمدح على أداء الواجب، واحتمال المشاق في طاعة الخالق، على أنه يجوز أن يكون إيجاب الواجبات بناء على أن لها وجه وجوب في أنفسها، وما يقال من أنه لو كان كذلك لوجب على الله تعالى أن لا يجعلها شاقة علينا بأن يزيد في قوانا لأن وجه الوجوب لا يتوقف على كونها شاقة، كرد الوديعة، وترك الظلم يجب سواء كان شاقاً أو لا، فليس بشيء لجواز أن يكون وجوبها بهذا الوجه. ولأن الوجوب وإن لم يتوقف على كونها شاقة لكن لم يكن منافياً لذلك، فيجوز أن تجعل شاقة لغرض آخر.
The opponent argued with several points: The first – that imposing hardships without a temporary benefit corresponding to them would be injustice, and God is exalted above injustice, and that benefit is reward. Then, an act is not rationally obligatory for the sake of obtaining benefit, otherwise supererogatory acts would be obligatory; rather it is only obligatory for repelling harm. So it necessarily follows that there is entitlement to punishment for leaving it so that its imposition is proper. It was rebutted, after conceding the necessity of a purpose, that it is possible that it is gratitude for previous blessings, or that the purpose is something else, like obtaining joy from praise for performing the duty, and enduring hardships in obedience to the Creator, and also that it is possible that the imposition of duties is based on their having an aspect of obligation in themselves. And what is said – that if this were so, then God Almighty would have to not make them difficult for us by increasing our powers, because the aspect of obligation does not depend on their being difficult, like returning a deposit, and refraining from injustice is obligatory whether it is difficult or not – is nothing, because it is possible that their obligation is in this respect. And because obligation, even if it does not depend on being difficult, is not incompatible with it, so it is permissible to make them difficult for another purpose.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage contains the opponent's first argument and its rebuttal. The opponent argues that imposing obligations (which involve hardship) without a corresponding temporary benefit (i.e., reward) would be injustice; God is just, so there must be reward. Moreover, acts are obligatory only to repel harm, not to gain benefit (otherwise supererogatory acts would be obligatory). Hence, abandoning an obligatory act must entail entitlement to punishment to make the obligation proper. The author rebuts by first conceding that there must be a purpose, but offers alternatives: obligations could be gratitude for previous blessings, or the purpose could be the joy of praise or enduring hardship for the Creator. Furthermore, obligations may have inherent worth; the objection that God would then have to remove hardship is invalid, because obligation does not depend on ease (e.g., returning a deposit is obligatory whether easy or hard). Hardship may be added for another reason. Thus, reward is not the only possible purpose.

Key Terms
المشاق
المنفعة المؤقتة
الظلم
الثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That obligations must have a reward attached to avoid divine injustice.
Correction: The author will later show that reward is not the only possible purpose; obligations can be for gratitude or have inherent worth.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent's argument, what is the immediate benefit that justifies imposing hardship?
A) Gratitude
B) Reward
C) Inherent worth
D) Repelling harm

Key Takeaway
The opponent's first proof asserts that obligation without reward would be unjust, and since God is just, reward must exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponent argues that without punishment, obligation would be unjust. Do you truly live with a sense of accountability for your actions, or do you assume God's mercy will cover everything? How can you cultivate a healthy fear of punishment alongside hope for reward in your daily life?
?
If you were to honestly examine your actions, do you perform obligations only to avoid harm (punishment) or also to gain the benefit of reward? Which motivation is stronger in your heart right now, and what does that reveal about your relationship with God?

Rebutting the Opponent: Alternative Purposes for Obligation
The author rebuts the opponent by offering alternative purposes for obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's rebuttal to the opponent's argument, showing that reward is not the only possible purpose for obligations.

Core Idea
Obligation can have other purposes besides reward, such as gratitude or inherent worth.

Having presented the opponent's argument that obligations must have reward to avoid injustice, the author now responds. He concedes that there must be a purpose, but argues that reward is not the only possibility. This lesson explores the alternative purposes he offers.

Guided Path
1
Read the rebuttal passage

2
Identify the three main alternatives
The author lists: gratitude for blessings, joy from praise, and inherent obligation. See how each provides a purpose without requiring reward.
3
Examine the objection and response
The author then addresses a potential objection: if obligations are inherent, why make them difficult? The response uses examples like returning a deposit, which is obligatory whether easy or hard.
4
Synthesize the overall argument
Consider how the rebuttal weakens the opponent's claim. The purpose of obligation need not be a reward; it can be something else, and hardship can be added for another reason.

Second Proof of the Opponent and Its Rebuttal
The opponent argues that without reward and punishment, people would neglect duties; the author rebuts with sufficiency of promise and threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's second argument regarding reward and punishment and how the author rebuts it by appealing to the sufficiency of divine promise and threat.

Core Idea
General promise and threat, not necessary obligation, are enough to motivate obedience.

The opponent argues that if reward and punishment were not necessarily due, people would become lazy in worship and bold in sin. The author counters that the overall scriptural promise and threat, along with strong presumption of their fulfillment, provide sufficient motivation. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the structure of the argument sink in before analysis.
The passage is concise: opponent's claim then rebuttal.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find why the opponent thinks reward and punishment must be obligatory. Notice the reasoning about human psychology.
The opponent relies on the idea that without certainty of compensation, people avoid hardship.
3
Analyze the author's rebuttal
See how the author responds: general promise/threat, strong presumption of fulfillment, and abundant traditions are sufficient. Note the final point about mere possibility not being harmful.
The rebuttal is practical, not theoretical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أنه لو لم يجب الثواب والعقاب لأفضى ذلك إلى التواني في الطاعات، والاجتراء على المعاصي، لأن الطاعات مشاق ومخالفات للهوى، لا تميل إليها النفس إلا بعد القطع بلذات ومنافع تربى عليها. ورد بأن شمول الوعد والوعيد للكل وغلبة ظن الوفاء بهما، وكثرة الأخبار والآثار في ذلك كاف في الترغيب والترهيب ومجرد جواز الترك غير قادح.
The second – that if reward and punishment were not obligatory, that would lead to laziness in acts of obedience and boldness in committing sins, because acts of obedience are hardships and violations of desire; the soul does not incline towards them except after being convinced of pleasures and benefits that compensate for them. It was rebutted that the comprehensiveness of promise and threat for everyone, the preponderance of the belief in their fulfillment, and the abundance of reports and traditions in that regard are sufficient for encouragement and deterrence, and mere possibility of non-fulfillment is not harmful.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This is the opponent's second argument and its rebuttal. The opponent claims that if reward and punishment were not necessarily due, people would become lazy in performing duties and audacious in sinning, since duties involve hardship against desires, and one only performs them when sure of compensatory pleasures. The author rebuts by stating that the generality of the divine promise and threat, the strong presumption that they will be fulfilled, and the many scriptural reports are enough to encourage and deter. The mere possibility that reward/punishment might not be due does not undermine this motivation. Hence, the opponent's practical objection fails.

Correction: The author shows that the general comprehensiveness of divine promise and threat, strong presumption of their fulfillment, and abundant scriptural reports are sufficient to motivate obedience and deter sin, even if reward and punishment are not strictly obligatory.

Key Takeaway

Third Proof and Rebuttal: Actuality vs Obligation
Opponent's third proof and rebuttal with Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's third proof from scriptural texts and the rebuttal that distinguishes actual occurrence from logical obligation, including the Ash'ari school's view on breaking the threat.

Core Idea
Scriptural promises guarantee occurrence, not divine obligation.

The opponent raises a strong argument: if God's threats and promises in the Quran and hadith are not necessarily fulfilled, would that not imply lying? The author responds by clarifying that actual fulfillment does not entail logical necessity, and then notes that the Ash'ari school actually holds that threats can be broken—a point that will be explored later.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's third proof
The opponent cites verses and hadiths promising reward and punishment, arguing that if not obligatory, God would break His promise.
This is a common objection from those who think divine justice requires strict enforcement.
2
Find the author's rebuttal
The author says that the texts guarantee actual occurrence (al-wuqu' al-battah), but that does not mean God is obligated or the servant has entitlement.
The key distinction: actuality vs. necessity.
3
Note the Ash'ari position on breaking threats
The author mentions that the school allows the possibility that punishment does not occur (al-khilaf fi al-wa'id), making the objection even more problematic.
This will be discussed further in the section on pardon (al-'afw).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - الآيات والأحاديث الواردة في تحقق الثواب والعقاب يوم الجزاء، فلو لم يجب وجاز العدم لزم الخلف والكذب. ورد بأن غايته الوقوع البتة، وهو لا يستلزم الوجوب على الله، والاستحقاق من العبد على ما هو المدعي. هذا والمذهب جواز الخلف في الوعيد بأن لا يقع العذاب، وحينئذ يتأكد الإشكال، وسنتكلم عليه في بحث العفو إن شاء الله تعالى.
The third – the verses and hadiths that have come concerning the realization of reward and punishment on the Day of Recompense; so if it were not obligatory and non-existence were possible, then breaking of promise and lying would follow. It was rebutted that its end is actual occurrence absolutely, and that does not entail obligation upon God, nor entitlement from the servant as is claimed. This, and the school [allows] the possibility of breaking the threat, i.e., that punishment does not occur; then the difficulty becomes more serious, and we will speak about it in the discussion of pardon, God willing.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage contains the opponent's third argument and its rebuttal, plus a note on the school's position. The opponent argues that scriptural texts (verses and hadiths) promise reward and punishment on the Day of Judgment; if these were not necessarily due and could fail to happen, that would entail lying and breaking promises on God's part (which is impossible). The author rebuts by stating that the texts guarantee actual occurrence, but that does not logically obligate God (i.e., it is not that He must do it out of necessity; He does it by His will), nor does it prove entitlement from the servant in the sense claimed. The author then notes that the school (Ash'ari) actually allows the possibility of breaking the threat (al-khilaf fi al-wa'id), meaning that God could forgive sins and not punish, which would make the opponent's objection even more problematic. This issue will be addressed later in the discussion of pardon. Thus, the scriptural proof does not establish the opponent's claim of necessary obligation.

Key Terms
الخلف في الوعيد
العفو
الاستحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That scriptural promises entail logical obligation on God, so if not fulfilled, God would be lying.
Correction: The author distinguishes between actual occurrence and obligation: the texts guarantee occurrence, but that does not mean God is under a logical necessity. The Ash'ari school even allows that punishment may be forgiven, which would not be a lie but an act of grace.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari school's view about the threat of punishment mentioned here?
A) It must necessarily be fulfilled
B) It may be broken, i.e., punishment may be forgiven
C) It is metaphorical
D) It only applies to non-believers

Key Takeaway
The opponent's proof from scripture fails because actual occurrence does not imply logical obligation, and the Ash'ari school permits forgiveness of punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you immediately assume you deserve punishment? This lesson shows that punishment is not an obligation on God. How does that change the way you think about repentance? Write down one sin you have been afraid God would not forgive, and then consciously trust in His mercy as you ask for pardon.
?
The opponents thought scripture proves God is obliged. Do you ever treat God's promises as if He were forced to fulfill them, forgetting His absolute freedom? Reflect on a time you felt entitled to something from God. Repent of that sense of entitlement and reaffirm that every good thing from God is a gift, not a right.
?
Do you rely on God's threat of punishment to keep you from sin, or on His mercy? If God were to pardon all your sins, would that make you less cautious? Examine your heart: Do you truly believe that God's mercy outweighs His wrath, and does that comfort or unsettle you? Commit to fearing God's punishment while hoping for His pardon, as the Ash'aris teach.

Objection and Answer on Mixed Rewards
Objection that reward and punishment are mixed with opposite elements, and its answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians defend the purity of paradise's reward and hell's punishment despite apparent mixing with opposite elements.

Core Idea
Paradise's bliss and hell's torment are pure because they exclude genuine hardship and voluntary virtue.

This lesson presents a classic objection in Islamic theology: if paradise dwellers still have some form of longing or obligation, does that not taint their reward? Similarly, do hell's inhabitants get some reward for avoiding sin? The author's answer clarifies that these elements are transformed or compelled, preserving the purity of each state.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection which claims that paradise reward is mixed with longing for higher rank and hardship of gratitude, and hell punishment is mixed with reward for avoiding sin.
Notice the objection has two parts: one for paradise and one for hell.
2
Understand the objection's premise
The objector assumes that any mixing with opposite elements diminishes the purity of reward or punishment.
This premise is challenged by the answer.
3
Read the answer and see how it addresses each part
The answer explains that in paradise, longing is absent because each person is satisfied, gratitude is a pleasure, and distractions prevent pain from avoiding sin. In hell, avoidance of sin is compelled, so no reward is given.
The answer shows that the supposed mixing elements do not actually constitute real hardship or voluntary virtue.
4
Reflect on the principle of compulsion
Consider why compelled actions (like avoiding sin in hell) do not yield reward. Only voluntary actions deserve reward.
This principle is key to understanding the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ثواب أهل الجنة يشوبه شوق كل ذي مرتبة إلى ما فوقها، ومشقة وجوب شكر المنعم وترك القبائح . وعقاب أهل النار يشوبه ثواب ترك القبائح فيها . أجيب بأن كل ذي مرتبة في الجنة يكون فرحاً بما عنده، لا يطلب الأعلى، ويعد الشكر لذة وسروراً لا يحصى، ويكون في شغل شاغل عن القبائح وذكرها، والتألم بتركها . وأهل النار لا يثابون لكونهم مضطرين إلى ترك القبائح .
For if it is said: The reward of the people of Paradise is mixed with the longing of every rank holder for what is above it, and the hardship of the obligation of thanking the Benefactor and abandoning evil deeds. And the punishment of the people of Hell is mixed with the reward of abandoning evil deeds therein. It is answered: Every rank holder in Paradise is happy with what he has, does not seek the higher, and considers gratitude an incalculable pleasure and delight, and he is busy with an occupation that distracts him from evil deeds and their mention, and from the pain of abandoning them. And the people of Hell are not rewarded because they are compelled to abandon evil deeds.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer unit. The objector claims that the reward of Paradise is not pure because each person longs for a higher station and must still thank God and avoid sin, which involve hardship. Similarly, the punishment of Hell is mixed with the reward of not committing sins. The answer explains that in Paradise, every person is completely satisfied with his rank and does not desire the higher; thanking God is itself an immense pleasure; and he is so occupied that he does not think of sins or feel pain from avoiding them. In Hell, the inhabitants are compelled to abstain from evil, so their abstention is not voluntary and thus carries no reward. The objection is thus refuted by showing that the supposed 'mixing' does not diminish pure bliss or pure torment.

Key Terms
ثواب
عقاب
شوق
شكر
قبائح
اضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that paradise contains real hardship or longing that diminishes reward.
Correction: The author explains that such feelings are transformed into pure pleasure or are absent due to contentment and distraction.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that reward in Paradise is mixed with hardship?
A) By stating that there is no hardship in Paradise at all.
B) By explaining that gratitude is a pleasure and the person is happily occupied, so no hardship is felt.
C) By arguing that the reward is only given after all hardship is removed.
D) By saying that longing for higher rank is itself a reward.

Key Takeaway
The purity of paradise's reward and hell's punishment is maintained because any potential hardship in paradise is transformed into pleasure, and any apparent virtue in hell is compelled, thus not rewarded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you view gratitude to Allah as a burden or a delight? Examine your daily attitude: when you thank Him, do you feel it as a joyful act or a mere obligation? What might change if you saw it as pure pleasure?
?
Are you content with what Allah has given you, or do you constantly yearn for more, believing that your current state is insufficient? The people of Paradise are fully satisfied; what is stopping you from finding that contentment now?
?
If you were completely absorbed in the pursuit of Allah's pleasure and remembrance, would you still struggle with abandoning sin? What 'occupation' in your life distracts you from evil deeds? Are you willing to seek that occupation?

Eternity in Paradise and Hell for Believers and Disbelievers
Establishing consensus on eternal reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus on the eternity of Paradise for believers and Hell for disbelievers, and why objections from al-Jāḥiẓ and al-ʿAnbarī are dismissed.

Core Idea
Eternal bliss for believers and eternal torment for disbelievers is agreed upon by all Muslims.

The author begins the tenth topic by establishing a point of absolute consensus: those who enter Paradise remain there forever, and those who reject faith—whether obstinately or after sincere but misguided effort—are eternally in Hell. This foundational teaching sets the stage for the rest of the discussion on eschatological matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage that states the consensus on eternity for believers and disbelievers. Notice the emphasis on 'لا خلاف' (no disagreement).
The phrase 'كافر عناداً أو اعتقاداً' covers two types of disbelief.
2
Understand the scope of the consensus
Note that even a disbeliever who made a sincere effort to find the truth but still rejected it falls under the general threat. This is a strong assertion.
This excludes those who were genuinely ignorant but not obstinate—but that is another discussion.
3
Examine the dismissed objections
The author explicitly dismisses the opinions of al-Jāḥiẓ and al-ʿAnbarī. Consider why their views are considered outliers.
Al-Jāḥiẓ was a Muʿtazilī theologian; al-ʿAnbarī was a Khārijī. Both held that some sinners might not be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث العاشر - لا خلاف في خلود من يدخل الجنة ولا في خلود الكافر عناداً أو اعتقاداً في النار، وإن بالغ في الاجتهاد لدخوله في العمومات ، ولا عبرة بخلاف الجاحظ والعنبري، وكذا الكافر حكماً كأطفال المشركين، خلافاً للمعتزلة، حيث جعلوا تعذيبهم ظلماً، فهم خدم أهل الجنة . وقيل : من علم الله منه الإيمان والطاعة على تقدير البلوغ ففي الجنة. ومن علم منه الكفر والمعصية ففي النار وأما من ارتكب الكبيرة من المؤمنين، ومات بلا توبة والمذهب عندنا عدم القطع بالعفو أو العقاب، بل إن شاء الله عفا، وإن شاء عذب، لكن لا يخلد في النار. وعند المعتزلة القطع بالخلود في النار. ولا عبر لقول مقاتل . ولبعض المرجئة : إن عصاة المؤمنين لا يعذبون أصلاً ، وإنما النار للكفار لنا وجوه .
He said: The Tenth Topic – There is no disagreement concerning the eternity of whoever enters Paradise, nor concerning the eternity in Hell of the disbeliever out of obstinacy or conviction, even if he exerted utmost effort [in seeking truth], because he falls under the general [verses]. No attention is paid to the disagreement of al-Jāḥiẓ and al-ʿAnbarī. Likewise, the one who is a disbeliever by ruling, such as the children of polytheists, contrary to the Muʿtazila, who consider punishing them an injustice, so they are servants of the people of Paradise. And it is said: Whoever God knows will believe and obey if he reaches maturity, then he is in Paradise; and whoever He knows will disbelieve and disobey, then he is in Hell. As for the one who commits a grave sin among the believers and dies without repentance, the established doctrine with us is not to assert definitely either pardon or punishment; rather, if God wills He pardons, and if He wills He punishes, but he will not remain eternally in Hell. And according to the Muʿtazila, the definite assertion is of eternity in Hell. No attention is paid to the saying of Muqātil. And some of the Murji'a say that the sinful believers are not punished at all, and that Hell is only for the disbelievers. For us there are proofs.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage begins the tenth topic (al-mabḥath al-ʿāshir) on eschatological matters. It first states the consensus that those who enter Paradise are there eternally, and that disbelievers—whether obstinate or convinced (but rejecting truth)—are eternally in Hell, even if they sincerely sought truth, because general scriptural threats apply. The objections of al-Jāḥiẓ and al-ʿAnbarī are dismissed. Regarding children of polytheists, they are treated as disbelievers by legal ruling (kāfir ḥukman) and will be in Hell, contrary to the Muʿtazila who consider this unjust; instead they will serve the people of Paradise. Another view is mentioned: God's foreknowledge determines whether such children would have believed or disbelieved had they lived, and they are accordingly assigned. Then the passage turns to grave sinners among believers who die unrepentant: the Ashʿarī position is that we do not assert definitely whether God will pardon or punish them, but they will not be eternally in Hell. The Muʿtazila assert definite eternal punishment, the opinion of Muqātil is disregarded, and some Murji'a say no punishment at all. The author concludes that we have proofs (lā wujūh), which are listed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
خلود
جنة
نار
كافر عناداً
اعتقاداً
جاحظ
عنبري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a sincere seeker of truth who ends up disbelieving will be excused from Hell.
Correction: The text states that even if he exerted utmost effort, he still falls under general threats and is not excused from eternal punishment.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the disbeliever who exerted utmost effort in seeking truth?
A) He will be forgiven because of his effort.
B) He will be punished less severely.
C) He is still eternally in Hell because he falls under general threats.
D) He is considered a believer if he made an honest mistake.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of Paradise for believers and Hell for disbelievers is a matter of consensus; no sincere effort excuses disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who rejects Islam after 'sincere' study, do you find yourself making excuses for them? How does this lesson confront your instinct to excuse disbelief?
?
If you are a believer, do you live with the certainty that your eternity is secure, or do you sometimes doubt? What would it mean for your daily life if you truly internalized the eternity of Paradise?
?
The author dismisses the opinions of disbelievers 'out of obstinacy or conviction'. Is there any area of your life where you are obstinate in rejecting truth? What would it take for you to surrender that obstinacy?

The Fate of Children of Polytheists
Ruling on children of polytheists and sectarian differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī ruling that children of polytheists are disbelievers by legal ruling and will be in Hell, and the contrary Muʿtazilī position.

Core Idea
Children of polytheists are judged by God's knowledge of what they would have done if they lived.

A difficult theological question arises: what happens to children of polytheists who die before reaching maturity? The author presents the Ashʿarī position that they are treated as disbelievers by legal ruling (ḥukman) and will serve in Paradise, while the Muʿtazila consider punishing them unjust. Another view appeals to God's foreknowledge of their hypothetical choices.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on children of polytheists
Read the Arabic from 'وكذا الكافر حكماً' to 'فهم خدم أهل الجنة'. Identify the two positions mentioned.
The passage also includes another opinion based on God's knowledge.
2
Understand 'disbeliever by ruling'
The Ashʿarī view is that children of polytheists are legally considered disbelievers, even if they are not personally culpable. This is because their parents' disbelief is attributed to them in this world.
This is a legal ruling, not a statement about their ultimate destiny in the afterlife—but the author states they will be in Hell.
3
Compare the Muʿtazilī objection
The Muʿtazila argue that punishing these children would be unjust, so they instead will be servants of the people of Paradise.
This is a partial agreement: they are not in Hell, but they are not fully rewarded either.
4
Consider the third view
The author mentions a view that God's foreknowledge determines their fate: if God knows they would have believed, they are in Paradise; otherwise, Hell.
This view reconciles justice with God's knowledge.

?

?

?
The Mu'tazila considered punishing an innocent child as injustice, but the Ash'aris rely on God's hidden wisdom. When you are faced with suffering that seems unjust, do you grumble or submit? What specific area of your life reveals a lack of trust in God's knowledge over your own reason?

The Fate of Grave Sinners Among Believers
Discussion on the fate of grave sinners and sectarian views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī position that grave sinners who die without repentance are left to God's will but will not be eternally in Hell, and how this differs from the Muʿtazilī and Murji'ī views.

Core Idea
Grave sinners are not eternally in Hell, but their ultimate fate is undecided.

One of the most debated issues in Islamic theology is the fate of a believer who commits a major sin and dies without repentance. The author presents the Ashʿarī position of suspended judgment (tawaqquf) regarding punishment, but affirms they will not be eternal in Hell, contrary to the Muʿtazila who assert eternal punishment, and the Murji'a who claim no punishment at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on grave sinners
Read the Arabic from 'وأما من ارتكب الكبيرة' to 'لنا وجوه'. Identify the three positions.
Note the phrase 'عدم القطع' – non-definitiveness.
2
Understand the Ashʿarī position
The Ashʿarīs say: we do not assert definitely whether God will pardon or punish the sinful believer, but we know he will not stay in Hell forever.
This is based on the idea that faith ultimately saves from eternal punishment.
3
Compare with Muʿtazila and Murji'a
The Muʿtazila assert eternal punishment; the Murji'a say no punishment at all. Both are rejected.
Also the opinion of Muqātil is dismissed.

?

Five Proofs for the Non-Eternity of Grave Sinners
Ashʿarī enumeration of five proofs for non-eternity of grave sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five arguments the Ashʿarīs use to prove that grave sinning believers will not remain eternally in Hell.

Core Idea
The five proofs show that divine justice, scriptural promises, and proportionality require eventual salvation for believers.

The Ashʿarīs offer five proofs to argue that even Muslims who commit grave sins will eventually be admitted to Paradise. These proofs draw on scriptural texts, the principle of justice, and the finitude of sin. In this lesson, we examine each proof and its logical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the five proofs. Then compare with the translation to ensure comprehension.
Notice the concise style: each proof is introduced with 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc.
2
Analyze the first proof
Understand that the first proof relies on scriptural texts promising believers entry into Paradise, and that they enter either after Hell or directly, implying eventual entry.
The phrase 'وفاقاً' means unanimously.
3
Analyze the second proof
The second proof cites texts that explicitly state believers will be taken out of Hell. This directly supports non-eternity.
4
Examine the third proof
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if a person who obeyed for a hundred years then drank wine were to be eternally punished, that would be unjust. Since even the Muʿtazila would not call it unjust (they consider it just), the Ashʿarī argue that true justice requires proportional punishment, so eternal punishment is disproportionate.
Common misunderstanding: this proof does not condone sin but highlights proportionality.
5
Study the fourth and fifth proofs
The fourth proof states that since sin is finite in time and degree, its punishment must also be finite for justice. The fifth argues that the believer's desert of reward is not nullified by grave sin, and reward can only be realized by exiting Hell.
The fifth proof references later discussion ('لما سيأتي').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا وجوه . الأول - النصوص الدالة على دخول المؤمنين الجنة وليس قبل دخول النار وفاقاً، بل بعده أو بدونه الثاني - النصوص الدالة على خروجهم من النار. الثالث - أن من واظب على الطاعات مائة سنة وشرب جرعة من الخمر، فلو لم يكن تخليده في النار ظلماً عندكم، فلا ظلم . الرابع - أن المعصية متناهية زماناً وقدراً ، فجزاؤها كذلك تحقيقاً للعدل. الخامس أن استحقاقه الثواب وعداً أو عقلاً لا يزول بالكبيرة لما سيأتي، ولا يتصور إلا بالخروج من النار .
For us there are proofs. The first: The texts that indicate the entrance of believers into Paradise, and not before entering Hell, unanimously, but rather after it or without it. The second: The texts that indicate their exit from Hell. The third: That if someone performs acts of obedience for a hundred years and then drinks a gulp of wine, if his eternal punishment in Hell is not injustice according to you, then there is no injustice. The fourth: That disobedience is finite in time and magnitude, so its requital is likewise, for the realization of justice. The fifth: That his deserving reward, by promise or intellect, is not nullified by a grave sin, as will come, and it is not conceivable except by exiting from Hell.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage lists the five proofs (wujūh) that the Ashʿarīs use to argue that grave sinning believers will not remain eternally in Hell. The first proof cites scriptural texts that promise believers entry into Paradise, and they do not enter Hell before Paradise—rather, they go to Paradise either after Hell (if punished) or directly. This implies eventual admission to Paradise. The second proof cites texts that explicitly state that believers will be taken out of Hell. The third proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if someone who obeyed for a hundred years but then drank wine were to be eternally punished, that would be unjust; but since even the Muʿtazila would not call such eternal punishment unjust (they consider it just), the implication is that it is not unjust—but Ashʿarīs argue that true justice requires proportional punishment, so eternal punishment for a finite sin is disproportionate. The fourth proof states that since sin is finite in time and degree, its punishment must also be finite to be just. The fifth proof argues that the believer's desert of reward, whether by divine promise or rational consideration, is not nullified by grave sin (this will be explained later); the only way such reward can be realized is by eventual exit from Hell. Thus the believer must eventually be saved.

Key Terms
وجوه
نصوص
دخول الجنة
خروج من النار
ظلم
متناهية
استحقاق الثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third proof suggests that sin is insignificant and believers can sin without consequence.
Correction: The third proof does not minimize sin; it argues that eternal punishment for a finite sin would be disproportionate, not that sin has no consequence. It emphasizes divine justice and proportionality.

Checkpoint
Which proof uses the idea that punishment should match the duration of the sin?
A) The first proof.
B) The second proof.
C) The third proof.
D) The fourth proof.

Key Takeaway
The five proofs collectively assert that divine justice and scriptural promises necessitate the eventual salvation of believing sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that a single sin after a lifetime of obedience could be punished eternally unless God decides otherwise, how does that affect your view of God's mercy today? Will you repent earnestly or take it lightly?
?
The third proof uses the example of someone who obeyed for a hundred years and drank a sip of wine. Have you ever committed a sin after a long period of obedience? How does this proof make you feel about God's justice?
?
The fourth proof says punishment must be finite because sin is finite. Does this give you a sense of hope or fear? How does it change your attitude towards repentance?

Responding to Scriptural Proofs for Eternal Punishment
Responds to Quranic verses used to argue eternal punishment for grave sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars reconcile Quranic verses that appear to threaten eternal punishment for grave sinners by interpreting them in context and qualifying their application.

Core Idea
Quranic threats of eternal fire are not absolute but qualified by repentance, minor sins, and context.

This lesson presents a detailed response to a key scriptural objection raised by those who argue that grave sinners (fasiq) will suffer eternal punishment. The author cites the problematic verses and then systematically addresses each point, offering interpretations that reconcile the verses with the Sunni position that no believing sinner will remain in Hell forever. By following this argument, you will see how the same verses can be understood differently when read holistically.

Guided Path
1
Read the cited verses
First, read the two Quranic verses (4:14 and 2:81) that threaten eternal punishment. Notice the general wording. Then read the translation to understand the apparent threat.
2
Follow the first move: conceding but excluding
The author concedes the generality of the wording but immediately notes that the repentant and those with minor sins have already been excluded from the first verse. This breaks the decisiveness.
3
Examine the nuanced interpretations
Go through each subsequent point: the condition of seeing punishment, the distinction between deserving and actual occurrence, the interpretation of 'khulud' as a long stay, and the restriction of 'fasiq' to disbelievers. See how each interpretation weakens the proof.
Pay attention to the method of reconciling conflicting evidences (jam' bayn al-adilla).
4
Synthesize the response
Reflect on how the author uses multiple interpretations to show that the verses do not conclusively prove eternal punishment for grave sinners. Consider the overall strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن يعص الله ورسوله ويتعد حدوده يدخله ناراً خالداً فيها) (۱) بلى من كسب سيئة وأحاطت به خطيئته فأولئك أصحاب النار هم فيها خالدون) (۲) والجواب بعد تسليم عموم الصيغ أنه قد أخرج من الأول التائب. وصاحب الصغائر، فلم تبق قطعية ،وفاقاً، فليخرج منها مرتكب الكبيرة أيضاً. على أن الاستحقاق فيها مغياً بغاية رؤية العذاب لقوله تعالى: (حتى إذا رأوا ما يوعدون) (۳) ولو سلم فاستحقاق العذاب المؤبد لا يوجب وقوعه، وأن معنى متعمداً مستحلاً قبله على ما فسره ابن عباس (رضي الله عنه أو المراد بالخلود المكث الطويل جمعاً بين الأدلة، وأن المراد بالذين فسقوا الكفار المنكرون للحشر، بقرينة قوله تعالى : ( ذوقوا عذاب النار التي كنتم بها تكذبون ) (٤) والباقي مختصة بالكفار جمعاً بين الأدلة، أو المراد بعدم غيبتهم سلب العموم أو المبالغة في المكث، وكذا الخلود، والمراد تعدي حدود الإسلام وإحاطة الخطية بحيث لا يبقى الإيمان .
And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will admit him to a Fire, abiding eternally therein (1). Yes, whoever earns evil and his sin encompasses him – those are the companions of the Fire, they will abide therein eternally (2). The answer, after conceding the generality of the wording: that from the first [verse] the repentant one and the committer of minor sins have been excluded, so it does not remain decisive, unanimously, so let the committer of the major sin also be excluded from it. Also, the deservingness [of punishment] in it is conditioned upon the sight of the punishment, due to His saying: (Until when they see what they are promised) (3). And even if it is conceded, the deservingness of eternal punishment does not necessitate its occurrence; and that the meaning of 'intentional' is one who considers it lawful, as interpreted by Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him), or the meaning of 'eternity' is a long stay, to reconcile the evidences; and that the intent of 'those who transgressed' is the disbelievers who deny the Resurrection, due to the context of His saying: (Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny) (4); and the remaining [verses] are specific to the disbelievers, to reconcile the evidences, or the meaning of 'they will not leave' is the negation of universality or exaggeration in the stay, and likewise eternity; and the intent of 'transgression of the limits of Islam' and 'the encompassing of sin' is such that faith does not remain.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage responds to the scriptural argument for the eternal punishment of the grave sinner (fasiq). It first quotes two Quranic verses (4:14, 2:81) that threaten eternal hellfire. The answer then proceeds: after conceding that the wording is general, the author notes that the repentant and those with minor sins have already been excluded from the first verse, so it is not decisively universal. Therefore, the grave sinner can also be excluded. Additionally, the deservingness in the verse is conditioned upon witnessing the punishment (as in 19:75). Even if eternal deserving is conceded, it does not guarantee actual occurrence. The interpretation of 'eternal' (khulud) is a long stay, reconciling all evidence. The 'transgressors' are interpreted as disbelievers denying the resurrection, due to the context of another verse (34:42). The remaining verses are specific to disbelievers to reconcile the evidence. Alternatively, the phrase 'they will not leave' denies universality or exaggerates the long stay. The encompassing of sin means transgression of Islam's limits such that faith no longer remains.

Key Terms
خلود
الفاسق
التائب
الصغائر
المكث الطويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that Quranic verses threatening eternal punishment apply universally to all sinners, including grave sinners who die without repentance.
Correction: The author shows that these verses are not universally applicable because they are qualified by repentance, the exclusion of minor sins, context, and alternative interpretations of key terms. The verses are reconciled with other evidence to show that believing sinners are not eternally punished.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the scriptural objection, which group has already been excluded from the verse threatening eternal punishment?
A) The polytheists
B) The repentant and committers of minor sins
C) The hypocrites
D) The prophets

Key Takeaway
The response demonstrates that apparent scriptural proofs for eternal punishment of grave sinners are not conclusive when verses are interpreted in context and reconciled with other evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about eternal punishment, do you automatically assume they apply to yourself or to someone else? How does this lesson force you to question that assumption?
?
If God's threats are conditioned and qualified as shown here, does that weaken your fear of sin or deepen your hope in His mercy? Be honest—what changes in your heart after understanding this?
?
This passage shows scholars reconciling seemingly contradictory texts. Do you have a habit of reconciling God's words with your actions, or do you selectively apply verses to avoid personal accountability?

Third Objection: Punishment of the Kafir by Analogy with the Fasiq
Objection and answer regarding the cessation of punishment for the kafir by analogy with the fasiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between the fasiq and the kafir regarding the cessation of punishment fails, and the three reasons given by the theologian in response.

Core Idea
Punishment for disbelief does not end because disbelief is infinite and analogy cannot override revelation.

This passage presents a third objection against the view that the punishment of a sinful believer (fasiq) is temporary. The objector argues by analogy that if the fasiq's punishment ends because his sin is finite, then the kafir's punishment should also end. The theologian responds with a three-pronged refutation, denying the causal link, the finitude of disbelief, and the validity of analogy when contradicted by revealed texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Note the logical structure: the objector uses analogy (qiyas) based on a common factor (jami') — the finitude of sin.
The objection is concise but contains a full qiyas structure.
2
Identify the three parts of the answer
The answer consists of three denials (man'). List them: (1) denial that finitude is the cause, (2) denial that disbelief is finite in quantity, (3) denial that analogy is valid when there is a revealed text.
Each denial targets a different assumption of the analogy.
3
Understand the special status of beliefs (i'tiqadat)
The passage ends with 'Wa fi al-i'tiqadat' — in matters of belief, analogy may not apply. This is a methodological point about the domain of rational analogy.
This connects to broader discussions in kalam about the sources of theological knowledge.
4
Engage with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your understanding of why the analogy is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - لو انقطع عذاب الفاسق لانقطع عذاب الكافر بجامع تناهي المعصية . والجواب - منع علية التناهي، ومنع تناهي الكفر قدراً، ومنع صحة القياس في مقابل النص. وفي الاعتقادات.
Third: If the punishment of the fasiq were to cease, then the punishment of the kafir would cease due to the common factor of the finitude of sin. And the answer: Denial of the causality of finitude, and denial of the finitude of disbelief in quantity, and denial of the validity of analogy in the face of the revealed text. And in [the matter of] beliefs.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This is another objection-answer pair. The objection uses analogy (qiyas): if the fasiq's punishment ends because his sin is finite, then the kafir's punishment should also end because his sin (disbelief) is also finite. The answer denies three points: (1) that finitude is the cause of punishment ending (it is not automatic), (2) that disbelief is quantitatively finite (it can be considered infinite in gravity), and (3) that analogy is valid when there is a revealed text (nass) that contradicts it. Additionally, in matters of belief (i'tiqadat), analogy may not apply.

Key Terms
تناهي المعصية
القياس
النص
الاعتقادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since both the fasiq and the kafir commit sins within a finite time, both punishments should be finite.
Correction: The theologian denies that finitude is the cause of punishment ending, and also denies that disbelief is finite in quantity. Moreover, analogy cannot override explicit revealed texts that state eternal punishment for disbelief.

Checkpoint
Why is the analogy that the kafir's punishment should also end rejected?
A) Because the kafir's sin is infinite
B) Because finitude is not the cause
C) Because analogy cannot oppose revealed text
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The analogy between the fasiq and the kafir fails because finitude is not the cause of punishment, disbelief is not finite in quantity, and analogy cannot override revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the eternity of punishment for disbelief, do you truly feel the weight of your own finite sins and the infinite mercy of Allah? How does this lesson change the urgency of your repentance?
?
Have you ever used a human analogy to question a clear divine command in your heart? In what area of your life do you need to submit to the revealed text rather than your own reasoning?
?
If disbelief is of infinite magnitude because it rejects the Infinite Creator, what does that say about the seriousness of every moment you spend neglecting your faith? What will you do today to ensure your belief is not taken lightly?
