The Persistence of Reward Despite Grave Sins
Argues that the believer's right to reward from faith and good deeds remains and necessitates eventual entry into paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that a believer's deserving of reward for faith and good deeds is not nullified by grave sins, and how this necessitates eventual entry into paradise.

Core Idea
The reward earned by faith and obedience cannot be lost through sin.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from the Ash'ari perspective, arguing that the believer's right to reward for faith and obedience remains intact even after committing major sins. The passage distinguishes between the Mu'tazilite rationalist view and the Ash'ari view based on God's promise, and concludes that the reward must ultimately be realized by exiting hell and entering paradise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the dense structure of this proof.
The passage is one continuous argument.
2
Identify the key terms
Pay attention to 'استحقاق الثواب' (deserving reward) and 'الكبيرة' (grave sin). These are central to the argument.
3
Understand the two perspectives
The author references two views: 'rationally according to you (Mu'tazilites)' and 'by promise according to us (Ash'aris)'. Notice that the Ash'ari view is based on God's promise, not rational necessity.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the proof's foundation.
4
Trace the logical flow
The argument moves from: (1) deserving reward, (2) persistence despite grave sins, (3) necessity of connecting reward to the believer in some state, (4) conclusion: exiting fire and entering paradise.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider the theological implications: even believers who commit major sins will eventually enter paradise. This counters the Mu'tazilite view that grave sinners are eternally punished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس ـ أنه استحق الثواب بالإيمان والطاعات عقلاً عندكم ووعداً عندنا . ولا يزول ذلك الاستحقاق بارتكاب الكبيرة لما سيجيء، فيكون لزوم اتصال الثواب إليه بحالة وما ذاك إلا بالخروج من النار والدخول في الجنة، وهو المطلوب.
Fifth—that he deserves reward for faith and acts of obedience, rationally according to you (Mu'tazilites) and by promise according to us. And that deserving does not disappear by committing a grave sin, as will come. So it becomes necessary to connect the reward to him in some state, and that is nothing other than exiting the fire and entering paradise, which is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof focusing on the deserving of reward (استحقاق الثواب). The author notes that the believer earns a right to reward for faith and righteous deeds. According to the Mu'tazilites (عقلاً عندكم), this is a rational necessity, while for the Ash'aris (وعداً عندنا), it is based on God's promise. This deserving is not annulled by committing a grave sin (الكبيرة), as will be explained later. Therefore, the reward must eventually reach the believer in some state, which can only mean exiting the fire (after any punishment) and entering paradise. Thus, the desired conclusion (وهو المطلوب) is that the sinful believer will ultimately be saved.

Key Terms
استحقاق الثواب
الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That committing a grave sin completely nullifies all previous good deeds and the right to reward.
Correction: According to this proof, the deserving of reward remains despite grave sins; it is not nullified.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the deservedness of reward according to the author's own school (Ash'aris)?
A) Rational necessity
B) God's promise
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Prophetic consensus

Key Takeaway
The believer's right to reward for faith and good deeds is not cancelled by grave sins, ensuring ultimate salvation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the reward for your faith and good deeds is absolutely secure and cannot be cancelled by any sin, does that comfort you—or does it make you complacent about your sins? Be honest: which tendency is stronger in your heart?
?
You know that you have committed sins after doing good. Do you truly believe that God's promise still holds for you, or do you secretly feel that your shortcomings have disqualified you? How does this lesson challenge that feeling?
?
Imagine standing before God with your deeds. The Mu'tazilite says you deserve reward by reason, the Ash'ari says by promise. Which perspective makes you more humble and more reliant on God's mercy? Which one causes you to despair or become arrogant? Examine your heart.

Mu'tazilite First Proof: Verses of Eternal Punishment
Mu'tazilites cite Quranic verses for eternal punishment of all sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite scriptural proof for eternal punishment of all sinners, including believers, based on Quranic verses.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites use general Quranic threats to argue that all sinners suffer eternal hell.

The Mu'tazilites advance several scriptural arguments to support their doctrine that anyone who commits a major sin, even a believer, will be eternally punished. This lesson examines the first such proof: a set of Quranic verses that appear to promise eternal hell for disobedience and sin. As you read the passage, pay attention to how the Mu'tazilites interpret these verses as general and unconditional.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the sequential citation of verses.
2
Identify each verse and its target
List the four verses cited. For each, note the sin mentioned (e.g., disobedience, murder, defiance, wickedness) and the punishment (eternal hell).
The Mu'tazilites take these as general, not restricted to disbelievers.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite reasoning
The author states these verses are 'meant for eternity and negation of exit' (مسوق للتأبيد ونفي الخروج). Ask yourself: why would this support the Mu'tazilite position that all sinners are eternally punished?
Consider how an Ash'ari might respond—by specifying these verses as for disbelievers only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة بوجوه : • الأول - الآيات الدالة على الخلود المتناولة ما للكافر وغيره كقوله تعالى: ﴿ ومن يعص الله ورسوله فإن له نار جهنم خالداً فيها (١) وقوله : ( ومن يقتل مؤمناً متعمداً فجزاؤه جهنم خالداً فيها ) ) وقوله : وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار كلما أرادوا أن يخرجوا منها أعيدوا فيها (۳) ومثل هذا مسوق للتأبيد ونفي الخروج. وقوله : وإن الفجار لفي جحيم .
And the Mu'tazilites argued with proofs: • First—the verses that indicate eternity (khulud) and encompass what is for the disbeliever and other than him, such as His Most High's saying: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His messenger, he will have the fire of Hell, abiding therein forever' (Surah al-Jinn 23), and His saying: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his reward is Hell, abiding therein forever' (Surah al-Nisa 93), and His saying: 'And as for those who are defiantly disobedient, their refuge is the fire; every time they wish to emerge from it, they are returned to it' (Surah al-Sajdah 20). And like this is meant for eternity and negation of exit. And His saying: 'And indeed the wicked are in Hellfire.'
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Mu'tazilites against the Ash'ari position. They cite several Quranic verses that threaten eternal punishment (خلود) for various sins. The first verse (Surah al-Jinn 23) threatens eternal hell for whoever disobeys Allah and His messenger—a general statement that includes believers who commit grave sins. The second verse (Surah al-Nisa 93) specifically promises eternal hell for intentional murder of a believer. The third verse (Surah al-Sajdah 20) describes that whenever the defiantly disobedient (الذين فسقوا) try to leave hell, they are returned, implying no exit. The author notes that such verses are expressed for eternity (التأبيد) and negation of exit (نفي الخروج). Finally, a verse from Surah al-Infitar (82:14) states 'the wicked are in Hellfire,' which the Mu'tazilites take as general for all sinners, including believers. These verses form the core scriptural evidence for the Mu'tazilite claim that anyone who commits a major sin, even a believer, will be eternally punished.

Key Terms
الخلود
التأبيد
نفي الخروج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These verses unconditionally apply to all sinners, including believers, proving eternal punishment for grave sins.
Correction: Ash'aris argue these verses are specific to disbelievers or that punishment for believers is temporary.

Checkpoint
Which verse do the Mu'tazilites cite that specifically threatens eternal hell for intentional murder of a believer?
A) Surah al-Jinn 23
B) Surah al-Nisa 93
C) Surah al-Sajdah 20
D) Surah al-Infitar 14

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites' first proof relies on Quranic verses they interpret as general threats of eternal hell for any disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the verse: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His messenger, then surely for him is the fire of Hell, abiding therein forever' (al-Jinn 23). This verse does not distinguish between believer and disbeliever. When you disobey Allah in a matter you know is wrong, do you feel the weight of this threat? Or do you silently excuse yourself because you are a 'believer'? Write down the last time you knowingly disobeyed and how you rationalized it. What does that say about the state of your faith?
?
The Mu'tazilites used these verses to argue that no sinner escapes hell. The Ash'aris responded that such verses are for disbelievers or that punishment can be temporary for believers. Which interpretation do you find more compelling—and more importantly, which one actually shapes your behavior? If you genuinely believed that every sin could land you in an eternal fire, would you make different choices today? Name one specific change you need to make.
?
The phrase 'And as for those who are defiantly disobedient, their refuge is the fire; every time they wish to emerge from it, they are returned to it' (al-Sajdah 20) describes a cycle of despair. Can you recall a time when you persisted in a sin, promising to stop but then returning to it? How does this verse mirror your own inner struggle? What would it take for you to break that cycle now, not out of fear alone, but out of love for the One who warns you?

The Fifth Argument: Reward for Faith Persists Despite Major Sin
Al-Taftazani presents the fifth argument: reward for faith remains despite major sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument for the possibility of forgiveness for major sinners: the reward for faith and obedience is not nullified by major sin.

Core Idea
The promised reward for faith remains valid even after major sins.

This lesson unpacks the fifth argument from our author against eternal punishment for believers who commit major sins. The argument hinges on a shared premise with the Mu'tazila: that the believer deserves reward for faith and obedience. The twist is that this desert is not cancelled by sin, so the reward must eventually be delivered, implying exit from Hell.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared premise
Notice that both parties agree the believer deserves reward for faith and obedience, though the basis differs (rational vs. promise).
The Mu'tazila hold it as rationally necessary; the author's school as a divine promise.
2
Understand the key claim
The argument states that this desert is not removed by committing a major sin. Ask yourself: why would sin not erase the desert?
The author says this will be explained later ('as will come later').
3
Trace the conclusion
Since the desert persists, the reward must eventually reach the believer. The only way is to leave Hell and enter Paradise. Thus, the major sinner is not eternally punished.
This argument assumes that reward and punishment are not temporally exclusive; punishment may precede reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس ـ أنه استحق الثواب بالإيمان والطاعات عقلاً عندكم ووعداً عندنا . ولا يزول ذلك الاستحقاق بارتكاب الكبيرة لما سيجيء، فيكون لزوم اتصال الثواب إليه بحالة وما ذاك إلا بالخروج من النار والدخول في الجنة، وهو المطلوب.
Fifth: That he deserves reward for faith and acts of obedience, rationally (عقلاً) according to you (Mu'tazila) and by promise (وعداً) according to us. And that desert (استحقاق) is not removed by committing a major sin, as will come later. So the reward must continue to attach to him, and that is nothing but exiting the Fire and entering Paradise, which is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument from the author's side. It begins with a premise: both the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ari/Maturidi agree that the believer deserves reward for faith and obedience—though the Mu'tazila hold this as rationally necessary (عقلاً) while the author's school holds it as a divine promise (وعداً). The argument then states that this desert for reward is not nullified by committing a major sin (كبيرة). The desert persists. Therefore, the reward must eventually reach the believer, which can only happen if the believer is taken out of Hell (after being punished for the sin) and enters Paradise. Thus, the major sinner cannot remain eternally in Hell. The argument assumes that the desert for reward remains intact despite the sin.

Key Terms

ثواب
إيمان
طاعة
كبيرة
عقل
وعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies punishment for major sins altogether.
Correction: The argument does not deny punishment; it asserts that the reward for faith remains and must be realized after punishment.

Checkpoint
On what basis does the fifth argument claim that the reward is not lost by sin?
A) Divine promise
B) Rational necessity
C) According to each school respectively
D) Consensus

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument shows that the desert for reward remains despite major sin, leading necessarily to the sinner's eventual exit from Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Which view do you find more natural: that reward is rationally deserved or given by promise? How does your answer affect how you approach God after sinning?
?
If you doubt that your reward for faith survives major sin, what does that reveal about your trust in God's promise? Will you today commit to trusting His word over your feelings?

Mu'tazila's First Scriptural Proof
Mu'tazila present scriptural proof citing verses on eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first scriptural proof for the eternal punishment of major sinners and the specific verses they cite.

Core Idea
General Qur'anic threats are used to argue for eternal punishment of sinners.

The Mu'tazila were known for their strict view that major sins lead to eternal punishment. They marshaled several Qur'anic verses that appear to promise eternal hellfire for sinners, including believers. This lesson presents their key proof, which relies on the apparent generality of these verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how each verse is cited as evidence for eternal punishment.
The verses are quoted with numbers; treat each as a distinct proof text.
2
Identify the logical structure
The Mu'tazila cite multiple verses to show that the threat of eternal punishment applies broadly to sinners, including believers who commit major sins.
3
Consider the implications
Think about how this proof supports the Mu'tazila position that major sinners (including believers) will be eternally punished.
This is the key point of contention between Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna.
4
Prepare for the refutation
The next lesson will cover al-Taftazani's detailed refutation. For now, note the strengths of this proof.
The refutation will challenge the generality and certainty of these verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة بوجوه :
•
الأول - الآيات الدالة على الخلود المتناولة ما للكافر وغيره كقوله تعالى: ﴿ ومن يعص الله ورسوله فإن له نار جهنم خالداً فيها (١) وقوله : ( ومن يقتل مؤمناً متعمداً فجزاؤه جهنم خالداً فيها ) ) (A) وقوله : وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار كلما أرادوا أن يخرجوا منها أعيدوا فيها (۳) ومثل هذا مسوق للتأبيد ونفي الخروج. وقوله : وإن الفجار لفي جحيم . يصلونها يوم الدين. وما هم عنها بغائبين وعدم الغيبة عن النار خلود فيها وقوله : ( ومن يعص الله ورسوله ويتعد حدوده يدخله ناراً خالداً فيها) (۲) لما وليس المراد تعدي جميع الحدود بارتكاب الكبائر كلها تركاً و إتياناً ، فإنه محال، بین البعض من التضاد كاليهودية، والنصرانية والمجوسية . فيحمل على مورد الآية من حدود المواريث. وقوله : بلى من كسب سيئة وأحاطت به خطيئته فأولئك أصحاب النار هم فيها خالدون (۳) .
والجواب بعد تسليم كون الصيغ للعموم أن العموم غير مراد في الآية الأولى، للقطع بخروج التائب وأصحاب الصغائر، وصاحب الكبيرة الغير المنصوصة إذا أتى بعدها بطاعات يربى ثوابها على عقوباته ، فليكن مرتكب الكبيرة من المؤمنين أيضاً خارجاً بما سبق من الآيات والأدلة. وبالجملة فالعام المخرج منه البعض لا يفيد القطع وفاقاً. ولو سلم، فلا نسلم تأبيد الاستحقاق، بل هو مغيـاً بغاية رؤية الوعيد لقوله بعده : حتى إذا رأوا ما يوعدون ﴾ (٤) ولو سلم فغايته الدلالة على استحقاق العذاب المؤبد، لا على الوقوع كما هو المتنازع لجواز الخروج بالعفو. وما يقال من أنا لا نسلم كون «حتى» للغاية بل هي ابتدائية . ولو سلم فغاية لقوله : ( يكونون عليه لبدا (٥) أو المحذوف، أي يكونون على ما هم عليه حتى يروا ، فخارج عن قانون التوجيه . وكذا ما يقال إنه لما ثبت الاستحقاق المؤبد جزماً وهو مختلف فيه حصل إلزام الخصم، ولم يثبت العفو، والخروج بالشك.
And the Mu'tazila argued with proofs:
The first: The verses indicating eternity (الخلود) that include what is for the disbeliever and other than him, such as His saying: “And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, indeed for him is the Fire of Hell, abiding therein forever” (1) and His saying: “And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever” (A) and His saying: “And as for those who are defiantly disobedient, their refuge is the Fire; every time they desire to emerge from it, they are returned to it” (3) and the like of this is meant for perpetuity and denial of exit. And His saying: “And indeed the wicked are in Hellfire. They will burn therein on the Day of Recompense. And they will not be absent from it” and absence from the Fire is eternity in it. And His saying: “And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will admit him to a Fire, abiding therein forever” (2) — but the intent is not transgression of all limits by committing all major sins (الكبائر) in leaving and doing, for that is impossible, given the mutual contradiction among some of them like Judaism, Christianity, and Zoroastrianism. So it is applied to the specific context of the verse concerning the limits of inheritance. And His saying: “Yes, whoever earns evil and his sin encompasses him, those are the companions of the Fire; they will abide therein forever” (3).
And the answer, after conceding that the linguistic forms are for generality (العموم), is that generality is not intended in the first verse, because of the certain exclusion of the repentant and those who commit minor sins (أصحاب الصغائر), and the one who commits a major sin that is not stipulated if after it he performs acts of obedience whose reward outweighs his punishment. Therefore, the believer who commits a major sin may also be excluded by the verses and proofs that have preceded. In summary, a general [text] from which some are excluded does not yield certainty (القطع) by consensus. Even if conceded, we do not concede the perpetuity (تأبيد) of desert (استحقاق); rather, it is limited by the limit of seeing the threat, due to His saying after it: “until they see what they are promised” (4). Even if conceded, its effect is to indicate the desert of eternal punishment, not its occurrence, as is the point of contention, because of the possibility of exit by forgiveness (عفو). And what is said—that we do not concede that “until” (حتى) is for limit, but rather it is inceptive—and even if conceded, it is a limit for His saying: “they will be upon it piled” (5) or the implicit [word], meaning they will be upon what they are until they see—that would be outside the rule of interpretation. And likewise, what is said: that since eternal desert is established with certainty—and it is disputed—[so] the opponent is forced, and forgiveness and exit are not established except by doubt.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage covers the first scriptural proof of the Mu'tazila and the author's detailed refutation. The Mu'tazila cite several Qur'anic verses that seem to promise eternal punishment for sinners generally, including believers who commit major sins. They argue that these verses indicate eternal punishment for such believers. The author responds by first conceding for argument's sake that the verses are general in form, but then he argues that the general application is not intended. He points out that certain groups are definitely excluded: the repentant, those who commit minor sins (صغائر), and those who commit a major sin but later perform enough good deeds to outweigh the punishment. Therefore, the believer who commits a major sin could also be excluded based on other evidence. He then states that a general text from which some exceptions are made does not yield certainty. Even if one concedes the general application, the author does not concede that the desert (استحقاق) for punishment is eternal; rather, it is limited until the fulfillment of the threat, as indicated by the verse 'until they see what they are promised' (سورة مريم 75), where 'until' (حتى) indicates a limit (غاية). Even if one concedes that the verse indicates eternal desert, it only points to the desert not to the actual occurrence, because the punishment could be lifted by divine forgiveness (عفو). The author then addresses a potential objection about the word 'حتى' being inceptive rather than for limit, and he refutes that interpretation. Finally, he notes that even if eternal desert is established as certain (which is disputed), the opponent's claim of forgiveness is based on doubt, so the argument does not decisively refute the author's position.

Key Terms
خلود
تأبيد
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the Mu'tazila's first proof?
A) The verses show that only disbelievers are punished eternally.
B) The general verses indicate that all sinners, including believers who commit major sins, deserve eternal punishment.
C) The verses are abrogated and no longer apply.
D) The verses only apply to hypocrites.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's proof relies on the apparent generality of Qur'anic verses promising eternal punishment to support their doctrine of eternal damnation for major sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read these verses about eternal punishment, does your heart tremble with fear or do you feel a sense of distance, thinking 'that's for others'? What does your immediate reaction reveal about how seriously you take your own sins?
?
The Mu'tazila used these verses to argue that even believers who commit major sins deserve eternal Hell. Does this thought make you examine your own life for sins that you have normalized or excused? What is one sin you need to repent from today with full sincerity, not assuming that Allah will simply overlook it?
?
If you were standing before Allah and He asked you why you didn't take these warnings seriously, what would you say? Are you living today as if you truly believe in the reality of Hellfire, or have you become complacent? Write down one concrete change you will make in your daily routine to align your life with the fear of Allah's punishment.

Refutation of the Mu'tazila's First Proof
Al-Taftazani refutes the Mu'tazila's first proof by challenging generality, certainty, and the necessity of occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's refutation of the Mu'tazila's scriptural proof, including arguments about generality, temporary desert, and divine forgiveness.

Core Idea
General verses do not yield certainty; eternal desert does not guarantee eternal punishment.

After presenting the Mu'tazila's proof, al-Taftazani systematically refutes it. He concedes that the verses are general in form but argues that they are not universally applicable. He further distinguishes between desert for punishment and actual occurrence, opening the door for divine forgiveness and the possibility that forgiveness may be granted even for major sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: the author first concedes a point, then offers exclusions, then questions certainty, then introduces a distinction between desert and actual punishment.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'istikqāq' (desert) and 'ghāya' (limit).
2
Analyze the first argument: exclusions from generality
The author argues that the general verses exclude the repentant, those who commit minor sins, and those who perform good deeds outweighing their sins. Therefore, the verses do not apply to all major sinners.
3
Understand the distinction between desert and occurrence
Even if one concedes the general application, the verses only indicate desert for punishment, not that the punishment will actually occur. It may be lifted by divine forgiveness (ʿafw).
This is a crucial theological point: God may forgive without contradicting the threat.
4
Examine the linguistic argument about 'until'
The author points to the phrase 'until they see what they are promised' to argue that the punishment has a limit. He refutes objections that 'until' might be inceptive, showing it indicates a limit.
This ties into the broader argument that eternal desert is not established.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
The refutation shows that the Mu'tazila's proof does not decisively establish eternal punishment for major sinners. The door remains open for divine mercy.

The Three Verses of Eternal Punishment and the Principle of Exception
Author cites three verses and gives initial responses focusing on specificity and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author responds to three Qur'anic verses that appear to support eternal punishment for sinners by interpreting them in context and allowing exceptions.

Core Idea
General verses do not exclude exceptions like repentance or minor sins.

The author presents three verses that opponents use to argue that grave sinners will be eternally punished. He begins by giving a multi-layered response: first, one verse is specific to inheritance laws, not general. Second, even general verses allow for exceptions such as repentance and minors sins. This lesson covers these initial responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses and responses
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three verses and the author's responses.
2
Identify the specific interpretation
Notice how the second verse (An-Nisa 14) is interpreted concerning limits of inheritance, not general transgression.
3
Understand the exceptions to generality
The author argues that the first verse is not general because it excludes the repentant, those with minor sins, and those whose good deeds outweigh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصلونها يوم الدين. وما هم عنها بغائبين وعدم الغيبة عن النار خلود فيها وقوله : ( ومن يعص الله ورسوله ويتعد حدوده يدخله ناراً خالداً فيها) (۲) لما وليس المراد تعدي جميع الحدود بارتكاب الكبائر كلها تركاً و إتياناً ، فإنه محال، بین البعض من التضاد كاليهودية، والنصرانية والمجوسية . فيحمل على مورد الآية من حدود المواريث. وقوله : بلى من كسب سيئة وأحاطت به خطيئته فأولئك أصحاب النار هم فيها خالدون (۳) . والجواب بعد تسليم كون الصيغ للعموم أن العموم غير مراد في الآية الأولى، للقطع بخروج التائب وأصحاب الصغائر، وصاحب الكبيرة الغير المنصوصة إذا أتى بعدها بطاعات يربى ثوابها على عقوباته ، فليكن مرتكب الكبيرة من المؤمنين أيضاً خارجاً بما سبق من الآيات والأدلة. وبالجملة فالعام المخرج منه البعض لا يفيد القطع وفاقاً. ولو سلم، فلا نسلم تأبيد الاستحقاق، بل هو مغيـاً بغاية رؤية الوعيد لقوله بعده : حتى إذا رأوا ما يوعدون ﴾ (٤) ولو سلم فغايته الدلالة على استحقاق العذاب المؤبد، لا على الوقوع كما هو المتنازع لجواز الخروج بالعفو. وما يقال من أنا لا نسلم كون «حتى» للغاية بل هي ابتدائية . ولو سلم فغاية لقوله : ( يكونون عليه لبدا (٥) أو المحذوف، أي يكونون على ما هم عليه حتى يروا ، فخارج عن قانون التوجيه . وكذا ما يقال إنه لما ثبت الاستحقاق المؤبد جزماً وهو مختلف فيه حصل إلزام الخصم، ولم يثبت العفو، والخروج بالشك.
They will broil in it on the Day of Recompense. And they will not be absent from it. And the absence from the Fire is eternity in it. And His saying: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will make him enter a Fire, abiding eternally therein' (2) because it is not intended transgression of all limits by committing all major sins in omission and commission, for that is impossible, some of them being contradictory like Judaism, Christianity, and Zoroastrianism. So it is interpreted upon the context of the verse concerning the limits of inheritance. And His saying: 'Yes, whoever earns evil and his sin encompasses him, those are the companions of the Fire, they will abide therein eternally' (3). And the answer, after conceding that the forms are for generality, is that generality is not intended in the first verse, for certain exclusion of the repentant, the people of minor sins, and the committer of an unspecified major sin if he afterwards performs acts of obedience whose reward outweighs his punishments. So let the committer of a major sin among the believers also be excluded by what has preceded of verses and proofs. In summary, a general that has some of its individuals excluded does not yield certainty, by agreement. And if conceded, we do not concede the eternity of deservingness, rather it is limited by the goal of seeing the threat because of His saying after it: 'Until when they see what they are promised' (4). And if conceded, its utmost is indication of deservingness of eternal punishment, not of occurrence, as is the point of dispute, because of the possibility of exit by forgiveness. And what is said that we do not concede that 'until' is for a goal, rather it is inceptive. And if conceded, it is a goal for His saying: 'they become upon it as a dense herd' (5) or the omitted, i.e., they are upon what they are until they see, so it is outside the rule of interpretation. And likewise what is said that since eternal deservingness is established definitively while it is disputed, the opponent has been compelled, and forgiveness is not established, and exit by doubt.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents three verses that opponents use to argue that grave sinners will be eternally punished. The author first quotes the verses: 'They will broil in it... not absent' (Al-Infitar 15-16), 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger...' (An-Nisa 14), and 'Yes, whoever earns evil...' (Al-Baqarah 81). Then he gives a multi-layered response. First, the verse from An-Nisa about transgression is interpreted specifically for inheritance limits, not all sins. Second, conceding generality, the first verse (Al-Infitar) is not meant generally because repentance, minor sins, and outweighing good deeds provide exceptions. Third, even if the general is conceded, it does not prove absolute certainty of eternal punishment, only the possibility. Fourth, the phrase 'until they see what they are promised' (Maryam 75) limits the punishment to a goal, and it may refer to the threat rather than actual occurrence, as forgiveness is possible. The author also rejects counterarguments about the grammatical function of 'until' by showing alternative interpretations. Overall, the argument denies that these verses prove eternal punishment for believers, maintaining that general verses allow exceptions and that the evidence for forgiveness remains.

Key Terms
خلود (khulud)
عموم (umum)
الكبيرة (al-kabirah)
الصغائر (al-sagha'ir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A general verse like 'whoever disobeys' must apply to every individual without exception.
Correction: General verses can allow exceptions such as repentance, minor sins, and outweighing good deeds.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the verse 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger...' does not prove eternal punishment for all major sinners?
A) It is abrogated by later verses
B) It only applies to the limits of inheritance
C) The word 'transgresses' means all sins
D) It is specific to disbelievers

Key Takeaway
The three verses cited do not prove eternal punishment for believing sinners because they are either context-specific or allow exceptions like repentance and minor sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a major sin you have committed. Have you truly repented and sought to perform good deeds that outweigh it? What is one specific good deed you can do today to start that process?
?
The author argues that threatening verses have exceptions. Have you ever felt hopeless reading such verses? How does this lesson change your view of Allah's mercy toward you personally?
?
If you knew that a single sin could doom you forever, how would you live differently? Now that you've learned that repentance and good deeds can outweigh even major sins, what is one sin you will resolve to leave today?

The Argument from Generality, the 'Until' Clause, and the Possibility of Forgiveness
Author provides deeper arguments: generality insufficient, 'until' limits punishment, and possibility of forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's arguments that even if the verses are general, they do not necessitate eternal punishment; rather, they indicate deservingness and are qualified by a limit or the possibility of forgiveness.

Core Idea
A general statement does not prove actual occurrence, only deservingness; forgiveness remains possible.

The author continues his response by addressing the nature of general statements and the specific phrase 'until they see what they are promised.' He argues that generality does not guarantee eternal punishment in reality, only that it is deserved. Moreover, the 'until' clause indicates a limit, and even if eternal deservingness is established, forgiveness can still occur. This lesson covers these refined arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim about generality
The author says a general that has exceptions does not yield certainty by agreement.
2
Analyze the 'until' clause
The phrase 'until they see what they are promised' limits the punishment to that point, not indefinitely.
3
Distinguish deservingness from occurrence
Even if eternal punishment is deserved, it does not mean it will actually happen; forgiveness is possible.
4
Consider the counterarguments
The author rejects arguments that 'until' is inceptive or that eternal deservingness is definitively established.

?

?

The Meaning of Khulud: Eternal or Prolonged Stay?
Author refutes claim that khulud must mean eternity, showing it can mean a long stay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabic word 'khulud' can mean a prolonged stay rather than necessarily eternal, and how this interpretation reconciles conflicting scriptural evidences regarding the punishment of sinful believers.

Core Idea
Khulud can mean a long stay, not necessarily eternal; context determines duration.

In this passage, the author addresses a critical objection: the Qur'anic verse about intentional murder (An-Nisa 93) seems to threaten eternal punishment. Critics argue that 'khulud' must mean forever. The author shows that 'khulud' is flexible and can indicate a long but interrupted stay, using examples from everyday language. This allows for a reconciliation of scriptural texts without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the dense style—the author presents multiple interpretations and counter-objections in compressed form.
2
Identify the three initial interpretations
The author gives three ways to interpret 'intentionally' in the verse to avoid the conclusion of eternal punishment. Find each one.
The third interpretation directly addresses the meaning of 'khulud'.
3
Understand the counter-objection
The opponent argues that 'khulud' must be literal eternity, citing common usage, a Qur'anic verse, the reinforcing word 'abad', and analogy with disbelievers. Notice each proof.
The opponent's argument has four parts.
4
Follow the author's response
The author admits the common meaning is eternity but gives counterexamples ('eternal prison', 'eternal endowment') to show 'khulud' can mean a long interrupted stay. See how he resolves the issue.
The author uses linguistic examples to show flexibility.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why the author's interpretation avoids both metaphor and homonymy, and how it applies differently to disbelievers (eternal) and sinners (temporary).
The key is that 'khulud' is more general than 'eternity'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثانية بأن معنى ( متعمداً مستحلاً فعله على ما ذكره ابن عباس (رضي الله عنه) إذ التعمد على الحقيقة إنما يكون من المستحل أو بأن التعليق بالوصف يشعر بالحيثية، فيخص بمن قتل المؤمن لإيمانه. أو بأن الخلود، وإن كان ظاهراً في الدوام .. والمراد ههنا المكث الطويل جمعاً بين الأدلة. لا يقال: الخلود حقيقة في التأييد لتبادر الفهم إليه، ولقوله تعالى : ( وما جعلنا لبشر من قبلك الخلد) (۱) ولأنه يؤكد بلفظ التأبيد مثل : خالدين فيها أبداً ) ) (۲) وتأكيد الشيء تقوية لمدلوله، ولأن العمومات المقرونة بالخلود متناوله للكفار. والمراد في حقهم التأبيد وفاقاً. فكذا في حق الفساق لئلا يلزم إرادة معني المشترك أو المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي معاً لأنا نقول : لا كلام في أن المتبادر إلى الفهم عند الإطلاق والشائع في الاستعمال هو الدوام. لكن قد يستعمل في المكث الطويل المنقطع، كسجن مخلد، ووقف مخلد، فيكون محتملاً على أن في جعله لمطلق المكث الطويل نفياً للمجاز والاشتراك فيكون أولى، ثم ان المكث الطويل سواء جعل معنى حقيقياً أو مجازياً أعم من أن يكون مع دوام كما في حق الكفار، وانقطاع كما في حق الفساق . فلا محذور في إرادتهم جميعاً وحتى فلا نسلم أن التأبيد تأكيد، بل تقييد ولو سلم فالمراد أنه تأكيد لطول المكث، إذ قد يقال : حبس مؤبد، ووقف مؤبد.
And concerning the second (objection): that the meaning of 'intentionally' (is) as one who considers his action lawful, according to what Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) mentioned, since intentionality in reality is only from the one who deems it lawful; or that the attachment to the description indicates the cause, so it is specific to one who kills a believer for his faith. Or that khulud, although apparent in perpetuity, the intended meaning here is a long stay, to reconcile the evidences. It cannot be said: khulud is literally eternal because of the immediate understanding, and because of His saying: 'And We did not grant any human before you eternity' (1), and because it is emphasized by the word of perpetuity such as 'abiding eternally therein forever' (2). And emphasizing something strengthens its meaning, and because the general statements connected with khulud encompass the disbelievers, and the intended meaning regarding them is eternity, by agreement. Likewise regarding the sinners, lest one intend both meanings of a homonym or both literal and metaphorical together. For we say: There is no argument that the immediate understanding upon unrestricted usage and the common in usage is perpetuity. But it is sometimes used for a long interrupted stay, such as 'eternal prison' and 'eternal endowment', so it is possible. Moreover, making it for absolute long stay negates metaphor and homonymy and is preferable. Then, the long stay, whether made a literal or metaphorical meaning, is more general than being with perpetuity as in the case of disbelievers, or with interruption as in the case of sinners. So there is no harm in intending all of them. And even then, we do not concede that 'perpetuity' is emphasis, rather it is restriction. And if conceded, the intended is that it is emphasis for the length of stay, since it may be said: perpetual imprisonment and perpetual endowment.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection: that the verse about killing a believer intentionally (An-Nisa 93) proves eternal punishment. The author gives three interpretations: (1) 'intentionally' means deeming the act lawful, so it applies only to apostates; (2) the description 'intentionally' indicates causality, so it applies only to those who kill for the victim's faith; (3) even if 'khulud' implies eternity, here it means a long stay to reconcile evidences. Then a counter-objection is raised: 'khulud' literally means eternity, proven by immediate understanding, the verse about prophets not being eternal, and the use of 'abad' for emphasis. The author responds: while the common meaning is eternity, 'khulud' is used for a long interrupted stay (e.g., 'eternal prison'). Taking it as absolute long stay avoids metaphor and homonymy. This meaning is general, covering both eternal (for disbelievers) and temporary (for sinners). Finally, 'abad' (forever) is actually a restriction, not emphasis, as in 'perpetual imprisonment' which can be temporary. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الخلود (al-khulud)
التأبيد (al-ta'bid)
المكث الطويل (al-makth al-tawil)
المتعمد (al-muta'ammid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'khulud' always and only means eternal duration without any possibility of interruption.
Correction: The author shows that 'khulud' can be used for a long interrupted stay, as in 'eternal prison' or 'eternal endowment', which are not literally eternal. Thus, the word is flexible and context determines the duration.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author give that 'khulud' can mean a long but interrupted stay rather than eternal?
A) It is used in poetry to mean temporary
B) It is used for 'eternal prison' and 'eternal endowment'
C) It is applied only to disbelievers
D) It is a synonym for 'abad'

Key Takeaway
The word 'khulud' in Arabic is flexible and can denote a prolonged stay rather than eternal duration, allowing different interpretations for disbelievers (eternal) and sinful believers (temporary).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your own sins might lead to a temporary punishment rather than eternal, does that make you more careless? How can you guard your heart from taking Allah's mercy for granted?
?
If you truly believed that khulud for sinful believers is only a long stay but not eternal, would you fear the fire less? What does that reveal about the state of your faith?
?
The author argues for a contextual understanding of khulud. Are you willing to submit to the full weight of Allah's words even when they challenge your preconceived notions? What would it cost you to accept a meaning that demands more accountability?
